THE RED PAGE.
Como, N.S.W.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Night in mid-Autumn ! Bridge and bay and cliff,
Seethed in the moon’s milk, slumber silently.
A dying tide swoons to the distant sea.
The bland airs breathing round our little skiff
Sigh heavily from the gulfs of Space, as if
Faint with the fragrance of Eternity,
And in a web of silver symmetry
Dark ripples weave their mystic hieroglyph.
How glorious is Dian in her pride !
And near her, with a lustre interblent,
Throned at the zenith see imperial ride
A single star ! She passes : he, grief-spent,
Pa-les for one instant; then, a suicide
Superb, leaps flashing down the firmament.
The Rudiments of Fiction.
"Then the bowsprit got mixed with the rudder some-
times:
A thing, as the Bellman remarked,
That frequently happens in tropicalclimes,
When a vessel is, so to speak, ‘ snarked
One notes with regret that Ethel Turner is
also adrift on the sea of the girlish imagination.
Her new book, “The Little Larrikin,” (Ward,
Lock & Co.) is, so to speak, “snarked.” Like
“ The Family at Misrule,” “The Little Larrikin ”
is a story for big children which grown-ups should
read with pleasure, but the author has succeeded
less well in uniting the child-interest and the
literature-interest. And whereas formerly the
bowsprit and rudder only occasionally got mixed,
they are now in a tangle pretty nearly all the
time, with disastrous results to plain sailing.
“The Little Larrikin,” to use another Carroll-
ing metaphor, lives in “ Through the Looking-
Glass ” country. Everything about him is more
or less out of drawing, as i'f it had come home
very late last night, and would still be rather glad
of a lamp-post to ket£> it from falling altogether
into topsyturvydom. He is like the Marchion-
ess’s orange-peel and water —“if you make believe
very much it’s quite nice, but if you don’t, you
know, it seems as if it would bear a little more
seasoning.” In which utterance lies the whole
philosophy of the girlish imagination.
Miss Turner, in fact, is working on a radically
wrong method. She invents her characters as weil
As her situations, and her knowledge of life is
doubtfully equal to inventing situations. Her
very fluency undoes her. She observes very little
or not at all, and sits down to erect on a
slender foundation of fact an enormous super-
structure of fancy, which almost inevitably topples
ever at the first gust of criticism as to probabili-
ties and possibilities.
“Lol ” —the little larrikin—has a germ of truth,
doubtless, but Miss Turner’s imagination has
made a Jonah’s gourd of it. At ten years old he
would be amazing : he is only six, and impossible.
But possibilities must get out of the way of Miss
Turner’s imagination. If they don’t, it is “ vera
bad for the coo.” Yet nearly everything has
eharm and interest if you shut ycrnr mind and let
the author blow you whither her fancy list.eth.
“ The Little Larrikin ” would be admirable in a
fairy-tale. But it isn’t a fairy-tale.
And Miss Turner’s characters are getting
colorless and reminiscent: she spends her time in
the clouds weaving fancies instead of down on
earth gathering facts. The literary result is
too often charming nonsense. For example, take
a picture of a figtft between Lol and “ a thick-set
boy of twelve.” (Lol, as before said, is six;
" very little, with sweet eyes ; his hair curled in
fair, little soft rings; his mouth red and babyish.”
" He had been a little larrikin for two years”—
Binee the mature age of four ! has Miss Turner
any idea what'a4-jfear-old child is like?—“and he
was head of a smafl-boy ‘ push ’ he had organised
and recruited lately with several lads of nine and
ten.”)
Lo! breathed wildly on his hands for an instant, then
rushed wildly acress the yard and closed with the enemy.
He fought like.a little tigeu
When two or three minutes had gone and he was still
wneonquered, the thick-set boy experienced a feeling of
astonishment and rained his blows harder.
But he had not the scientific knowledge Lol had gleaned
lr»m Phil and Olera, and even his immeasurably superior
strength could not worst the little iad for several
minutes.
But Linley was flying madly downstairs, and hatless
ever tire tennis-court, through the vegetable garden, and
into the back lane. She burst open Roger’s hack gate
and fell upon the great stout lad, dragging him away by
the collar.
Lol was almost vanquished. His face was scarlet, his
eyes blazing ; in his breast were all the demons of fight
which closg quartets arouse in the born soldier. But his
strength vfes ebbing, his small fists losing their steadi-
ness, and the blood from his poor little nose made a
ghastly spectacle of him.
“ Oh, you great coward, you horrible, disgusting
oeward ! ” cried Linley, shaking the twelve-year-old boy
til his teeth bit his lips and his tongue and the sides of
his month (!). He hung his big head and shuffled and
looked sheepish, &o.
Now, for a shilling or so, Miss Turner could have
lured two newspaper-boys to fight to a finish
before her, and if she had selected one very small
and six, and one very thick-set and twelve, and
excited them both to a “willin’go” such as she
has described, she would see the big boy fall on
top of the little one (whose fists would not reach
his chin), and bear him down to the gutter, where
he would lie on him and choke him till Miss Tur-
ner cried “ Time!” If the encounter were earnest,
there would be no pretence of boxing, and the
“science” of a six-year-old in such a combat would
be ludicrous.
But Miss Turner has heard a little bit about
fighting, and read a little bit, and perhaps seen a
little bit—though no small six-year-old larrikin
ever dreams of “ fighting ” a thick-set twelve-
year-old ; and she has chosen to out her facts to
suit her fancy. It won’t do—not as literature,
anyway. And the example is thoroughly typical
of Miss Turner’s method in “ A Little Larrikin.”
Probably stimulated by Miss Turner’s earlier
successes, a South Australian girl, Miss Ella
Chaffey, has written a children’s story called
“The Youngsters of Murray Home.” She has
not a tithe of Miss Turner’s literary ability, yet
she has written a better and more truthful book
for children than “The Little Larrikin,” simply
because she has trusted to memory instead
of to imagination. Consequently her characters
are real, and her incidents are facts, and look like
facts, and although they are necessarily more
prosaic than Miss Turner’s clever fancies they
leave so ill-taste of improbability behind. Miss
Chaffey, in fact, is a realist in her small way;
and Miss Turner is an idealist without the know-
ledge necessary to make her ideas seem to flower
out of reality. And a detached ideal —an ideal
which is evidently impossible and unattainable—
has no value except in the hands of absolute
genius. Even Heaven and Hall are only interest-
ing because people - think they are going there.
The Hell of the Mars-dwellers isn’t worth specu-
lating about.
It is a curious thing that women save
nearly all their ideality for literature. The
average woman in ordinary life does not appear
concerned with dreams of impossible heroes or
wonderful countries where plum-puddings grow
on gum-trees. She talks, as a rule, of hard,
practical facts—Mrs. Blank’s gown, Mrs. Dash’s
diseases, Mrs. Three-Stars’ servant-girl. After
the boarding-sohool age, that is. Arguing from
life, you might say that her imagination had
dried up : she seems concerned with realism pure
and simple. Arguing from literature, you know if
is merely repressed. And as soon as the average
woman takes pen in hand, out the buried life
gushes ! Eive-sixths of the woman-beginners who
write fiction for The Bulletin never dream of
starting realistically, by describing the people
and things under their eyes, which they know
well, which alone, as a rule, they are competent
to describe. No! they invent some complex
situation, bolster it up with all the forces of their
imagination, dress it in language echoed from
the books they have read, and produce an unreal
monstrosity—because they have not the wit or
the knowledge to make their invention life-like
or probable. The men, less fluent as a rule, are
more realistic. They do not invent; they de-
scribe incidents in their experience, characters
they have met, scenes which have impressed them.
And a much higher average of their “ copy ” is
acceptable-flying low, they are nearer to Truth.
Doubtless imaginative fiction is the higher kind
of fiction —the most impressive and permanent;
but it requires the higher kind of qualities in
the maker. Realistic description is compara-
tively easy. There is wanted a little knack
of observation, and assimilation, and selec-
tion, and description ; but many people can
attain this with practice. Very fair results can
be attained by describing outsides merely—what
people do, how things look. When you go a
little farther, and describe what people think ,
what things are, more talent is required. And
when, advancing, you elevate thoughts and things
to an ideal height, and finally, rising higher still,
draw from individual thoughts and things their
ideal law, and apply the law to the farthest ex-
tent of human conceptions, the path grows too
difficult foyall but fictional genius. But it is
always good to begin at the beginning—with
Australian books post-free from The Bulletin
office for stamps or postal notes of any province:—

Items.
It is a commonplace that greatness, even noto-
riety, has a definite cash value in literature. The
men of thought, so-called, are often but the para-
sites of men of action or other men of thought.
Jones makes a name and earns the vulgar
scrutiny ; Bones, as author, or publisher, or book-
seller, or journalist, picks up the biographical,
aneodotal crumbs fallen from the-great man’s
table, and sauces them into an appetising dish for
reader s greed y to know even to the fashion in which
Jones scratches his leg at contemplative moments.
Publicity is the mother of purchasing patronage,
and Jones’s publicity is ready-made: his para-
graph advertisements are inserted gratis in all the
papers; his name metaphorically glares on ten-
foot hoardings throughout the country. So Bones,
as author, saves time and money by hiring John
L. Sullivan to pass for the writer of his book, and
win it instant vogue; or, as publisher, he wheedles
an approving comment from Gladstone, and
launches his literary nonentity at the tail of a
popular political kite; or, as prudent pressman,
he fawns to the local demigod and diaries
down thedemigod’sconversationalinanities against
the day of the demigod’s death. All interesting
examples of the parasitic method, doubtless justi-
fiable on low moral grounds as well as on the
exalted grounds of expediency or necessity.
Parkes, in this literary sense as in others, had a
good many parasites; and The Bulletin itself,
in its time, has whetted the edge of its circu-
lation on the hone of his notoriety. For jour-
nalism does not live by lying prostrate before
Moses and Confucius, or by continually point-
ing out the beauties of Herodotus or Livy.
Livy and Confucius, as popular dishes, have been
off for some time, and the public lack of demand
for them is most enthusiastic—probably because
there is a well-founded impression that they are
dead. The structure of successful journalism is
built on the proverb that a living dog will sell
ten times as many papers as a dead lion. I*
literature the same rule holds, though litera-
ture has its Napoleonio exceptions. But,
generally speaking, there is no advertisement
like Life, though Death booms your sale for
the moment that the living man is held in memory.
Now, Parkes has been dead eight months, and al-
ready is beginning to smell of the Mosaic and
Confuoian era. (Who was Parkes?) And every
month that his biographers dally—is it seven of
them there are ? or ten ? —their gold is turning
into silver, and their silver into copper. They
rely, perhaps, on lists of subscribers; but these in
Australia are apt to be but a flash in the pan : our
population purchases well, but it is so small. And
for the rest?
It is curious (notes the Saturday Review)
how language is apt to fail upon a sudden emer-
gency or at a supreme moment. Jackson comes
up with a black and greasy figure upon an ice-floe
in the Polar regions, whom he takes to be Nansen,
and all he can find to say is, “ I’m awfully glad
to see you,” to which the blood-besmeared and
smoke-begrimed man replies, “So am I to see
you.” After looking steadily into the strange
face, Jackson continues, “Aren’t you Nansen?”
“ Yes, lam Nansen.” “By Jove!” said Jack-
son, “I am awfully glad to see you.” “Thank
you very much,” said Nansen; “very kind of
you.” The two men might have been meeting at
Hyde Park Corner instead of on the pack-ice to
the south-east of Cape Flora—and yet what else
was there to be said ? Heine relates how he
managed to get an interview with Goethe. The
young poet felt that the supreme hour of his life
had arrived, and yet they only talked about the
plums that grew around Heidelberg.
J.J. : “In reply to 4 One who is there.’ With
a fair droving experience, I ean say that I have
met several men who would answer very well to
the description of * The Banjo’s ’ ‘ Clancy ’; and
it is no t uncommon thing round a camp-fire to
hear a voice that you would like to phonograph
for lonely hours. The Australian bushman is
essentially musical in an untrained way, and
strives after melody as does the bushworaan after
general culture. Shy? Yes, the bushman is shy
among city folk ; on his native heath, a New York
bagman is a fool to him for hittitig it off with all-
comers. When you catoh up to a man on a bush
trackyou may makepretty sure that in half-an-hour
you will know all about him, and ten to one it
interests you.”
J. F. Hogan: “Be Sydney Public Library.
I spent several afternoons there in the early part
of last year, and fully half of the books I sought
were not accessible. It was not pretended for a
moment that they were in legitimate use by
others ; they were simply lost, stolen, or strayed.
They were books treating of early Australian his-
tory, books that ought to be guarded and., pre-
served with the utmost care and vigilance, for
they are almost entirely out of print, and in a few
years copies will be possessed by only a very
limited number of private collectors and public
libraries. From what you write, confirmed and
corroborated by my own observation and experi-
ence, I fancy a select committee of inquiry is
about due at the Sydney Public Library.”
Translations.
LAIS.
How stately doth my Lais go !
With studied step, composedly slow ;
Superb, as some tall mountain fir,
Whom Zephyr’s wing doth.slightly stir :
(For surely beauty is allied
By Nature very near to Pride):
The groves indeed mild breezes move,
But her the gentler gales of Love.
Her cheek with native crimson glows,
But crimson softened by the rose :
’Twas Hebe’s self bestowed the hue,
Yet health has added something, too:
But if an over-tinge there be,
Impute it to her modesty.
Her voice most clear, yet ’tis not strong;
Her periods full, though seldom long ;
With wit, good-natured wit endow’d ;
Fluent her speech, but never loud.*
Her light-spun robes in every part
Are fashioned with the nicest art,
T’ improve her stature, and to grace
The polish’d limbs which they embrace.
How beautiful she looks when drest!
But view her freed from this disguise,
Stript of th’ unnecessary vest —
’Tis Beauty’s self before your eyes.
So exquisitely soft her limbs
That not a bone but pliant seems;
As if th’ embrace of Love —so warm !
Would quite dissolve her beauteous form.
She, too, like Helen, can inspire
Th’ unfeeling heart of age with fire ;
Can teach their lazy blood to move,
And light again the torch of love.
—Halhed and Sheridan, from AmsT^ENETea.
A pine-tree standeth lonely
In the North on an upland baro;
It standeth whitely shrouded
With snow, and sleepeth there:
It dreameth of a palm-tree,
Which far in the Fast alone
In mournful silence standeth
On its ridge of burning stone.
JAMES THOMSON, from HEINE.
THE GOOD GOD.
One day the good God got out of bed
In a very good humour for us ’tis said;
He put his nose to the window light—
“ Perhaps their planet has perished quite. 1 *
Not yet: in its corner very far
He saw it twinkling, our little star.
If I can think how they get on there,
Said he, the devil may take me, I swear,
The devil may take me, I swear !
Black or white, frozen or broiled,
(He S&id, like a father to children spoiled),
Mortals whom I have made so small,
They pretend that I govern you all;
But, God be praised, you shall also see
That I have ministers under me.
If I dbn’t give the sack to one or two pair,
My children, the devil may take me, I swear,
The devil may take me, I swear !
To make you live in peace divine,
Have I not given you women and wine ?
Yet in my teeth with prayers and boasts
The pigmies call me the Lord of Hosts :
And even dare to invoke my name
When they light the murderous cannon’s dame 1
If I ever commanded column or square,
My children, the devil may take me, I swear,
The devil may take me, I swear!
Who are these dwarfs so richly drest,
On gilded thrones in sumptuous rest?
The head anointed, so proud and pert,
These chiefs of your insect-swarms assert
That I have blest their rights of place,
That they are kings by my special grace.
If it is by me that they reign thus< there,
My children, the devil may take me, I swear,
The devil may take me, I swear !
Then these other dwarfs, all black, of whom
My poor nose hates the incense fume ?
They make of life a dismal fast,
And in my name fierce curses cast
In their sermons Very fine, said he,
Only, by gad, they’re Hebrew to me :
If I believe\anything they declare,
My children, the devil may take me, I swear,
The devihmay take me, I swear!
Children, enough of this : no sect
But the good kind hearts shall be my eleot.
Make love to each other and live in joy,
Without any fear«that God will annoy ;
Laugh down the'great and the Ganting orew——
But suppose the mouehards should hear me S
Adieu !
If into heaven those fellows fare,
My children, the devil may take me, I swear,
The devil may take me, I swear.

is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir,—Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, 11. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney.”

Bards of the Backblocks .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN PETE COMES HOME.
The drought was bad, and on the farm
The life was rather slow,
So Peter went to work at White s
About a year ago.
Though father ’n’ me have done the work
And kept the place in trim,
The drought-scourge would have beat us, sure,
If’t hadn’t been for him.—
For Jack, who’s up the country now,
Was always one to roam ;
But mother says if tunes get good
She must have Peter home.
It’s in the brickyard run by White
Pete works, and every day
He’s up and out before the sun
To trudge three miles aw r ay.
He started as a “ pug-boy ” first,
(White made the sand-stocks then)
But since they’ve put the engine up
Pete’s worked among the men.
Sometimes he gets all caked with clay
As white as ocean’s foam ;
And then the youngsters shout with glee
All glad when Pete comes home.
We wait for him of evenings now,
For “ not a bit to eat,”
Says mother, when the children cry—
“ Just wait awhile for Pete.”
And when the sun’s last glances set
The west with red aglow,
And Pete comes up the bridle-track
Past where the wattles grow,
There’s not as fine a picture in
The Louvre, or at Rome,
As when the youngsters run to meet
Our Pete when he comes home.
Yes, Pete’s the idol of the house
And worshipped by us all;
He’s just a hero’s picture, too,
So straight and strong and tall.
And mother says when she goes first—
When her work here is o’er,
And God has called her to her rest
Upon the Golden Shore ;
And when Saint Peter says “ Come in,
No longer, loved one, roam ! ”
She’ll rest right there outside the gate
And wait till Pete comes Home.
ULOOLA.
A SONG OF GOLD.
Oh, there’s great exhilaration in the bosoms of
the boys,
Who are sailing for the goldfields in the West;
Though the dear old days are dead, there’s a roar-
ing time ahead,
And the bonny birds are flying from the nest.
Let the old folk bide alone, for the whole wide
world’s your own,
And there’s yellow gold in plenty in the West !
For it’s gold ! bright gold !
And it’s yours to handle, to have, and to hold !
Will you sell your homes, as they have been sold,
For the bright, hard gold ?
Oh, there’s grief and tribulation for the mothers
of the boys,
For the sisters and the sweethearts left behind.
Ah, the good old time is dead ! Ah, the weary
wait instead !
But the ship is scudding on before the wind ;
And it’s well for those who go to the gay new life,
you know,
But it’s cruel hard for those who stay behind 1
But it’s gold ! bright gold !
And it’s yours to handle, to have, and to hold !
Will you sell your hearts, as they have been sold,
For the bright, hard gold ?
Ah, there’s mighty jubilation in the hearts of all
the boys
Who are drinking in the grogshops of the town ;
And the gas flares overhead till the wild carouse is
sped
And the jolly boys have knocked their last sovs.
down,
What with billiards, dice, and gin, you can make
the gold-boys spin,
When you leave the blessed diggings for the
town.
And it’s gold ! bright gold !
And it’s yours to handle, to have, and to hold !
Will you sell your souls as they have been sold,
For the bright, hard gold ?
Oh there’s strange inanimation on the faces of the
boys
Who went gaily to the gold-fields long ago :
Though the parched earth is their bed, very quiet
are the dead,-
Very peaceful are the sleepers lying low.
They are scattered here and there, does it matter
why or where,
When their mothers’ hearts were broken long
ago?
It was gold ! bright gold !
It was theirs to .handle, to have, and to hold !
Did they sell their lives, as they have been sold,
For the bright, hard gold ?
M.L.
DORA WILCOX.

city, have placed upon this market at a J?|?ic© Hitllol*tO TTll“
known Here a Cigar of exceptional quality.

comes from Havana, and arrangements have been made for supplies from
there of the choicest Tobacco (Vuelta Abajo).
BONANZA Cigars are made of this Tobacco, with a necessary portion of
equally-choice Seed Leaf, and together they form a Cigar second only to the
recognised brands of high-class Havanas.
4 Full-sized Cigars for 6d.!!— win at first no doubt
be prejudicial; but machinery, overcoming the great cost of production, just

The “ BONANZA”
comes as a pleasant surprise to many who, though capable of appreciating a
good Cigar, have lately been prevented through retrenched circumstances from
indulging in the hitherto expensive luxury.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 28, 1896.
Prick, 6d.
THAT SULKY NIGGER.
“Speaking at Bathurst Convention Premier Reid explained that his object in going to Queensland was to use his utmost exertions with the Government of that
province so that some way out of the difficulty which has arisen there with regard to the representation of Queensland in the coming convention might be discovered.
In this mission, Mr. Reid added, he claimed to represent not only the Government and the people of N.S. Wales, but the whole of the population in other parts of
Australia.”
G.H.R.: “Come out from under that hat; I know yer! ”

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, of
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
* Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Jilaclc and White.
A nyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting jihotograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artist ic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

Notice to Subscribers in, Victoria, Queensland,
S. Australia, Westralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Intervrovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 3s. 2d.
added for postage. England £l, with 6s. 6d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 28, 1896.
The Toy Convention.
The amateur Federal Convention which has
been amusing itself with the manufacture
of a toy constitution at Bathurst got on
very well with its work. Or, to be strictly
accurate, it got on very well until it struck
the two great snags which are always in the
way, and after that its progress was uncer-
tain and precarious. The first of these in-
evitable difficulties is, how to divide the
Federal power among the different provinces
with such mathematical accuracy that no
person or province shall have one half-
fraction more than his or its correct share.
The second is, how to divide the charges of
the Federal Government with such awful
correctness that no province will pay one
half-farthing more per annum than it should
do. When the Federal question runs up
hard against these two difficulties, the apostles
of unity and fraternity generally resolve into
their old provincial elements, and declare
themselves ready to die in the last ditch
before their especial local Pedlington will
sacrifice anything whatever for the beloved
“ Cause,” and as it is practically impossible
to split the very last hair into six exactly
equal parts, the Federal question is always
bringing up with a shock against these pro-
blems, and getting no further. Nominally,
Federation is a matter of unity and combina-
tion, but the debates upon it deal far more
with the question of dividing and sub-
dividing things than with putting them
together. And the question, so far, has
absolutely failed to get any “ forrarder.”
* * * *
It is generally assumed, as a matter of
habit, that the Federal Legislature shall con-
sist of two Houses. Why it should consist
of two Houses more than of one or three or
four has never been very definitely ex-:
plained ; but from sheer inability to get
out of the ancient groove—whether it is
worth while getting out of it or not—the
bi-cameral system seems to be accepted as
inevitable. One proposal is that the two
Houses should both be elected by the people
on a population basis ; another that the
Lower House should be elected on the basis
of population, and the Upper House should
consist of, say, ten representatives from each
province, so that the largest and the smallest
provincesshould possess equal authority. If the
former scheme is adopted, it is evident that
Victoria and New South Wales will carry a
majority of votes in both Houses, and if they
choose to combine on any question where
their interests clash with those of the other
provinces, they can control the Federal Par-
liament. Under the other system, Victoria
and New South Wales, with 2b million
people, will have a decisive majority in the
Assembly ; while in the Council, Westralia,
Tasmania, S.A. and a couple of Queensland
members, representing altogether about
700,000 people, could, if they combined, man
down the representatives of the other
2,800,000 Australians. If the population
basis is adopted all round, the obvious result
is that the representatives of one-eighth part
of the country could boss the other seven-
eighths, supposing it possible to imagine any
question arising on which Victoria and New
South Wales could be unanimous on one side,
and the remaining four states on the other.
If the system of equal State representation
is adopted in one House, then so far as that
House is concerned one-fifth of the people
can boss the other four-fifths. It is the cus-
tom among paper constitution-makers to say
that the Commonwealth is wholly impossible
until those alleged difficulties are sur-
mounted, and as both of them can’t be sur-
mounted the result looks like a deadlock.
* * *
The Bulletin opines that the difficulty is
much more apparent than real, and it doesn’t
matter nearly so much as is commonly sup-
posed which solution is adopted. If both
Houses aie elected on the basis of one mem-
ber to so many electors all the country over,
it is certain that for the present two small
States will carry a majority of votes. When,
in course of years, population becomes more
generally diffused, the discrepancy, if it is
one, will right itself. If one House consists
of an equal number of representatives from
each province, the population of the thinly-
peopled provinces will, for the present, lie
much over-represented so far as that House
is concerned, but when a more general diffu-
sion of population sets in that discrepancy
will also right itself more or less. As there
is all the rest of Time for things to straighten
themselves in, there seems a fair chance that
they will come right in the end. Exactly
the same discrepancies exist under the present
system, so that if Federation is postponed
until it can be arrived at on a mathematically-
accurate principle it only means that while
it is waiting Australia must live for an in-
definite period under an utterly lopsided
provincial principle. In South Australia,
about 100 square miles in the immediate
vicinity of Adelaide can boss, in the local
Parliament, on the population basis, the other
903,000 square miles of the province.
Melbourne, Bendigo, and Ballarat only
amount to a spot altogether, yet when repre-
sentation goes by population alone they can
outvote all the rest of Victoria; and the
people of two eastern counties can do the
same to the balance of New South Wales.
Again, in spite of a vigorous effort to put re-
presentation in New South Wales on a strict
population basis, things are so woefully
mixed that whereas 31,000 electors return
one set of 19 members, 53,000 other electors
also return only 19. In other words,
part of the population has nearly twice
as much political power per man as
another part, despite the best efforts to make
things absolutely fair all round. In Queens-
land things range from 19 members to 18,298
electors up to another 19 members for 28,605
electors. In S.A. the disproportions are
actually worse than in Queensland ; in Vic-
toria only a little better, while in Westralia
they are so unspeakably lopsided that 887
electors return 11 members, and another
8858 electors also have just 11 representa-
tives among them. If this sort of thing is
the best which five Parliaments can do within
their own borders, then the proposal that
the authors of this unspeakable muddle
should fix up the Federal Parliament on a
basis of absolutely fair representation is
mere drivel. And if Australia can crowd
through under the above conditions, then it
can get through equally well under either of
the proposed Federal systems, despite the
fact that neither is an absolutely ideal
arrangement. The proposal to delay Fede-
ration until the Parliament can be built
on an absolutely level system of representa-
tion hasn t much to recommend it, seeing
that it means leaving all power indefinitely
in the hands of six Parliaments which are
constructed on a more unequal principle
than the worst Federal legislature yet sug-
gested.
* * * *
The financial difficulty is the brother and
other near relatives of the representation
problem. It is generally understood that
the Central Government shall take over the
customs, excise, post, telegraph, and railway
revenues, and against this it shall be respon-
sible for the cost of its own maintenance
(Parliamentary and Ministerial salaries and
the like), the interest on the public debt,
naval and military outlay, costs of Federal
courts of justice, and working expenses of
customs, excise, post, telegraph, and railway
services. As regards the surplus which
ought to remain after meeting these out-
goings, the Bathurst delegates, like many
other politicians, haggle dismally, and make
elaborate plans for its distribution on an
ideallj 7 honest system among the provincial
treasuries, and it seems to be generally
agreed that until this absolutely fair basis
can be arrived at Federation ought not to
move. And the basis is hard to arrive at.
With a uniform tariff some of the provinces,
notably W.A. and Queensland, will contri-
bute much more customs and excise duties
per head than others—for the present, at all
events ; and if the surplus is returned on a
population basis a great injustice will be
done. Also, N.S.W. will put into the com-
mon fund a larger net railway revenue than
any other province, so in this respect she will be
badly treated on the population basis. Again,
S.A. will supply the most profitable post
and telegraph department, and Victoria will
hand over the biggest railway system as her
contribution to the assets and the smallest
debt per head of population as her share of
the liabilities, and if the surplus is divided
out according to population both these pro-
vinces will be also greatly injured. Also,
S.A. and Queensland, which have the biggest
interest bill per head, will gain most in the
present by lumping the liabilities of the six
provinces, and Victoria and N.S.W. will
gain least, so again the population basis goes
wrong —in fact it goes wrong everywhere.
Yet the surplus can’t possibly be returned
according to area, for then the province
which gave the least would get back most ;
nor can it be apportioned according to the
amount of revenue contributed, for when
border tariffs and differential railway rates
are abolished, goods for the Riverina will
pay railway freights and Customs duties at
Melbourne, and those for Western Vic. and
N.S.W. will do likewise at Adelaide or Port
Augusta, and the actual contribution of each
province will be impossible to arrive at.
* * * *
The Bathurst convention, like many other
people and institutions, wrestled desper-
ately with this alleged difficulty, and pro-
pounded elaborate and foolishly impossible
plans for its extinction. Meanwhile, The
Bulletin flatly denies that there is any
financial difficulty, or any more financial diffi-
culty than usual. As matters are at present,
about Half the customs and railway revenue
derived from North Queensland is spent on
the South. The land revenues derived from
the country districts of all the provinces are
largely spent in and around the capitals.
The salaries of public officials are extracted
from the population at large, and also ex-
pended mostly in the capitals. The railway
expenditure in Victoria has been so badly
adjusted that one-third of the province has
far more line than it requires, and another
third hasn’t the semblance of a railroad, yet
all the province has to foot the bill. There
is no attempt anywhere to spend public
money in the localities by which it. is con-
tributed, or to hand back the surplus, where
there is one to the exact people who
supplied it, and if the provinces can’t
make even a semblance of adjusting
their finances on a fair system it is
absurd to demand that the Federation should
do so either. Moreover, even though the
Central Government could adjust things with
such dreadful accuracy that it could give back
to each province every copper of its fair share
of the surplus, it wouldn’t make things one
whit the better. To give the Melbourne
Government the share belonging to Portland
or|Mildura, and let that Government spend
it on railway works in South Gippsland ; or
to let the Sydney authorities annex that
portion of the surplus which belongs to
Broken Hill and expend it on building a new
Parliament House in Sydney ; or to give the
money of North Queensland back to South
Queensland, is quite as unfair as if part of
the Victorian or Tasmanian surplus found its
way to Townsville or the Northern Territory.
Nobody has ever yet discovered a way
whereby this problem can be truly adjusted,
and no one ever will. Federation, no matter
how it is arranged, can’t make the problem
any worse than it is. So far as Federation
is concerned, in fact, there is no problem at
all. The shortest way out of it is not to
hand back the surplus at all, but to let it be
expended by the Central Government in
public works wherever in all Australia they
seem to be most needed and most likely to
prove profitable, with no regard whatever
whether the money is spent where it came
from, or in some other place. The provin-
cial Governments never troubled about this
question ; all they demanded was that the
revenue raised in New South Wales or Vic-
toria or Queensland should also be spent
there. In the same way the business of the
Federal Government is to see that the
revenue raised in Australia shall be wisely
expended there, and it can’t be expected to
cut the details any finer than the provincial
authorities have done.
The Australian Commonwealth—One
Step Nearer.
In Sydney, some weeks ago, the editor, lessee
and printer of Truth newspaper were charged
at the Central Criminal Court with publish-
ing a seditious libel on Queen Victoria.
The Crown law authorities prosecuted. The
lessee and printer were acquitted. In the
case of the editor, the jury disagreed—it is
said that there were nine for acquittal and
three for conviction—and the accused was
discharged under recognizances to come up
for re-trial if called upon. Last week it was
officially announced that he would not be
called upon.
* * * *
The prosecution was stupid : the accused
hinted that it was malicious. Truth is a
Sunday newspaper notorious for plain speak-
ing and strong language, and it is antagonistic
to the existing N.S.W. Ministry and its
members. It has been specially severe on
certain actions by the Crown Law authorities
which may have been merely imprudent,
but which looked prejudiced as well. “ The
Crown law authorities ” is only a form of
words including individuals who have
been attacked by Truth in a very galling
. manner. It is conceivable enough that
the smart of their wounds may have stimu-
lated a desire for vengeance. Officials have
eyes, hands, organs, dimensions, senses,
affections, passions ; are fed with the same
food, hurt with the same weapons, as a pri-
vate individual is. If you prick their vanity,
they bleed ; if you tickle it, they laugh ; if
you poison their reputation, they politically
die ; and if they think you wrong them, they
are as apt as others to use their public oppor'
tunities to wreak a private revenge.
* * * *
The prosecution may not have been malici-
ous : it was certainly stupid. The language in
the article complained of was coarse ; most
of it was absolutely true ; a great deal of it,
if not untrue, was unnecessary. As a mere
woman, Queen Victoria is probably no
better and no worse than the vast majority
of her sex at the present day. She may be,
as Beaconsfield phrased it in a moment of
after-dinner expansion, “ physically and
morally incapable of government ” ; but the
dictum would apply just as well to almost any
other woman placed in her position. Conse
quently, there is little point in assail-
ing her in the fashion of the in-
criminated article, as an individual, and
calling her “a semi-senile old woman of over
seventy,” “ podgy - figured, sulky - faced,”
“flabby, fat, and flatulent,”—and so on, in
language of which the meaning seems to have
been considered less than the alliteration.
* * * *
But this language is not seditious. The
article quotes Shelley’s hymn for Liberty.
Surely that is not seditious. It recounts the
disgraces of Queen Victoria’s immediate
royal ancestors—
Princes, the dregs of their dull race, who flow'
Through public scorn—mud from a muddy spring—
Rulers who neither see, nor feel, nor know,
But leech-like to their fainting country cling.
But, if that be sedition, it is sedition which
may be found in every English history—
sedition which is taught in every Australian
State school. The article ably summed up the
results of Queen Victoria’s reign, and finds
the chief of them to be an increase of
£150,000,000 to the British national debt—
the only thing about the Government which
is national, as Cobbett remarked when
satirising “Her Majesty’s” army, “Her
Majesty’s” navy, and “Her Majesty’s”
beloved Commons. But this is not sedition.
The article called the Prince of Wales a “turf-
swindling, card-sharping, wife-debauching,
boozing rowdy.” These are statements
which cannot be proved, and The Bulletin
does not believe they are true ; but they do
not constitute sedition.
* * • *
Wherein, then, did the sedition lie ? Ap-
parently in the foolish imagination of the
Crown Law authorities. This paper glanced
at the article at the time of the issue, and
noted its extravagance, and forgave its
extravagance for the sake of the essential
truths which it contained—truths which can-
not be proclaimed too often; and straight-
way forgot all about it. So, doubtless,
within a week or so, did practically every-
body else. But, by pillorying its authors,
the Crown law authorities rescued it from
oblivion, stimulated the public to closely
read it and reflect on it, and so made its
lesson ineffaceable. Which, if the Crown law
authorities were really unbiassed defenders
of monarchy, was a very stupid thing
to do. The British authorities digested
this inference long ago in the case of
John Wilkes, who indeed hore many re-
semblances to John Norton, Truth’s offend-
ing editor. And six years later, says His-
torian Green, “ the failure of the prosecu-
tion directed against an anonymous journalist
named ‘ Junius ’ for his letter to the King
established the right of the Press to criticise
the conduct, not of Ministers or Parliament
only, but of the sovereign himself.”
* * * »
In Hyde Park, London, one Sunday three
years ago, the writer listened to an inflamed
orator denouncing royalty to a knot of
incurious dawdlers. “ Queen Victoria,” he
said passionately, “Queen Victoria is a
German .” The first omitted
w'ord was a coarse dissyllable ; the second a
coarse and suggestive monosyllable. A
policeman standing by was moved to mildly
remonstrate. “ Come, come, Harrison,” he
said, “stow that.” “Yes,” said the orator,
with a fierce expletive, “andyou are one of her
bullies ! ” The policeman smiled good-
naturedly and walked away, followed by the
jeers of the crowd. In Australia he would
probably have knocked the man down,
hustled him to the lock-up, and a sentence
of from two to five years’ penal servitude
would have followed. Witness Reseigh
Martin. The fact is, that in Australia, and
particularly in New South Wales, not only is
officialdom less able than in England,
but it is more bigoted and tyran-
nical, and it has an ignorant and
bigoted section of the public to support it.
The English law authorities would never
have dared to arrest a member of the House
of Commons in the circumstances attending
the recent arrest of Crick, member of
N.S.W. Parliament. The English Rey-
nolds's Newspaper publishes in almost
every issue matter more derogatory to
the Crown than that which led to the arrest
of Norton, and is unchecked. English
'speakers and writers frequently and openly
discuss the desirability of a British Republic,


and no official dreams of hampering them,
The Australian democracy permits interfer-
ence with the freedom of speech and of the
Press which would not be endured in monar-
chical Britain
. * * * *
It is not, The Bulletin believes, that
Australians are less independent, but that
they are more indifferent. It requires a
/ii . ~ , u , ,
gieat deal to rouse them to active protest
agamst official encroachments. Of the con-
tempt of native-born Australians for mon-
archy there can be no question. Their atti-
tude towards it is one of cynical sufferance,
apathetic dislike, rarely rising to a spasmodic
intensity of hatred. The Australian’s charac-
teristic irreverence almost atones for his
characteristic apathy. Scorn of dogmatic
authority seems to be his birthright : he
brings all things to the test of reason. Well
may his pastors and would-be spiritual
masters deplore a lack of ereduUt/which
makes their creeds an empty shell. Some
day Britain s hereditary monarch will have
occasion to deplore it too. '
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Evolution of Bankruptcy in
Melbourne.
A debate upon Insolvency Law Amendment in
the Vic. Leg. Council lias disclosed some in-
teresting facts re the system by which an in-
fluential mob of Melbourne traders and specu-
lators shook themselves free from liabilities
without bringing their names before the public.
Mr. Gbimwade, speaking as a commercial
authority, said of the I.L.A. Bill lately passed
through the Assembly : “ The provisions as to
compositions with creditors might very well be
repealed altogether. Until 1891 no one had
availed himself of these provisions, but in that
year one composition was effected, and it was
then that it was decided that these proceedings
should be kept secret. There was nothing
whatever in the Act to justify that course, but
the man who made that composition in 1891
threatened the department that if the particu-
lars were published he would issue a writ for
.£20,000 damages, and the Solicitor-General of
the day gave instructions that particulars
of compositions should not be furnished
to the press. Immediately that became
known a number of insolvent people hastened
to avail themselves of the opportunity, and
in 1892 no fewer than 83 compositions were
effected, the total liabilities amounting to
£5,074,000. That was the year in which men
made compositions of a farthing in the £ for
immense sums, and then went to Europe by
mail-steamer and returned to the colony to
splash mud from their carriage-wheels upon
their unfortunate creditors. Altogether, within
three years, about 200 compositions were
effected to the extent of £8,000,000, and that
was the greatest scandal that ever happened in
the business annals of Melbourne.” Mr. Grim-
wade might have added “or any other city.”
The private compounders didn’t in all cases
enjoy the secrecy which they desired, and which
the Government had endeavored to obtain
for them. The case of B. J. Fink, for instance,
became public almost immediately after he had
paid his small copper coin, and after a period of
uncertainty as to whether the Act did, or did
not, justify any “gagging” of the press, lists
of names of “illegitimate bankruptcies” (to
coin a suitable term) were published in one or
more Melbourne weeklies. By that time, how-
ever, secrecy had served its purpose. The
bloom of scandal had been rubbed off these
private compositions by Time’s effacing fingers
ere they were recorded in print. Smellbourne
air was tainted with a sentiment of toleration for
this new departure in insolvency proceedings,
and commercial self-respect had become almost
a dead letter—thanks mainly to the Solicitor-
General who allowed that one man in 1891
to show an easy way of “going bung”
to his 199 followers. Bulletin readers
do not need to be told that Melbourne
is to-day the paradise of reckless insolvents.
Certificates of release from liabilities are granted
by the Full Court, even though they have been
refused by the Insolvency Judge, under circum-
stances which would not be held to justify a
certificate in any other Australian province.
The existing state of affairs places every
cautious, shame-fearing trader at a monstrous
disadvantage. Men who strive hard to meet
their liabilities are compelled to admit them-
selves fools in a worldly sense, working only
for reward in the world to come, and if they
entertain serious doubts concerning a future
state, their temptation to file a schedule is
strengthened every time they read of a scandal-
ous insolvent slipping quietly through the
Court.
A Matter of Self-Reliance.
The Bank of New South Wales held its half-
yearly meeting last week in Sydney, and the
chairman of the institution took the oppor-
tunity to get a serious remark off his chest.
The said remark wasn’t entirely or wholly new ;
almost every affluent old gentleman who runs a
bank has said it, or something very like it,
before. Still, it was interesting in its way, and
can stand reproduction :
Political unrest here and in other parts of the world,
varying'and occasionally disappointing markets for our
products, all have their effect. But, slow as it may be,
there is no reason to doubt as these causes pass away—as
they surely must do through the good sense, energy, and
rigid economy of our people—so will the revival of trade,
enterprise, and business become more pronounced and
vigorous, and, what is important to the hank, a profitable
outlet he found for our surplus funds and capital. I, per-
sonally, in common with many others, deplore the grow-
ing inclination of the Governments of many of the
colonies to pass measures in the direction of interfering
with the resourceful disposition of the people. Reliance
on ourselves has in the past done much, if not every-
thing, to bring into existence the enterprise which has
made these colonies what they are, and I regret very
much to see that the tendency of legislation is to sup-
press some of the qualities in our national character
which in the past have done so much to make us self-
reliant.
The Bulletin has no doubt that the affluent
old gentlemen who constantly get off this time-
honored wheeze really believe in it; it is pos-
sible to believe in almost anything if one tries
hard enough. Nevertheless there is something
pitiably absurd in the Australian who “ leans
against a post ” rising up to tell himself how
self-reliant he is, and how it is his self-reliance
that has made the country “ what it is.” He
has plenty of good qualities, no doubt, but self-
reliance is just the one he has never shown, up
to date. The Australian has done a wonderful
lot to open up this great country—with bor-
rowed money. There is hardly a road or bridge
or ditch or culvert or public building or half-
mile of railroad which doesn’t represent bor-
rowed money; this great nation can’t even lay
down new wood-blocks on the Parramatta-road,
or repair the Museum roof, or put up Gov.
Phillip’s statue, or patch an old dredge, or
take a few snags out of a river, or shore
up a decayed wharf, or do anything under
the sun, without begging J. Bull to put up
the money to do it with. It imports its rail-
way managers from England, likewise its
bishops, all its principal clergymen, its mili-
tary commanders, college professors, irrigation
experts, and many others. A good half of its
banking business is done for it by its big brother,
Bull. It insures its furniture and other pro-
perties mostly in foreign offices, because it can’t
believe that its own institutions are decently
solid. It only occasionally respects or believes
in one of its own artists, writers, singers, or
actors who hasn’t gone ’Ome and been guaran-
teed by J. Bull as being deserving of respect.
When it finds a new gold-mine it promptly lies
down flat and prays to Bull to supply the
money necessary for working it. Its one
big industry—wool-growing—is largely run
by Bull, who owns probably one - third
or one - half of the show. Of the
coastal shipping business, three - fourths is
directly or indirectly owned abroad, and when
any new departure is suggested the first thing
is to go and ask Bull to put up the money,
and the second is to ask him to build the
ships. The Australian banks, for three years
past, have been in a chronic state of wander-
ing ’Omewards to ask Bull, with all due
grovel, to reduce the interest and give
them more time, and generally to let
them live a little longer. Even when it was
proposed, after much hard incitement by a
Belgian expert, to put up one humble, unpre-
tentious beet-sugar mill at Tenterfield, almost
the first suggestion was that someone should
pack up his bag and bolt for Britain to raise the
necessary capital, and whenever the depression
gets unusually bad the one inspiration is to
borrow the money from Bull to start the sand-
shifting job, or the mud-shovelling contract at
Shea’s Creek. The spectacle of the Australian,
of all men in this world, talking of the “resource-
ful disposition of the people ” and the national
spirit of self-reliance, and complaining about
State Socialism as tending to destroy these
beautiful alleged traits of the public character,
is a ghastly joke. State Socialism may go right
ahead in Australia without worrying over the
possible destruction of this country’s “ resource-
ful spirit.” There never was enough of that
alleged spirit to be worth talking about.
Under Sixteen.
Pastor Rentoul, a blathersome Vic. bigot, has
been attacking the Judges for their administra-
tion of the age-of-consent clause of the Crimes
Act—quoting the opinion of the weekly organ
of the W.C.T. U. in corroboration of himself.
Chief Justice Madden is one of the attacked.
Now, even Chief Justices cannot be always
wrong, and, in the case in question, the
beauteous Madden was luminously right. A
girl, aged almost 16, had been “ keeping
company ” with a boy aged 19—with the
full knowledge of the girl’s parents. They
walked out together at night with the full
knowledge, etc. In evidence, the girl admitted
that she, of her own accord, told her “ betrayer ”
that she was 17. A child was born. Forthwith
the parents of the girl interviewed the boy
(“betrayer” is the word used by Rentoul,
W.C.T.U., and the weekly), and demanded
that he should “marry his victim.” Mrs.
Grundy’s old, old method of compensating for
one child by more children. The parents of the
boy (again, aged 19) entered their veto —and
the boy had perforce to plead his “ age-of-con-
sent ” as a bar to the union. Then the police
were informed, and brought action. The lower
court committed for trial. The jury brought
in a verdict of guilty, with a recommendation
to mercy. Chief Justice Madden endorsed the
recommendation, stated there was no crime, in
the case, and delivered a formal sentence of six
months, to be suspended during the good
behaviour of accused. This plain tale sets
down Mr. Rentoul’s bigoted hysterics as one
more case of clerical twisting of facts to help
out an uncharitable sermon.
An Unobjectionable Surplus.
The Federal revenue problem, which Samuel
Griffith declared to be the practically insuper-
able bar to union, and which the Bathurst Con-
vention and the daily press have been much
troubled about of late, isn’t a very large affair
after all when carefully considered. The situa-
tion pans out somewhat as follows: If the
Central Government, when there is one, takes
over the external Customs (interprovincial Cus-
toms being abolished), excise, postal, telegraph
and railway revenues, its income will be about
£18,700,000 per annum. If it pays interest on
the public debt, working expenses of the rail-
ways, posts and telegraphs, and maintains the
naval and military establishments and the
Federal courts, its outlay will probably be about
£15,000,000. Therefore it will have a surplus
of ,£3,700,000, while the provincial Govern-
ments will go short. By reason of this quite a
number of ponderous schemes have been
devised for ascertaining by a stupendous system
of statistics exactly how much each pro-
vince contributes to the general revenue,
and how much each gets out of it, and thus
apportioning the surplus among them in a way
that will aggravate all parties concerned. The
Bulletin rises to suggest six possible and
feasible and perfectly simple ways out of the
dilemma. No. 1 expedient is to let the Central
Government keep the surplus, and use it for the
construction of public works; there is a vast
amount still to be done in railway building and
harbor construction, and the making of tele-
graphs and main roads, and other things ; and
the idea of actually building railways out of
revenue instead of with borrowed money is bril-
liant and almost wholly novel. Then the Pro-
vincial Governments would simply have to hustle
to make up the deficiency, but by increased
land and income-taxes, absentee-tax, tote-
percentages, State drink or tobacco mono-
poly, tax on race-horses, increased fines upon
drunks, &c., bicycle tax, taxes on crests, coats-
of-arms, carriages, foreign insurance offices,
annexing unclaimed moneys and general
economy, they should get through. A second
device is to saddle the Federal Government
with the cost of the Public Instruction system,
which will reduce the surplus to about
.£1,700,000 and proportionately relieve the
local authorities. A third way out is for the
Central Government to fix uniform excise duties
throughout Australia, but to let them be col-
lected and appropriated by the local treasuries.
This would reduce the annual surplus to about
£1,250,000, which is little enough for railway
construction expenditure throughout a whole
continent. A device which no one seems to
have suggested yet is that (say) half of the sur-
plus should be used for public works, and the
remainder applied in reduction of the Federal
debt. Again the superfluous money might be
got rid of on tolerably fair terms by giving to
each province an endowment in proportion to
the amount raised by direct taxation. In fact,
there is practically no end to the ways in which
that surplus—which hasn’t yet arrived—can be
disposed of. No Australian Government that
this paper knows of has ever had the slightest
difficulty in getting rid of its surplus.

Gone Down Below.
About the most unobtrusive disappearance of
the late N.S.W. session was that of the Lands
for Closer Settlement Bill. It vanished so very
silently that the ignominious backdown of the
Reid Government passed almost unnoticed.
The province badly wants a system whereby
the State shall have power to resume large pro-
perties, especially in the neighborhood of towns,
and more especially in the Eastern Division,
and cut them up into small farms to be sold or
leased on easy terms to agriculturists. The
success of this policy in Maoriland has suffi-
ciently proved that it is not only possible, but
profitable, provided it is under honest and
capable management. The trouble about the
Reid Government’s scheme was that it abso-
lutely reeked with possibilities of corruption
and was wholly inefficient as well. It didn’t
provide that the land-tax valuation or any
other fixed and recognised valuation should
decide the price of the property. It did not
provide for compulsory resumption, which is
the one thing really wanted if the State is to
get the right sort of land for its purpose. The
Government was merely to negotiate for the
property, and get it for 50 or 500 per cent,
above its value, as it does when it resumes land
for railways, wharves, and other public pur-
poses. The Bill, as introduced, gave
the Ministry power to pay for the land
(if it and the seller were both agree-
able), not in cash, but in 4 per cent,
debentures at par, and, as 4 per cent, deben-
tures are worth about £ll2, this meant that a
favored seller who was the uncle of somebody of
importance might get State paper, worth
£4480 in the open market, for land which he
was nominally selling at £4OOO. Further, it
gave the Government power to go behind
Parliamentary agreements, and vary the terms
of purchases sanctioned by Parliament, and to
take over land at no fixed value at all, merely
leaving things to be settled afterwards by ex-
pensive law proceedings or arbitration. The
Bulletin exposed all the ugly possibilities of
the proposed system when the Bill first appeared,
and almost immediately afterwards it dropped
out of sight, and has never since been heard of.
New South Wales badly wants an honest and
efficient Land for Closer Settlement scheme ; but
because it wouldn’t accept one whose sole ap-
parent purpose was to enable the politician’s
uncle to sell out secretly to the Government at
a price which might be twice or twelve times
the value of the property, it has been compelled
so far to go without. It will be interesting to
notice if the lost measure reappears next session,
or if it and the Local Government scheme and
the much-promised State Bank have all gone
permanently into one grave.
Murder Made Easy.
The state of N.S.W. law in regard to death
certification is singularly idiotic. In the first
place, a medical certificate is not compulsory,
though it is commonly got under the mistaken
notion that the law demands it. It is legally
sufficient for the tenant of the house to report
particulars to the Registrar within a month,
and, as a matter of fact, 10 per cent, of N.S.W.
deaths are registered yearly without a medical
certificate. Under the present law, murder
can be most easily committed under the very
nose of the authorities without danger or with
extremely small danger of detection. For
example, a doctor has under Ins care a patient
seriously ill. He sees him on Tuesday
night; on Wednesday morning the “ friends,”
having murdered the man overnight, tell
the doctor his patient is dead. The
doctor, knowing the serious condition of
the night before, gives a certificate without
viewing the body (for the law does not require
the doctor to view it), the undertaker sends the
coffin, the body is duly buried, and once more
the adage “ murder will out ” fails utterly.
Again, a doctor sees a man who is very ill.
That night the patient is removed elsewhere,
and under another name recovers. Meanwhile,
his friends have, as before, got a certificate from
the first attendant; an undertaker buries a
stone-filled coffin on viewing the certificate;
insurance money is claimed and got; and the
dead man and his friends divide the spoil in
another country. And such crimes and frauds
will remain possible and easy so long as medical
certificates are not absolutely necessary, and,
even should certificates be made necessary,
unless and until the law demands the viewing
of the actual corpse by the signing doctor.
Dear Bulletin, —The question of law reform
is surrounded by difficulties which your recent
contributor ignores. Law, undoubtedly, should
be general, equal, and certain. The legal rule
is, generally speaking, plain enough ; the un-
certainty arises when you attempt to apply the
rule to a certain set of facts. The process of
muling the facts, which are most frequently
disputed, and applying the rule to them is one
ot a purely scientific character. The ever-
increasing complexity of modern life renders it
impossible for any Act of Parliament to do
anything but lay down a general principle,
winch must be applied to each case as it arises
Ihe law may say “ Do not steal ” ; but it then
has to be settled what kinds of taking are
theft. At first sight it seems easy enough to
say what acts fall within this prohibition and
what not; but cases arise which it is difficult to
place on one side or other of the line dividing
the two classes. No matter how carefully de
tails are laid down by statute this will in some
measure remain true. The question of inter-
pretation arising in any case may be reduced to
this form : Granted the law is that the con-
ditions A give rise to the legal consequences X.
does the specific set of facts m fall within the
genus A . It is the difficulties inherent in such
an investigation which give rise to the crv of
the uncertainty of the law.—R.R. J
The cables say (and it is probably true, never-
theless) that—“ The United States ironclad
lexas, 6300 tons displacement, has sunk in
Brooklyn naval dockyard, settling down in the
niud at the bottom.” The alleged reason is
a broken valve below water-line. A warship is
about as reliable as G. H. Reid’s finance. Two
years ago the U.S. ironclad Atlantis tried its
ram on a decayed derelict that was spoiling the
appearance of the ocean. The derelict was
scientifically rammed, but continued to float,
while the Atlantis crawled in a precarious con-
dition back to port. If the weather had not
been good, the mysterious disappearance of a
warship would have been recorded. When the
British ironclad Camperdown did its celebrated
feat of ramming the flagship Victoria, the Vic-
toria turned turtle and went to the bottom of
the Mediterranean—but the Camperdown only
escaped the same experience by a stroke of
luck. Even naval experts know what brittle
theories warships are. That is why the Aus-
tralian squadron never gets into Bass Straits
except during the fine weather at Melb. Cup
time. Let a warship out in the dark or in the
wind—and there is immediate danger that it
will run into itself or fall overboard. That is
why the Victorian miracle of defence, the
Nelson, is always connected by telephone with
the mainland.
Cornelius Job Ham, of the Vic. Leg. Council,
is much shocked by a clause in the Insolvency
Law Amendment Act which provides that the
court shall not release an estate “ unless the
offer of composition appears reasonable and cal-
culated to benefit the general body of creditors.”
Ham can’t understand why the creditors should
not be left to accept or reject the composition
as they may think fit. The innocence of Ham
is pathetic, all things considered. He has lived
all these years and been photographed in civic
robes—he looks a veritable archangel in those
robes—and yet he has never heard of bogus
creditors forming a three-fourths “majority”
to overrule the objections of the genuine
“ minority ” that can’t help itself. If every in-
solvent’s schedule were a truthful statement of
debts and assets the composition offered would
always be reasonable, and the court would in-
variably release the estate. The new clause is
intended to protect bona-fide creditors from
being compelled (as they are compelled under
the present law) to accept the majority vote of
other creditors whose claims will be regarded
with reasonable suspicion by his Honor. There
is much talk about “deserving insolvents” in
Vic. Leg. Council. Among traders there are
very few deserving insolvents who can’t pay 7s.
in the £.
N.S. Wales Assembly is carrying government
by caucus to the extent of not even pretending
to turn up to roll-call (by the way, why don’t,
they have roll-call ?). Everything having been
“ cut-and-dried ” beforehand, the inference
taken seems to be that there really is no neces-
sity why anyone should be “ in the House” on
any bill, or motion, except the bare mover and
seconder and the well-paid Speaker. Small
wonder that there was a rush after the billet
of Parliamentary caterer ! But isn’t all this
too expensive and time-wasting? Why not
this way: Let the “ proceedings ” be printed
beforehand, the House open formally with
prayer, and Jollyanfat (prayer without fast-
ing for him) rise grandiosely and say—“ Mist-
speaker begmove Proceedings be taken as
printed.” Lyne {if present) : “ Mistspeak beg-
second.” Speaker: “ Quesch’n is thathese
Proceedingstaken asprinted. Alltbat pinion
say Aye; cont No ; I savit.” Jollyanfat :
“Begmove House d’now ’journ.” Lyne :
“Begseck.” Speaker: “ Ques is Housenow-
journ those that pinion say A cont No I savit.
House nowjourned.” This would be ever so
much cheaper, ever so much more effective, and
ever so much jollyanfatter all round. And
ever so much more dignified than the present
“ tomfoolery on wages.”
That cumbrously-named N.S.W. institution,
“ The Deaf and Dumb and Blind,” situated at
Newtown-road, Sydney, held its annual meet-
ing the other day under the presidency of Gov.
Hampden, and passed a vote of thanks to the
N.S.W. Government and Parliament for a sub-
sidy of ,£450 received during the year. Then
Rev. S. W. Wilkinson moved that the compiittee
for the ensuing year consist of the Rev. Dr.
Sellors, Rev. Luke Parr, Rev. W. Dunstan,
Rev. Joseph Oram, and Rev. Si Wilkinson.
This motion was seconded by Rev. Dr. Sellors
and carried. The foundation-stone of the Deaf
and Dumb and Blind Institution was laid a
quarter of a century ago by Gov. Belmore,
and then and for 20 years after it boasted free-
dom from sectarianism in fact and in color.
What is the atmosphere with which a commit-
tee, constituted as above, can be supposed to
surround any State-subsidised establishment ?

The “Ironmaster,” which forms this
week’s bill at Sydney Lyceum, is a distinctly
powerful and effective drama, though its motive
is not especially new, and the same idea has been
worked up often, both before and afterwards,
generally by much worse craftsmen than Ohnet.
There is some faint semblance of the problem play
about it: its moral is that the good, high souled
woman who has made, for some cause or another,
a loveless marriage with a mere casual acquaint-
ance, has the same horror of being pawed by her
husband and of being left alone with him at
night in a strange house as if he were any
other stranger. The fact that a certain law-
ful formula had been mumbled over them
doesn’t ease the situation in the least. The
central fact of the “ Ironmaster ” is that not even
16cwt. of marriage service can make it one whit
less horrible for a woman of fine feelings to be
pawed under the aforesaid circumstances, and the
restof thedramanaturally evolvesout of this. There
are quite a lot of fine situations in it, and an un-
fathomable amount of soul. It is a play wherein
the anguished heroine persistently beats her chest,
like the gorilla dolefully smacking himself in the
great African jungle ; but it presents the pro-
blem in a perfectly clean aspect, and is bright
and eloquent and garnished with four appropriate
intervals for drinks. Mrs. Potter has now got
her mannerisms down to zero—her zero, that is.
She still expresses emotion by throwing her head
back with her eyes closed and her hand clutching
her breast, and petrifies in that attitude; also she
turns up her eyes in the old painful manner and
has the same old difficulties with her hair. But
she condenses herself more, and doesn’t sit and
think on so much furniture, and her strange accent
is more under control, and she has ceased to paint
her forehead, and on the whole her Claire de
Beaulieu is a really good performance in almost
every point.
• * • *
Bellew’s hero goes one better ; it is a very
fine performance in every point. It is difficult to
suggest any matter in which the manly ironmaster
could be much improved, unless the hat which he
wears at the duel was made a less glaring misfit.
The rest of the co. may be set down as satisfac-
tory. Miss Linda Raymond, who has steadily
improved of late, adorns the wicked Duchess with
a glassy smile and a dazzling set of teeth and a
cast-iron laugh and a general aspect of depraved
flitter which are very realistic, and Montgomery’s
>uke is noteworthy, partly for its own sake, partly
for the actor’s growing resemblance to Frank
Cates, deceased. Other people, including Oily
Deering, arouse no special execration, and Orlando
Daly is specially commendable, inasmuch as it
appears from the bill that he plays two separate
characters who come in together; and it is only-
one man in a hundred who can enter along with
himself in a state of absolute calm. This journal
here smothers its emotion by remarking that the
“ Merchant of Venice ” is billed for Saturday.
* # * *
The trouble between the rival Sydney man-
agements, after raging furiously for some days,
arrived at a peaceful settlement. It was an in-
teresting disturbance while it lasted. The old
management flatly refused to quit, and wouldn’t
let the new management’s people in to paint the
scenery for their forthcoming production. When
they did get in they were promptly hustled out.
Finally, acting on legal advice, they lay down flat
on the floor, and refused to stir. There being
three policemen present it was impossible to shift
them with hot water or similar severe measures,
so they were carried out and deposited on the
pavement. The old management issued writs
against the new one claiming large damages for
slander, libel, assault, illegal entry, disturbance,
midnight marauding, moral incendiarism and pro-
spective breach of the peace, or something to that
effect. Also, it took to sleeping on the premises
as a guarantee of possession, and it is vaguely
alleged that someone representing the new
management came round and whistled at it disre-
spectfully through the keyhole. Both manage-
ments went round and warned the paiiers against
inserting the other fellow’s advt. Next, the new
management applied to the Courts for an injunc-
tion, or a carnation, or a pelargonium, or some-
thing, and got a temporary one, good for 24 hours.
And then, just as the public had made up its mind
that the trouble would end in both plays being
performed simultaneously on the same stage, with
the giddy, frivolous Chinatowners falling over the
serious dead body in “ Face to Face,” and the
deserted wife moaning with her infant up against
the lady whose fancy dress stopped at her knees,
and that the two proprietors would denounce each
other from the footlights like the two-headed
infant in a state of disagreement, it was announced
that a settlement had been arrived at. All the
cases were withdrawn, and everybody parted in a
dreadful state of mutual esteem, and it wasn’t even
explained whether the two firms had played
Yankee-grab for the theatre, or had fought 18
rounds in the paint-room up against the wet
scenery.
* * * *
The first night of the Firm’s management
at Her Majesty’s was a big affair. H.M.’s, with
its bright coloring, its spacious vestibule, and its
vast dimensions, ought undoubtedly to be the
fashionable theatre of Sydney—no other one can
compare with it as a first-class show-house; and
the instant W. and M. took possession it started to
blossom like the big sunflower or the bread-fruit
tree. All the daughters of Judah were there in
barbaric magnificence, and all their fathers rolled
up, and created a general impression that der
synagogue was oudt. The audience, which was an
immense one, mostly came in late with its jewellery
clattering like crockery before a gale, and it talked
loud all through the first act, both of which are
reliable signs of fashion; the audience in the Rignold
and Woods and Cosgrove days kept silence, and
never banged its bedizened bosom deafeningly
with its fan. The “ Trip to Chinatown ” went
much better than at its original first night. It was
45 minutes shorter, so that it didn’t leave the same
feeling of deadly weariness behind it, and an airy,
frivolous play can look bright when it ends at 11,
whereas the same play ending at 11.45 makes the
listener feel like Job wearily scraping himself with
a potsherd on that ancient ash-heap in Uz. The
principal change in the cast is the substitution of
Julian Mitchell for the departed Patrice as Willie
Crow. Patrice didn’t look at all like a male per-
son, so the new man, who looks painfully male, is
much more convincing. Also, though The Bul-
letin prefers his performance to the other, he
looks so old and tired and leaden-eyed that his
name doesn’t fit ; no one could suppose for a
moment that he hadn’t left off growing 10 years
ago at least. Harry Conor is as humorous as
aforetime, and Frank Lawton’s wonderfully
melodious whistle is all it used to be. Sadie
M‘Donald, whom some veracious chronicler repre-
sented as crippled for life through dislocation of
the back in a high kick, seems perfectly fresh and
in good condition. Which same remark applies to
Bessie Clayton, the dancing contortionist, and the
bright-eyed widow, Geraldine M‘Garin. And
“Chinatown” in general is the same airy frivol
about nothing that it was aforetime, and the
frothy head on it is just as unstable as of old. The
“Milk White Flag” follows on Saturday for one
week, the last of the present co.
* * * *
George Rignold re-opens Sydney Royal on
Saturday with a stupendous blast of “ Henry V.”
The Bulletin, having just founded its great
Society for Being Rather Tired of Shakespeare,
has grave doubts whether “ Hen. V.” is quite the
thing Sydney wants most at present, but Melb.
reports of this latest revival leave no doubt that it
is a great pageant, full of historical accuracy and
ancient frill, well-played, well-mounted, and
showing G. Rignold at his best. And G.R. at his
best is something very considerable.
