Another Cinematographe the ‘ ‘ only
authentic ” one, run by Sestier and Barnett—was
announced to put in an appearance at Sydney
Criterion on Tuesday night, and as the advt. says
it includes a complete panorama of the Melb. Cup
and a special picture of Newhaven it ought to
boom beyond all precedent. How it does boom
will be fully expounded next week. Meanwhile,
it is something beautifully appropriate that the
first Australian picture presented by the new
machine should be a horse-race. Of course it had
to be either that or a football-match.
* * * *
The well-known and beloved G. L. Good-
man quits the “Firm” shortly to boss Adams’s
Palace of Varieties, and the Firm proposes to
express its regret at the severance by inflicting a
testimonial-benefit on its old guide and mentor,
said atmospheric disturbance to take place at the
Lyceum on the afternoon of next Thursday (Dec.
3). As the immensely rich relative who died, or
was murdered, or otherwise pegged out in London
some time ago, and left a portion of his stupendous
pile to Goodman, has now proved only worth £5
or so, and Goodman’s share doesn’t nearly cover
his proportion of the law and burial and tombstone
expenses, Sydney can roll up to his testimonial
with a light heart. There seems something incon-
gruous about helping to testimonialise a friend
who has just come in for a half-billion or so, but
in the case of a friend who didn’t come in for any-
thing, and had to subscribe towards the tombstone
as well, the position is different. The Firm’s
manager is so universally popular that his farewell
show should be held at Her Majesty’s, the only
theatre large enough to accommodate all the people
who ought to be there.
* * *
‘ ‘ Stars of All Nations, ” at Melb. Princess’s,
is a novel show, insofar that it represents about
two-thirds of an ordinary-sized variety-entertain-
ment doing duty for a full programme in a theatre
where much is expected, and prices rank accord-
ingly. High-toned accessories and surroundings
cannot disguise the fact that twelve music-hall
artists served up in eight courses, with orchestral
overture and operatic selections, form a thin menu
in comparison with former generous spreads pro-
vided at the glittering Princess’s. “Stars of All
Nations,” there is reason to fear, will not beam
upon surging audiences for four consecutive weeks
unless either the tariff be reduced or the company
of stars enlarged.
♦ » » *
Henry Lee, who comes to us with a high
reputation as a comedian, impersonates Great
Men past and present, from Shakespeare to Glad-
stone, and is so confident of his ground that he
actually has the nerve to recite or talk in each
character. R. G. Knowles, “ the very peculiar
comedian,” is a genuine success, despite an unin-
cisive voice, and manages to patter off a series
of gags more or less old, with an air
of freshness and spontaneous-seeming humor.
Clotilde Antonio, a trim-built little contortionist,
apparently in her teens, will be considered a
fetching feature of the show. This small sister to
Adelina Antonio does all that is accomplished by
the muscular Eclairs (of Fitzgerald Bros.’co.), and
more besides. Her most surprising feat is the
only ungraceful item on her list. Balanced upon
her head, with her legs extended “ split ”-
wise, she scrapes a popular melody from a
violin. It is a gruesome caricature of the popular
tune, but even a musical critic couldn’t slate the
fiddling of a damsel so strangely situated. The
Delevines, three Satanic gambollers of the Girard
class, are a good turn, also four other turns are
neat and pleasing. Arthur Garner has drawn
upon ’Murka for the bulk of the company, and
been careful not to engage any rubbish. Perhaps
this entertainment errs on the side of respect-
ability. The happiest music-hall turn is not
always the most virtuous, for the heart of man is
desperately wicked at all times, and his best girl
likes to blush once in a way.
** * *
The variety show at the Opera House is as
meaty as usual. On Saturday night Rickards
arrived again, looking ten years younger than his
average age, and his disciples found him fully up
to previous sample. The house, which rose at
Arry before he started to sing, was loth to let him
go. In fact, the only person glad to let Rickards
go was his own perennial self, who went vigorously
all the time.
** * *
Bland Holt’s gigantic bare-legged drama,
known—for want of a more suitable title—as
“ One of the Best,” was set up at Melb. Royal on
Saturday night. A conflagration of city ware-
houses broke out in opposition to this gaudy
spectacle, but an enormous attendance at the fire
made no appreciable difference to the gathering
inside the good old shop, from which fact one may
assume that the new season’s goods have come to
fill a public want. “One of the Best,” as The
Bulletin remarked six months ago, is a military
melodrama in which grim-visaged war gets no
show whatever. It doesn’t deal with a departure
of troops for the Soudan, followed by fearful
slaughter of dancing dervishes and waving of the
Union Jack, after the last round of car-
tridges has been served out to a beleagured
garrison. Some peaceful treachery by Cosgrove
at Walter Baker’s expense forms the plot. Baker
has been suffering from Cosgrove’s treachery ever
since they began to act together, also a misguided
damsel in distress has had reason to curse the day
when Cosgrove aforesaid first folded her to his
black heart, and still they allow the remorseless
person to go on stealing papers under easy cir-
cumstances, as though nothing were previously
known against his character. In this instance
Cosgrove steals British army secrets from a
safe in the major-general’s office, aud the
crime being inevitably fixed upon Baker
the army assembles in great force—probably the
greatest force ever known at the Royal—to see
Baker drummed out of the service. Then the
misguided girl, driven mad as usual by Cosgrove’s
bitter sneer, gives him away to the majah-general,
consequently Baker is drummed back into the
army for all he is worth, and the curtain falls upon
a picture of that experienced hero thanking
Heaven that he has kept his philabeg unspotted
before the world.
* * * *
Outside the lavish display of Highland
regiment and mounted infantry (or officers on
horseback) “ One of the Best ” is not a particu-
larly exciting drama. It contains too much dia-
logue and not enough incident. About half of
the dialogue is divided between Bland and Mrs.
Holt, who supply the story with a comic relief
which never varies in tone or purpose. Mrs. Holt
doesn’t recognise Bland when he appear in a kilt,
and Bland doesn’t recognise her when she
appears in “rationals.” In the first act
they talk freely of getting married, and
in the last act they are still talking on
the same subject, and, during the meantime,
they have talked of very little else. Nevertheless,
the consummate cheerfulness of these skilled comic
relief-givers enables them to get through with
honors. Miss Watson, Miss Franees Ross, Charles
Brown, Harford, and other people, including
Albert Norman (whose speaking voice is a lighter
and more wobblesome tenor than ever), and not
forgetting Corlesse, participate in Bland Holt’s
latest. The Bulletin was about to say latest
triumph, but it will wait for further evidence on
this point.

“ A Mother of Three ” is now concluding
a giddy career of four weeks at Melb. Bijou. On

mention that the Past doesn’t contain a serious

is a farcical Past, and Mrs. Ponderbury herself is
a frivolous item in three acts.
** * *
At a matinee benefit to the Frantz Family
last week, a fine manly figure of a military per-
sonage appeared upon Melb. Opera House stage
and spoke a few words, in a deep, resonant voice,
to the effect that he was handing to the Family
some souvenirs of the admiration which their
athletic entertainment had aroused in amateur
athletic circles. Then the warrior hastily disposed
of the souvenirs, and retired in not-undignified
confusion. Such incidents as this always remind
one that cheek is the first requisite for a public
performer. The amateur spouter had an imposing
stage appearance, a voice big enough to fill two
theatres, and a hearty manner of speaking his
words—but being troubled by the idea that he
was making a fool of himself, he felt truly happy
to escape from public view. What a contrast to
the ordinary actor who, sans appearance, voice, or
“ heart ” to recommend him, can strike an attitude
in the centre of the stage and proclaim himself the
gallant hero he thinks he is every time he gets a
chance!

One of the best-played minor parts in Rignold’s
Henry V. was a corpse in the battle-scene. He was an
intelligent corpse with a fine eye for grouping. He lav
with his arm covering his face, and, to impart a touch of
realism, a soldier rolled him over. He had to move his
arm to allow the roll to proceed, but, once stationary
again, he covered his face with it once more, and lav
calm in the peace of picturesque death.

Dear B.— I wanted a play typed last week, and a city
agency undertook to provide me with a “ typiste.” But
the young lady selected, a discreet ingenue of about 22,
flatly refused the job when informed of the nature of the
typing she was required to do. “Oh ! I couldn't possibly
type a play,” she exclaimed. Notwithstanding a good
pay-prospect and the threat that her name would be taken
off the agency’s books if she declined, she resolutely stuck
to it that her parents wouldn't allow her to type " thea-
trical stuff,” and departed with a fine air of outraged
decency. This statement may look dizzy, hut it’s frozen
fact.—B.E.
The putrescent Purity Party (writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent) were annihilated before the annual
entertainments tribunal, and the giddy • musie’alls
marched forth virtuously victorious. Dry, quartz-faced
gins wearing eye-windows to intensify tlie glassiness of
their stony stare, a id limp, black, rusty robes that rustled
against their sapless .frames, testified with cicada-shrill-
ness to the voluptuous abandon and abandoned volup-
tuousness of the devilishly-delirious “halls.” One fruit-
less searcher after the fascinating secret of polygamic
passion told the Licensing Committee in a reptilian
whisper that in the Oxford Promenade she had heard a
depraved woman say “ WELL ! ” to a debauched man.
The defending manager suggested that it was merely a
brief way of telling the man he was a “ deep ’un,” but
Mrs. Chant’s colleague affirmed that the female’s vocal
tone rang erotic echoes.
A. G. Knowles’ well-known “ or what ” gag came in for
branding, but the duff-headed, French-bean-bodied par-
son’s wife lost the point among her own pronounced
physical peaks, and the condemnatory count fell
fiat. And the gag is this :- “ Basely ungrateful, she
turned on me one day and asked, ‘Do you mean mar-
riage, or what?’ ‘And —I—said— ‘or what.’ So she
left me, and I’ve been an ‘or whater’ ever since.” It
speaks well for the London halls when this is the most
formidable indictment that can be brought against them
in a twelve months, and, in fact, the clever “ turns,” giant
“ chuckers-out, ’ and business-like mercenariness of the
international vestals combine to rob adorned seductive-
ness of its charm, and make the halls dully respectable to
all but touring cricketers and village chawbacons.

When Barney Levy was a lot younger than he is now
and travelling in advance of Dan Barry, he was asked to
go on to a country town and enquire as to biz prospects
and engage a hah. “ Keep down expenses,” entreated
Barry, as they parted. Next day Barry received a wire
occupying nianj' telegraph forms ; something like this:
“ Dear Dan, —I have arrived here in good health after
pleasant journey—fine town—good pub. to stop at—
pleasant, lively people—large farming district—good
crops—prosperous times—all think you would do very
well —splendid hall, fine seating accommodation, cen-
trally situated, good dressing-rooms, good stage-splen-
did stands. At this point ordered beer for the company.
In fact everything that is calculated to give us a thor-
oughly good season, but that another company has
engaged the hall for a long period. Shall I continue on?”
Then Daniel Barry calculated the cost of the wire and
lifted up his voice in prayer.

The Wild-Cat Column.
The Bank of New South Wales states that the
net profit for the half-year ended 30th September is
£87,660, a slight improvement on the £86,460 and
£84,737 of the two previous periods. A 9 per cent,
dividend absorbs £87,458, and £202 added to previous
balance makes £22,331 carried forward. The paid-up
capital is now £1,946,940. The reserve has been increased
by £6673 premiums on new shares, and stands at
£1,190,250, so the total shareholders’ funds are over
£3,100,000. The business keeps steadily expanding 1 , and
the coin reserve alone now stands at the enormous figure
of £6,635,337. Taking coin, Government debentures, &c.,
together, the B.N.S.W. has £7,920,000 of liquid assets,
equal to more than 40 per cent, of its entire liabilities to
the public. These immense reserves don’t help much in
the making of big profits, but they render the Bank of
N.S.W. distinctly the strongest institution of its kind in
Australia and one of the strongest in the world.
♦ * * *
Re the Queensland National Bank disaster it is
interesting to consider what Premier Nelson said about that
broken-up institution at the time of the ’93 suspension.
In those days he personally overhauled and audited the
Q.N.8., or thought he did. ' Said Nelson : “The duty has
devolved upon me of making a most minute and searching
examination into the affairs of this institution. That I
have done, devoting nearly the whole of my time to it
since the House adjourned about a fortnight ago. I have
given the matter the most thorough and the most search-
ing examination. I have had an opportunity of seeing
all the securities of the bank, and even the confidential
books belonging to that institution. I have also had the
able assistance of my colleague, the Secretary for Lands,
who has had a large experience previously in banking
concerns; and the result of our investigations is that I
am able to report, as I have already reported to my col-
leagues, that, in my opinion, in which my colleague, the
Secretary for Lands, concurs, the assets of the bank,
taken at a fair and reasonable valuation, and assuming
any fair method of realisation, are more than sufficient to
pay the debts of the bank to the Government, as well as
to the outside public. I give that opinion after
the most mature and deliberate consideration,
and after having made myself acquainted and be-
come seized of the whole of the facts connected
with that bank.” Now, considering that the latest
report distinctly implies that the bank wasn’t sound in
’93, despite Nelson’s conclusion, arrived at after “ most
thorough and most searching examination,” and seeing
“all the securities” and being acquainted with “the
whole of the facts ” and with “ most mature and deliber-
ate consideration” on top of all, does Nelson regard him-
self simply as an incompetent financier and his colleague
as a bewildered ass, or what do they both think they are ?
The proposal that the shareholders of the Q.N.
Bank should go out without being called upon to pay up
one farthing of the uncalled capital towards reducing the
shortage, and that the depositors should stand all the loss
that isn’t covered by the money now paid-up, looks more
and more like an awful piece of assurance. The share-
holders, in the ordinary course of affairs, would be called
on to pay the £360,000 still uncalled, and then be sum-
marily ejected because—there still being a deficit of a
million or two in the assets—they have no visible interest
in the concern. There might even be a demand for the
return of the unearned dividends paid since ’93. The
proposal made by, or on behalf of, the shareholders,
is simply that, in return for the unconditional surrender
of the deficiency, and as a reward for letting the deposi-
tors find their own way out of the muddle, which they
had no hand in creating, and as a reward for having the
whole burden of the" Government’s preferential claim
slumped on to them, they are to let the shareholders off
all their liability. Otherwise : “In consideration of me
refusing to account for one sum of money I owe you, you
will let me off paying the other.”
One thing is certain : It is no earthly good put-
ting the Q.N.B. into liquidation if it can be carried on in
some decently honest fashion, even at a big sacrifice.
Liquidation means stopping what income there is, and
even a very bad bank has some kind of liquid business
yielding an income. It means heavy liquidation expenses,
and big legal charges, and the locking-up of what cash
there is, and cash is scarce enough up North just now
without locking-up any more. Also, it means forcibly
putting a vast amount of property into a dreadfully sick
market, some of which property might get better in time,
and doesn’t look as if it could get very much worse. But
unless there are very different managers and very different
management it is better to liquidate ; if anything like the
old crowd is to run the show, the Q.N.B. hasn’t the
shadow of a chance of ever being a decent institution.
* * * *
For the third half-year in succession S.A. Go-
vernment has paid its interest at the Agent-General’s
office without a hitch. The cost is £lOO a year for each
million of the public debt, whereas the bank used to
charge £250 per million. The saving is fully £2OOO a year.
Some of the other provinces still pay over £4OO a year for
the “ management ” of each million of their public debt
the Agent-General, as shown above, could do it for a
quarter the amount, and if Australia was federated, and
there was only one public debt managed by one Agent-
General, it could probably be done for one-eighth the
amount. The saving on this little unconsidered item
alone should be £40,000 a year at the very least —enough
to pay interest and sinking-fund on £1,000,000 of debt.
The fact that “ leading financiers” —the same who demon-
strate the impossibility of a State Bank—proved the S.A..
idea, when first mooted, to be absurd, impracticable,
ruinous, childish, and the invention of somebody quite
ignorant of finance, is only another evidence that leading
financiers are largely liars and fools. Maoriland, by the
way, tried to follow S.A.’s example, and discovered that
her old financiers had so tied up her early loans that the
management can’t be shifted from the Bank of England,
so she goes on being bled as before. The other five pro-
vinces apparently go on being bled because they are
accustomed to it.
* * * *
Victorian 4 per cent, locally-held stock to the
amount of £2,085,000 falls due in about three months, and
Premier Turner proposes to issue new stock in place of
it, probably at 3 per cent. Nothing definite is said about
issuing it locally, but presumably that must be intended ;
Mr. Turner declared some time ago that he intended
in future to rely on the local market, and he is generally
regarded as a man of his word. Moreover, to deliberately
abolish a local stock to replace it by an English one would
be a truly awful example of the blessed virtue of self-
reliance. If a local 3 per cent, loan can be floated at par
it is a decidedly great idea. But if the same loan were
put on the London market, which is very different from
what it was a few months ago when the Westralian 3 per
cent, razzle-dazzle came off so gloriously, it almost cer-
tainly would not fetch par, and the Bull Government
would take £12,500 oijit of it for stamp duty, and banks
and brokers would take at least £12,500 more for expenses,,
and the holders would give none of their interest back in
income-tax as local holders do. Therefore, Turner, like
the other two Treasurers who are on the same lay, if he
really issues at 3 per cent, instead of (say) 3j, is offering
the Victor a i a good deal less than he would probably
have to offer John Bull under the same circumstances.

Cornwall Fire and Marine Insurance Co. (Tas.)
shows a net profit for the past year of £5951. The insur-
ance business yields only £1846 of this, and "he remaining
£4105 is interest realised on the investment of the co. s
own capital, so the Cornwall is doing nothing very mag-
nificent. A dividend of 61 per cent, absorbs the usual
£5OOO, and leaves a total credit balance of £7451. ine
Cornwall has no reserve fund ; it has toiled at the good
work of insuring combustible and sinkable property foi
55 years, and paid £185,000 in dividends, and this accu-
mulated £7451 is all that it has left to show for its labors.
Also the net premium income is £13,281 —not much after
more than half-a-century.
* * * *
A proposal is on hand for the amalgamation of
the National Mutual Life Association and the Mutual Life
of Victoria. This is the sort of thing which has long
been wanted. There are distinctly too many assurance
offices in Australia for the business which the present
population can supply, and as the result of depression
and over-competition five large offices have shown an
actual decrease in business for several years past. Too
many office premises and managers and staffs in propor-
tion to the available trade also run the expenses far too
high. Amalgamation means making one staff and set of
offices, managers, directors, auditors, advts., &c., do the
work of two, and therefore it means less expense and
bigger bonuses. The National Mutual and the Mutual of
Victoria have both a good reputation, and if they amalga-
mate they will make the second largest office in Australia,
and the 'third largest—the A.M.P. and Maoriland Govt.
Department being first and second—in Australasia. The
benefit to the policy-holders through saving of expenses
should be very considerable, especially to the policy-
holders of the Mutual of Victoria, whose expense rate is
high, and its business, despite the apparent absolute
soundness of the institution, not at all progressive.
* * * *
A few new legal difficulties—about four of them
are alleged to have arisen about the N.S.W. land-tax.
It is claimed (1) that as the Appeal Court was not estab-
lished before the tax-notices were issued as provided by
the Act, these notices are null and void ; (2) that the
Appeal Court, not having been established before the
issue of notices, is null and void also ; (3) that the action
of the Commissioners in privately receiving notices of
objection and adjusting disputes is illegal, because there
is nothing in the Act authorising the Commissioners to
act as a Court of Appeal; (4) that the whole business
needs to be started again from the beginning to put
things on a right basis. This rather sultry legal pro-
nouncement was submitted to Parliament, and Premier
Reid merely stated that he “ had taken a higher opinion.”
But as he didn’t state whether the higher opinion said
that the whole business was really null and void or not,
this didn’t advance things much.
» * * *
That quaint, old-fashioned concern, the Mutual
Fire Insurance Co., of Tasmania, announces that it is
doing well. This co. never states its premium income,
income from investments, losses or working expenses, to
supply information being incompatible with its old Van-
demonian dignity. In an intricate sort of way, however,
the fact can be dug out of the statement of assets that
the profits seem to be £3770, and the dividend has gone
down from 10 to 8 per cent. Also, the reserve (£10,000)
has been stationary for at least nine years past.

* * * *
Judge Chomley (Vic.) has decided a point of
some importance to auctioneers and bidders. He held
that where an auctioneer has instructions to conduct a
sale under a reserved price he must sell for cash and not
on credit. That is, it is to be presumed, unless an agree-
ment to the contrary is notified.

A Bulletin man, the other day, met two
teamsters—Australians— tramping with 10 horses apiece
and 7-ton loads of groceries from Sydney to Orange.
Rail-freight, in 7-ton lots, £4 7s. per ton. Their freight,
£4 per ton. Time of journey, eight days. Back loading,
7 tons wool, at £2 10s. per ton. Cost of horse-feed, £1
per day per team. Value of waggon, new, £65 ; of team,
£l5O. Nett profits—after allowance for wear and tear of
team and waggon, shoeing, maintenance of men, and all
incidentals—£s per week per man. Employed “ nearly
all the year.” Some teamsters were working off the
storekeeper’s dead-horse in winter-feed, &c.—not they.
Would thev have a drink? Thanks; they didn’t drink.
’« * * •
Economies are necessarily practised in every
possible direction at, and in connection with, the Broken
Hill mines, and, as a result, a good deal of sand-like con-
centrates and ore is sent away loose. Railway trucks at
the best are not water-tight, but those on the Broken Hill
line being rather more open than usual, heavy losses of
metal are complained of. The Junction is the heaviest
loser, chiefly on account of the sandy character of its
ore. ’ The traffic is worth specially catering for, of course ;
but in ordinary circumstances the answer to the com-
plaint would lie that concentrates and fine ore are not
proper things to carry loose. Anyway, the man who
would send away his wheat unbagged and lost it would
win little sympathy ; and wheat has no greater capacity
for “running” than these mine products.
The South is not being rushed for its lovv-in-
silver concentrates, and a big pile is accumulating at the
mine.
There is tremendous rivalry between the Han-
cock and the May jig people, and an army of mine-share-
holding “ barrackers ” has been gathered round each ; but
so far no fair comparative test has been made. Lately
the B.H. Pro. has been making a long series of experi-
ments, and officers generally are said to conclude that for
their purpose the May machines are the more suitable.
The Central is also adopting that class of machine. At
the British, however, the Hancock plant is, as pre-
viously noted, doing exceedingly good work. And the
North, after trying a May plant (erected certainly under
difficulties) has resolved upon adding a Hancock plant.
While “barrackers” for both processes are being ener-
getically whipped up it is as well to remember that much
depends upon the manner in which the product is to be
disposed of. Speaking generally (and that is all that is
possible on account of the extreme variation in the ore),
Hancock’s seems the better adapted for bringing up a
large quantity of ore to a moderate fineness ; May’s for
bringing up a smaller quantity of ore to a higher stan-
dard. And yet if that bold generalisation stands it is
difficult to see why the B.H. Pro., which smelts its own
stuff on or near the mine, should prefer the May—unless
a wholesale shifting of works is contemplated.
The North continues to put out its 000 ft. level
west cross-cut without profitable results or prospects
thereof. Junction North appearances are also, at the
least, no better than they were a couple of months back,
on account of the occurrence of a large intrusion of
country rock. But as good ore-contracts have been made
continued divs. are assured.
Further litigation is probable over the Junction
South (“ Block 41 ”), which, under a new name, the
public are, at time of writing, being invited to subscribe
into.

The Sunday Times understands that the flota-
tion of Barrass and Conley’s property at Cobar is assured,
and congratulates those who have bought the shares. The
writer congratulates those who sold them. Last week the
mine was examined by A. T. Brown, the well-known ex-
pert of the Broken Hill millionaires. Cobar has it that
he is satisfied. Howell is also going to report, it is said.
The mine cleaned up some four or five hundred tons on
Friday last. Result not known at time of writing.
* * * *
Pambula, N.S.W. “The Jingera Mineral Pro-
prietary has knocked off all hands for a time. It is under-
stood that the closing is not on any account due to want
of first-class ore.” And the good, grandmotherly, child-
like Herald trustingly prints yarns of this sort. The
Bulletin pronounces no opinion on the Jingera Pro-
prietary, but it wouldn’t be surprised to read in the
Herald that it had been closed down because of its
enormous richness—that it was far too rich to work. Big
things were foreshadowed for this mine many times, and
the writer hopes the climax has not at last been reached.
But the public will seemingly get a bit of a rest now, and
it badly wants one.

» * * *
The Tomingley Gold-mines (Ltd.) is one of the
latest N.S.W. floats in London. Capital £llO,OO0 —£21,000
cash, balance up to 680,000 in shares. The D.T. London
correspondent says the subscription was only limited, and
attributes it to the state of things in London. It is the
same old business, played in the same old way, and is
called mining. Sometimes it does luckily turn out to be
mining, otherwise it is gambling—a game in which the
owners get a little, the promoters and London crowd get
a heap, and the underwriters, or the public, sooner or
later get stuck with shares and the mine.

mining and metallurgical engineers for the purpose of
developing approved mining properties.

Tuena, N.S.W., has a “boon,” if not a boom,
within measurable distance. The Amos has gone the
way of most N.S.W. mines, the Golden Dyke and others
are in a state of suspension, but “ a copy of the lease
under the M. on P.L. Act on Mr. Jones’ property has
been registered. The labor conditions require 615 men,
which, if fulfilled, will be a boon to the district.”
Gov. Jerrysmith, of Westralia, states in his large,
indefinite way that W.A. will yet prove to be a marvellous
country, and a lot of discoveries remain to be made there
w'hich will startle the world. The Bulletin agrees with
H.E. Some of the discoveries already made there —the
discovery that the Londonderry had dried up, for instance,
and that Bayley’s Rew'ard had closed, and that a borrow
of £7,000,000 was in prospect—not only startled the world
but flabbergasted it. And when a general winding-up of
the wild-cats sets in, things will probably be discovered
that will make the world’s hair stand up straight.
• * » *
Premier Forrest lately cabled the Westralian
Agent-General, asking why the awful slump in the share-
market, adding that nothing had occurred locally, or was
likely to occur, to cause depression or to give anxiety.
The A.G. admitted the “lull,” but “there was every
reason to believe ” —good old stock phrase—it wouldn’t be
of much longer duration. Any man acquainted with
Westralian mining who pretends he can’t account for the
slump is a rogue. If he really can’t, then he is an ass—
and a big one. The list of London co.’s working mines in
W.A. numbers over 200. Of these, two mines produce
more gold than all the rest put together. For a long,
wearisome time the Londoners were fooled by “ want of
machinery.” They have now got machinery. Then it
was a railway that was wanted, and they have now got it.
Then it was the water difficulty. That can never be
wholly removed, but water has in many instances only
helped to kill the mine. Within the last three or four
months a number of English co.’s have crushed. Usually
the first was good, the result of accumulation. Share-
holders are now trying to recover from the shock of sub-
sequent crushings. In other cases it has dawned on Bull
that the rich chutes on some of the mines are so narrow
and short that a few crushings will exhaust the show.
Take the Lady Loch. The first 1000 tons was very good
—went several ozs. When the writer said that that
was not a true test he was howled at in Coolgardie.
Shares were then nearly £4. Why are they now down to
265. ? When the public get a list of the failures in W.A.
it will see the reason for Bull’s nightmare. Fortunately
some really great mines have been found on the field, but
the “ cats ” are so numerous that the real slump has not
set in yet.

Boulder Main Reef, 206 tons, 310 oz. It has also
struck telluride ore, and pains are taken to wire that, in
this instance, the telluride is not associated with calcite
to any great extent, “ the gangue being chiefly blue
quartz and rock closely resembling the so-called diorite.”
From which it will be understood that the other telluride
lodes have vanished, as things composed of calcite often
do. Presumably they are now composed of thin air.
* * * *
Wired from Perth that Mr. Modest Maryanski,
in reporting the discovery of telluride ore in the Boulder
Main Reef, declared that this proved beyond doubt the
permanency of the field. The writer will await confirma-
tion of this weird story. Mr. Maryanski is a most able
man. He is a Pole trained at Freiburg, and was after-
wards metallurgist in Russia ; he has filled big mining
positions in the States and Mexico. He is a strong man,
too, or he would have died from the shock of seeing some
of the mines in which the co. he represents became
heavily interested before his advent.
Brown Hill mine (W.A.) is sending SCO tons to
Dry Creek works, S.A. It has had a lot of bad fortune.
Thousands of pounds were thrown away on a worthless
plant, and, worse luck, the ore-body, which Is very rich on
the surface and down to about 180 ft., suddenly changed
into a comparatively barren body largely composed ol
calcite. This news is too horrible for publication in Cool-
gardie, but it is nevertheless true.
* * * *
Mt. Magnet, W.A., is in a state of collapse.
Of the 269 leases over 200 have hung up during the year,
and the Warden has nothing to do but hear applications
for exemption. Business is paralysed, and store-keepers
threaten to quit unless something is done by Govt.
* * * *
British Australasian is doing its utmost to
keep up the boom-end of the Westralian log, but is grow-
ing disheartened, now that the permanency and richness
of the field has been established, that the market should
be left to the sport of every £2O seller. It wishes success
to the company scheme (a million capital) to repel the
ruinous bear.
* * * *
The Boulder Main Reef mine, Kalgoorlie,
suffers from a name which is usually confounded with
the Great Boulder. It is a block accidentally left out in
the mad rush for leases around the Boulder. As soon as
pegged it was taken over by the Venture syndicate, and
is probably by far the best show of many owned by that
crowd.
* * * *
Wired East that the condensed-water supply at
Kalgoorlie is in excess of the demand, and is now selling
at £1 per 100 gallons. As it is over 20 times the price
of water elsewhere, it is still a little too dear for gardening
and bathing purposes.

Waihi, M.L., 2040 tons for bullion to the value
of £13,143, said to be the record month since the mine
started. Waitekauri, another M.L. mine, 1670 tons for
51660 z. bullion, valued at £5670. The bullion is com-
posed of gold and silver, a common feature in many M.L.
mines. It is time that gold should be calculated in Aus-
tralia by value and not by ounces. That is the system all
over the world ; any other causes confusion. Croydon
gold is described by ounces, although its value is only
half that of pure gold.

A Bulletin correspondent :—This far-away
Warratta (N.S. W.) district may reasonably expect a boom.
Recent erushings at the one-horse local battery have gone
very well. A trial crushing, Hussley’s East Warrata,
yielded 4oz. sdwt. per ton, and 12 tons from Welsh’s West
Warratta gave a total of 14J40z. The Federal lease pro-
mises good yields when properly worked, also the Brilliant
and others should pay decently. Priest Geologist Curran
has recently visited the district and appears to be favor-
ably impressed with the reefs. The field is most effec-
tually isolated from civilisation : the telegraph-line, be-
tween Milparinka and Wanaaring, goes bung about twice
a week, and if one has important business to transact at
the warden’s office he will most probably find that many-
dutied official away looking after tick in travelling cattle.
The ore is highly mineralised and needs more than battery
treatment. The right class of men are acquiring original
interests in the field. Broken Hill men have got large
holdings; also Premier Kingston, E. W. Hawker, J. 11.
Paterson, E. A. Murphy, and other Melbourne and Ade-
laide men.

Tenders are hereby called for staining the
tegs of the alleged Sydney Highland Brigade,
which is mostly named Smith, or words to that
effect. It would be all very well for fine, stalwart,
sun-tanned, hairy-legged savages named Donald
Urquhart, Roderick MTavish, and so on to
parade the streets in kilts ; but for a lot of miser-
able Sassenachs with milk-white knees, evidently
Undressed for the occasion, to exhibit themselves
in the public thoroughfares is, in the opinion of
The Bulletin, to place themselves within the
true scope of the indecent-exposure law.

what such Boards have powers to do. One glori-
ous prerogative is to visit schools—which is joy for
the lydies. Another is to grant four holidays in
the year to each school. It needs to be told that
Vic. schools are indebted to the Board for Cup-

to State schools ! One year the Flemington
Board, moved thereto by the W.C.T.U., decided
to deny the holiday to a local school. In con-
sequence thereof the teachers had to put in an
appearance —to solemnly teach the two small
children who alone answered to the muster-roll.
The staff took the contract in turns—the unem-
ployed section taking up a strategic position on
the back fence, which commanded the finest race-
course in the world. The galling item in the inci-
dent was the fact that the two children belonged
to a publican who had a bar on the course, and
who had sent them to school for safe custody.
The board never again denied Cup-day to that
school, and the Prahran lydies may as well note
the fact.
* * * *
The arrest of the almost-forgotten Sydney
levanter, Armand Jerome, supplies a whole essay
on the art and mystery of getting away. The
common clumsy method of getting away consists
in bolting at full speed for the nearest port, and
rushing, with a pallid expression and a carpet-bag
in each hand, on board the first departing steamer.
Then, if the levanter is not stopped before the
vessel gets away, he is generally blocked at the
first port of call, and brought back. The artistic
way is to lie low in a secluded country spot, and
grow a shaggy beard and let your hair become long,
and get accustomed to thick boots and ancient
clothes, and acquire a coat of sunburn, and learn
the great Australian adjective, and then assume
a swag and patiently fade away into the far west.
If the levanter can drink colonial beer, and play
euchre with appropriate curses, and can walk
without his feet giving out, the chances are that
he may depart steerage in the fullness of time from
Albury or Cooktown, absolutely unnoticed.
Jerome did the first part of this contract all right;
he lay low successfully for months in a cottage not
many miles from Sydney on the Southern line.
But apparently he had no distinct plans beyond
that, and, instead of judiciously going west with a
swag when the first hue and cry was over, he re-
mained till even the police began to be aware of
his existence. The Bulletin has a profound belief
in going west, as the best course for the levanter.
Not only is it better for him—provided, of course,
he doesn’t go west in a bell-topper and a tail-coat
and put up at the best hotels—but it does a little
to open up the country, and promote the develop-
ment of the interior, and do the other things that
the local politician is so anxious about. A steady
stream of absconders would be a godsend to many
a one-horse township lying out on the dusty road
towards the sunset.
* * * *
The uses and abuses of the bicycle are a
dally feature of the police-news. An anxious
Vic. widower was disturbed the other night by an
unusual sound in bis daughter’s bedroom. Pro-
ceeding to investigate, he arrived in time to see a
young man leap out of the window of that apart-
ment into the back-yard. The father, who had
been a crack runner in his day, reckoned that after
the winding the fugitive would get in his drop
from the first floor back and subsequent scramble
over the yard fence it would be easy to run him
down. But, alas, Romeo sailed away on a bike.
“Send references”: In Sydney it is im-
possible to obtain a situation without good local
reference; all testimonials from other provinces
being looked upon as dubious. Therefore, the
new-comer and the bad egg is constrained to ad-
vertise for a clerk, &c., with a note to send refer-
ences. And when they come, he coolly picks out
the best and tacks them to his own answers to
advertisements.
H.S. : About good hair-
crops. The Israelites (sect) al-
ways wash and comb their hair,
which grows to nearly 18in. Not
a single hair is cut off face or
head, yet not one of them is
bald. The fact that they are
vegetarians may have something
to do with it.
* * *
The crew of the wrecked
Dayspring who recently landed
at Rockhampton had some blood-
curdling experiences out in the
Pacific. Sharks continually fol-
lowed the boat, and the survi-
vors tell how the ferocious brutes
would swim into the shallow
water of Long Island, and then
allow the waves to roll them
almost on to the beach, in order
to get the remains of turtle
slaughtered and left there by
the stranded sailors.
* * *
Wealthy men in various
parts of the provinces have, it is
said, discharged their ordinary
message and stable-boys, and
applied with success to N.S.W.
boarding-out authorities for waif-
boys instead, and thus not only
escape paying wages but gather
in booty at the rate of ss. a
week per boy under the Chari-
ties Act.
* * *
The first regular mail com-
munication between Hobart and
Launceston, Tas., was estab-
lished in 1816. One Robert A.
Taylor, a Government messen-
ger, performed the journey per
medium of Shanks’s pony, leav-
ing each settlement on alternate
Sundays. The railway carries
the mail now a little quicker,
but not much. The mail train
takes nearly 12 hours to accom-
plish the one hundred and
twenty-odd miles. A midnight
stoppage of two hours at a for-
lorn little siding is made, pre-
sumably to allow the engine to
get its second wind, while the
passengers listen to the seduc-
tive music of the ’possums that
inhabit the forest thereabouts.

The official excuse for
militia decline in Vic. is that
the uniforms are too sombre.
Scarlet is to be generally adopted so as to attract
recruits!
Man the superior animal ! A
deaf old lady crossing the tramline
at Sydney Railway dropped a parcel
between the rails. As she stooped
to pick it up a “ through ” (Leich-
hardt) tram approached at full speed;
and half-a-dozen males waved their
hands, blasphemed, and shouted,
“She’ll be killed,” while a young wo-
man dropped her umbrella, dashed
past them, and pulled the old lady
off the rails just as the tram thun-
dered by.

X.Z.: “Coffin-sellers grow more
insistently enterprising daily. Re-
cently one big physician was called
in by a swell hotelkeeper to attend a
foreign gentleman, suddenly taken ill.
Death occurred same night, and the
widow, a stranger in a strange land,
sought counsel of the doctor, who,
pitying, mentioned a certain grave-
filler. A few days after, a fine fat
turkey was left at Dr. Galen’s door,
with the compliments of Mr. Dash
Blank, undertaker, &c., 0001 Asterisk-
street, Querybourne. Evidently an
illustration of the aphorism that grati-
tude is a lively sense of favors to
come. Now the other side. A poor
governess died in a school, and, as
soon as the breath left her, the doctor
in attendance hauled from his pocket
a handful of the cards of a certain
undertaker, and pressed one on the
head-teacher. The undertaker was
employed, but the doctor will never
see the inside of that school again.”
* * *
Tasmania, but a century old,
and with but about 4,000,000 acres of
land alienated, can boast more ruins
than any other province of the group.
Cheap labor—a man cost the settler
but from £1 to £9 per annum —en-
couraged the erection of palatial
mansions on small estates. When
wheat fell from pounds to shillings per
bushel, and prices generally came
down by the run, the up-keep of the mansions
was no longer possible. Hence the hundreds of
crumbling ruins, where the swallows build in safety
and the mopokes hoot at night.

Cheerful items from the report of the Vic.
Presbyterian General Assembly committee on the
state of religion :
The masses were as worldly and pleasure-loving as ever,
and the rush to all kinds of amusement knew no abate-
ment. It was lamentable to know that a very large
number of young men had no vital connection with the
church of their fathers. The following hindrances were
referred to :—Sunday trains and steamboats, a disposition
among young men on the Sabbath to go cycling, and read-
ing works of fiction on the Lord’s Day. The Minister and
Commissioner of Railways had been communicated with,
protesting against the running of Sundaj' excursion trains
to Healesvillc, Ferntree Gully, and Bacchus Marsh. With
a view to united action on questions affecting the moral
and religious welfare of society the following subjects were
brought forward :—Art unions, Sunday trading, religious
instruction in State schools, the employment of barmaids,
the Divorce Act, and the duty of endeavoring to stop such
theatrical performances as have an unchaste and immoral
tendency. Decided : That influence be used at the next
general election to secure the return of members of Par-
liament pledged to support the reading of Scripture-
lessons in State-schools, and that a list of queries be sent
to congregations.
A list of queries ! That ought to settle the matter.
One query : “ Ha’ ye forgoten the collaikshun ? ’
Another : “Is your dochter teachin’ theology to a
Chinaman and lairnin’ to ride a bicycle? ”

The Nemesis of the cheap nurse. A Syd-
ney woman advertised for a young girl to mind a
child of two years in return for a good home and
2s. 6d. per week. A well-dressed matron brought
a young girl in response. The matron ran a small
shop and gave her address; the girl had never
been in service before, and, seeming a quiet
youngster, was engaged. Five weeks later it
SO-LONG, SAPPHO!
Moorabinda sighs in sadness,
Naught beguiling;
Faces fair forget their gladness,
Lips, their smiling;
Trembling drops on eyelids quiver
For the friend across the River,
Gifts returned unto the Giver —
Good-bye, Sappho !
From the Gulf, where hot sand shimmers,
To the Bight,
East and West, wherever glimmers
Austral light,
Wave-washed Bluff and Thargomindah,
Bischoff, Barrier, Bolinda,
Tribute pay with Moorabinda—
So-long, Sappho !
When she meets the boys out yonder
Over Jordan,
Clarke and Kendall, “ Vag.”—we wonder—
Boake and Gordon,
Australanders all, should greet her,
Whisper “ Press ” to Father Peter—
Pass her in and safely seat her—
Welcome, Sappho!
Tasmania.
JOHN DRAYMAN.
“DAY AND NIGHT."
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By light of day
We sit the length of feet
And yards apart.
He looks this way !
A chance for eyes to meet,
With beating heart.
But by the moon
Not measured yards or feet
We think are best
Between, and soon
It is our lips that meet
At Love’s behest.
LULIO

At a Melb. Presbytery meeting the other
day a shepherd reproached pious James Balfour
with having made the “ somewhat cruel and cold-
blooded remark ” that ministers were not sutler mg'
any more depression than other people who lost
money when the boom burst. the speakei
pointed out that it was better to be an ex-director
of the Freehold Investment Co. in “straightened
circumstances” than an ordinary parson at the
best of times. Balfour’s sentiments, however,
were subsequently whitewashed.
*-* * *
The most gorgeous insolvent this country
lias ever seen appeared the other day in Sydney
Bankruptcy Court to state that his liabilities were
various and assets nil. His evidence (as reported
in Sydney D.T.) reads like a hunk from a W.A.
mine prospectus :
He came to N.S.W. about 15 months ago, in the Ormuz,
travelling second-class. He brought out with him an
amount of £2OOO in Bank of England notes, which lie
carried in a licit. His father was Henry Lambert, mem-
ber of Parliament 25 years ago for the county of Wexford,
as well as deputy-lieutenant of the county, ami a justice
of the peace, His father’s income came from his own
private estates, which were principally in Ireland. Bank-
rupt was educated by a private tutor, and then went to
the London University, lie was in two railway accidents,
and was laid up for 18 months. He took over some pro-
perties worth about £400,000, when he was 21 years of
age, to avoid the legacy duties, and had since been paying
off the mortgage upon them. He had made a good deal
of money in arranging loans. He made £40,000 in one
transaction. He raised a loan of £700,000 for a MI
Salamons, from Martin’s Bank, in Lombard-street, through
his friend Charlie Morrison, who was wort h £27,000,000.
Bankrupt got the £40,000 in cash, and with it bought
tlie equity of redemption of land at Woodford. He, how-
ever, had £7OOO returned to him for giving up a little
ditch. After this, he lost £30,000 in a mine in California.
He had been staying at the Hotel Metropole whilst m
Sydney, hut when his address became known lie had such
a lot of people calling on him with gold specimens, and
claiming relationship with him, that he left, and went to
live quietly at a coffee-palace. One man, who said he got
remittances from home, tint whose remittance had been
delayed, obtained £37 from him, and bankrupt never saw
him again. Bankrupt had nothing now but his clothes.
He went to Tasmania last November, and inspected every
mine in the colony.
This vast person who has been in the habit, with-
out an effort, of knowing people worth ,£27,000,000
and calling them “ Charlie,” evidently broke down
as the natural result of coming to these “beastly
kawlinies. ”
* * » *
The S.A. Colored Immigration Bill pro-
viso exempting Tasmanian and Maori aboriginals
from the operation of the Act, recalls that the
last Tas. male aboriginal was decapitated after
death. His skull reposes safely in England, but
the hospital sawbones who actually secured the
trophy is not yet “officially ” discovered. He was
a careless, dare-devil Goth was that sawbones.
Fined for driving through a country township
after sunset without lights, he ostentatiously ad-
vertised himself by galloping up and down the
main street nearly all day with three big lighted
coach-lamps hung on long poles, strapped to the
footboard of his buggy
Unless the degenerate half - castes on
Flinders’ Island may be considered representative
of a dead-and-gone race, there will be no response
to S. A.’s kindness in leaving Tas. and Maori
natives out of the Colored Immigration bar.
Meanwhile, the skulls of King Billy and Truganina
are understood to be facetiously grinning their
acknowledge!!.- ,'" to Premier Kingston.

* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The curse of the
“ guinea-a-set ” man is strong in the
land, while all over Australia many
eminent and accomplished molar-mer-
chants are practically starving behind
their brass-plates. One Southern dental
assassin now cabs it from door to door
and books orders. It is this fiend’s
custom to extract everythin!i. .Slightly-
decayed teeth are ruthlessly dislodged
to make way for “ our lovely guinea
seats, Madam,” and he smilingly col-
lects a deposit and passes on to the
victim next door. Nearly every
chemist puts out his little show-case,
and, he, too, under the proposed N.S.W.
Dental Act, will claim recosrnition at
the hands of the Dental Board because
he once had occasion to drop the iodo-
form spatula to extract a temporary
canine for Smith’s nurse-girl. If the
framers of the Act now before Parlia-
ment had an inch of backbone they
would nail their colors to the mast of
a (Bill which would compel every non-
diplomued man in N.S.W. to undergo
an exam, before being allowed to prac-
tice. Then, should the Bill be rejected,
the province’s maxillae may go to Sheol,
and it will be at liberty to call in the
local blacksmith to treat the next case.
The writer holds a D.D.S. diploma, but
the tidal - wave of dental quackery
knocked him under, and lie’s now scrub-
cutting on the Bogan. —J.S.S.

H. ; “ The regrettable loss of
Melbourne undertaker Sleight gives me
an opportunity of dilating on the ad-
vantage of the Paris kind of Gothenburg
undertakery system, called the ‘ Pompes
Funebres.’ The commune, or munici-
pality, farms a monopoly of all the fune-
rals, and the farming company makes
40 per cent, profit, besides giving poor
people a decent funeral for nothing.”
* * *
The Boggabri (N.S.W.) Indian-
hawker murder recalls an E. Coast,
Tas., incident. A mere stripling shot
an Assyrian, concealed the body, and
plundered the effects. He burnt most
of these, keeping some pipes and
“ flash ” jewellery, which he presented to
male and female friends. Detection soon
followed, but the death-sentence was
commuted to “ life,” and, at the present
time, the shooter languishes in Hobart
Gaol. This affair scared the hawkers
for a time—but that phase did not last
long, as they recognised and traded on
the protection of the State.
* * *
Victoria proceeds to abolish that
old British law whereby a magistrate can
order that the body of a suicide shall be
hustled secretly into the ground at night
without any religious services. The Bill
provides that the suicide shall have
“Christian burial.” And it was Attor-
ney-General Isaacs who drafted it. A
Jew in search of Christian burial seems
a bit incongruous.

“ A Mason’s Wife ” sends to The Bulle-
tin a letter clipped from Masonry of 28th May-
last, and reckons that should it meet the eye of
many Masons Bro. J. 0. Roy craft will have to
build a hotel as big as the Australia :
THANKS TO TASMANIAN BRETHREN.
(To the Editor of Mason it, yJ
Dear Sir and Brother,— Having occasion to visit Laun-
ceston the week before last on business, and finding my-
self a stranger in a strange land, I had the opportunity of
feeling what true Masonry is. On meeting a brother
named J. C. Roycraft, J.P., the Resident Secretary of the
Australian Widows’ Fund, he not only assisted me in my
business, but very kindly placed his steam launch and
horse and buggy at my disposal, and also invited me to
his house, where his wife and daughter treated me right
royally and made me feel as if we had known each other
for years, and I can assure you I was sorry when my time
came to leave ; it was the first occasion that we had met,
and my sincere wish is that I shall again have the plea-
sure of seeing them. I also received a very cordial recep-
tion from Bro. Gilmore, the M.P. for Beaconsfield, and
Bro. Colgrave, the proprietor of the Ophir Hotel of that
place. I had no time to visit any of the lodges, but from
what I hear they are more particular who they admit into
the Order than we are in Victoria. The Masonic Hall is a
very substantial building and situated in a very nice part
of Launceston. Taking the residents as a body, they are
extremely slow, all waiting for something to turn up. If
any brethren are visiting the place, I shall be only too
pleased to give them an introduction to Bro. Roycraft,
who is one of those Masons you do not meet with every
day. I had the. pleasure of seeing your paper there.
Yours fraternally, Alex. Phillips. 129 Swanston-street,
14th May, 1896.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Nov. 24, 1896.
Mr Dear Myee—
The new Victoria (Sydney Ladies’) Club was
opened last Thursday under moist circumstances.
A damp and dripping crowd struggled out of rain-
cloaks and clashed umbrellas wildly in the passage
to the detriment of the hitherto untrodden pro-
prietary carpets, shook hands with hostesses on
the staircase and inspected the premises, or
listened to music in the drawing-room. At the
first assembling of a club the ice cannot be ex-
pected to be broken, and the guests wandered
about in a sorrowful way, or backed nervously in
among the crowd, wondering at the musical feats
of a certain infant. Some chatterers on the out-
skirts of a listening group made a foreign recitress
look so angrily at them that I am sure she was
thinking things about them in Trench. I feel
tired already of all the convulsions there are going
to be, because some members will talk when others
are performing, and then nobody will ever sing or
recite again if anybody deliberately tries to drown
her voice. And everybody will walk out of the
room when anybody begins to warble, and notices
to be silent will be posted up and totally ignored.
And several other things.
Social par-writers, you may observe, trium-
phantly note that there were Men at Thursday’s
gathering. There were also roses, refreshments,
and many of the Nicest, including two Misses
-Brand, bringing a mild and self-repressive family-
manner with them. Dressing was variegated, on
account of the weather. Some of the gay muslin-
clad women looked like draggled birds of paradise,
others in blouse and skirt flattened their noses
against the side window-panes, and gazed wist-
fully through the teeming rain on the Phillip-street
tennis-court, which was to have come into their
plan had the day been fine. A chatty visitor
brought up the tone of the whole party by airing
his overwhelming acquaintance with the British
nobility ; the air absolutely scintillated with glit-
tering fragments of “ Burke’s Peerage.”
A general dampness prevailed everywhere last
W f e .r W hen the weather took up, the first night
of the Ironmaster,” at the Lyceum, reduced a
sympathising audience to sniffs and tears, and a
weeping d.c. enthusiastically waved its moist
handkerchief at the performers after the curtain
fell and rose again. Mrs. Potter, as Claire de
Beaulieu, has a part necessitating her sitting a
good deal on a couch in several different costumes.
All her gowns match the yellow plush of the sofa,
and are very fetching. Her best blue silk frock is
quite visible above the shoulders, both back and
ront, and keeps on ” without that firm whale-
bone skeleton which some inquiring minds found
to be the support of the inscrutably abbreviated
black satin gown of Francillon. Daring touches
of absinthe-green chiffon on the blue dress—the
only cerulean tint in the play, by the way, for the
Ironmaster is quite mild —and beetle-wing em-
broidery with velvet bows make an artistic
'V'd ? fc sta £ e women upholster themselves ;
fU rC', P - dresses her part. Linda Raymond, as
the Duchess, looks rather like an animated draw-
ing-room suite in her toniest frock, but gets exactly
the right effect of not being exactly chic in her
bunday frock of purply-striped silk fashionably
confected with etceteras.
The wedding-scene set was applauded on the
first night. I here are tones of scarlet in a dark-
green room with yellowish-brown furniture : also
the whole act is a lesson in French wedding eti-
quette. ihe men are all in full dress. French
bridegrooms always look like waiters, and wear
their swallow-tails all the festal day. Brides don’t
believe m giving their white satin only an hour’s
existence, and they, too, wear their finery all day.
So that is why Claire stands pale and haughty,
like a wax figure in a hair-dresser’s window, in
her pretty bridal white, while Kyrle Bellew, as
the wronged bridegroom, denounces her. If she
only had to run off and get into her travelling
dress it would save a domestic wrangle. It is
unusual for Kyrle to do the storming while Mrs.
P. stands quietly by ; in all their other plays if
there s any storming to be done Mrs. Potter is
right there, and the gentleman gets a down
worried expression which seems to appeal to every
married man in the house. By the way, the red-
plush footman has been convincingly top-loftv
through two plays now, and I throw in a note of
admiration for his haughty demeanour in carrying

The swagger luncheon on board the Yamashiro
Maru last Friday, has set people praising the
supremacy of Japanese cooking. But the Jap at
home has only the most primitive kitchen arrange-
ments. He is quick and adaptive and soon learns
to fix up European dishes for tourists. He himself
is a dainty feeder and eats hardly any meat. He
likes fish, fresh-caught and raw, straw-colored tea.
blends sweets with salmon and drinks safe - , a spirit
which makes him giddily drunk for a quarter of
an hour and then dies out of him. He is never
dull or heavy through over-gorging. A Chinese
P°P, prepare a more elaborate meal than any-
body, but the eater has to swallow it in faith with-
Vin S^ eCtl ”?.u he ki ,* chen > or hi s appetite would
be killed. Chows can eat anything. In the
Canton market rats are a staple, fattened on the
execution-ground, where they swarm over the
skulls and bones of the victims of the law.
Resuscitated “ Chinatown ’’makes Her Maiestv’s
festive once more. On Monday there were some
ingredients of a brilliant d.c. A beauty-bride in
blushful rose-pink with a wreath of roses on her
hair and a little curl in the middle of her forehead
was stared at as much as the stage-folk, and the
husband bore it as became the bridegroom of a
sensation wedding. Bessie Clayton’s fire-dance is
°.. y novelty—a large black void streaked by a
whirling figure kicked about by two muscular
lavender legs and fluttering voluminous garments
in different-colored limelights—like a whole fleet
of ships flying all their signal flags at once in dirty
weather. It is called a “ fire-dance ” because
there is no illusion of flames, and Bessie doesn’t
dance—she only contorts. The “Firm” is em-
phasising the revival by issuing artistic souvenir
programmes.

Mrs. Coke, the American woman-lawyer, is a
bright, active woman who speaks very pronounced
Amurnkan.” Visiting a friend’s house, she
heard our “ locusts ” for the first time in the
garden, and was so much taken with their ear-
piercmg shriek that she had two caught for her.
Going she suddenly exclaimed, “Sakes alive, I
clean forgot those bugs.” So the “bugs” were
put in a paper bag and carried off.
The Sydney Anglicans are much exercised in
mind over the special recognition of Presbyterian-
ism by Gov. Hampden. Shortly after the cable
informed us that Queen Victoria and the Czar
knelt together in some Presbyterian Church —as a
sort of mutual concession to Anglican suscepti-
bilities and Greek exclusiveness—H.E. and suite
attended worship at St. Stephen’s Presbyterian
Giiurch, Phulip-street. It was only the other day
that the Governor advocated the erection of new
seats in St. James’ Church. It is rumored that
the Koman Catholics are trying to get him to hear
Mass at St. Mary’s Cathedral.

A picture chess-board has been designed and
executed by a Sydney lady artist. The squares
are landscapes m oil, alternated with silver-grey
ones. Ihe tiny scenes are mostly on “the
harbor, and are charmingly painted. As the
board is framed and glazed, it is practicable, and
is calculated to give players a sensation of travel-
fing at breakneck-speed over miles of country as
they lift their pawns across the little brooklets
and over the tree-tops and hills.

Mothers of attractive headstrong daughters
are sometimes tempted to envy other people their
ugly docile ducklings. That too-managing Melb.
matron is still remembered who, some years agone
kept each of her dashing daughters engaged six-
deep so as to guard against any possible defection.
In the end, one of them achieved by far the most
brilliant match ever made by an Australian-born,
while the other, even before her widowhood, had
to earn her own bread and butter.
Melb. milliners assert that the expenditure on
hats and bonnets this season almost touched boom-
year s record. Head-pieces were never more ex-
pensive, by reason, amongst other things, of the
vast quantities of trimmings that even the most
modest chapeau requires now-a-days ; the demand
lor high-priced millinery surprised and delighted
those in the trade. Women, who a year or two
ago would have ‘ done ’ the lawn with a paltry
guinea s worth of flowers and feathers on their
heads cheerfully gave five or six and even eight
sovs for head-gear this season. New arrivals
cruelly declare that the bonnet-show at Fleming-
ton would be considered distinctly vulgar by smart
Londoners or chic Parisians, and that the gaudy
style of hat-architecture has disappeared in Paris
and passed to ‘ ’Arriet ’ in ‘the little village.’
Certainly those confections sent out by the best
English and Continental houses made an emphatic
appeal for less grotesque shape and more subdued
tints ; indeed, a most expensive bonnet, imported
straight from Paris, was an innocent-looking little
affair composed of white straw with a couple of
deep-pink roses and a heron’s top-knot in it.
The Austral Salon meeting on Thursday even-
ing last would serve as an excellent model of an
evenings entertainment for the Svdney club
which seems to be following in the" wake of the
A.S. so slavishly As even the faults and imper-
fections of the Melb. club are being reproduced in
the Sydney one, it would be worth the new club's
while to take a note when the original does a
thing unusually well. The evening was filled with
music, recitation, and high-class refreshments and
rounded off with a reading from Ibsen’s ‘Lady
Ingher of Ostrat. ’ The ‘ Carl of Norraway ’ has
been having a day out at the Salon lately. Some
short time ago the members were ‘wrastling’ with
the obscurities in the ‘ Master Builder ’ and up-
holding Hilda Wangel as the type of modern
girlhood. Countless dreary meetings on this
alleged intellectual gathering-ground have severely
scared mere males away from women’s literary
societies of every sort, and it is only pleasant and
entertaining gatherings like last Thursday’s which
will lure them back.

r. Hubert Parry’s ‘Judith,’presented for the
first time m Australia at Melb. Town Hall by the
local Philharmonic Society, is undoubtedly a
strong oratorio of the second class, with plenty of
room for dramatic effects in its solos, and a good
deal of martial movement in its choruses; also a
fair amount of barbaric noise in the incidental
music. Hie subject is steeped in battle, murder
massacres, and pillaging*. As far as two choruses
in black and white could manage it they gave an
e ective representation of these horrors. Madame
Limly Spada made a telling enough Judith
vocally, to subjugate the most unrelenting Holo-
fernes. Miss Odell Fawcett, when once she had
got on good terms with the orchestra, let her
dramatic contralto have its own way ; but she
needs to speak her words more distinctly. Philin
Newbury had plenty of scope in his solos, and the
last one brought the house fairly down with a
ravishing top note. The audience was large and
cordial, and would have been bigger still but for
tbe attractions of the fire raging in Little Flinders-
street Many women even in quasi evening-dress
turned their semi-bare backs upon the oratorio at
the last moment and begged their escorts to take
them off to view the flames.

, A curious will is that of a recently-deceased
Melb. spinster, who left £BOO of her hard-earned
tC \ th<! for the protection of animals,
300 to a friend, and <£s per annum to a relation
who had given her a home for 25 years. Cats and
dogs were her hobby. Some people thought she
owed her mortal disease to her constant associa-
tion with them ; but, at all events, she was always
trotting up lanes and diving under bridges with
parcels of food for homeless curs, or carrying
diseased mongrels to the nearest chemist for
treatment or merciful release. One of her pecu-
liarities was to induce cabmen and such like to
adopt stray animals, and then visit them weekly
and scold them for fancied defects in their treat-
ment of the waifs.

Premier Reid (says a Bulletin corre-
spondent) was successfully initiated into the
preliminary mysteries of Freemasonry at Lodge
Centennial (Sydney) the other night. Brother
rteid says it was not nearly so terrible as he
had feared. Boaz is nothing; wait till Brother
Keid knows all about Tubal-Cain. The lod-e has
specially enlarged Hiram Abiff’s grave for him
and every Mason in Sydney has got the date of
his third degree m his note-book and has regis-
tered a solemn vow to be there to see the fun.
Newly - appointed S.A. Police Commis-
sioner Madley had a peculiar experience at Melb
Cup meeting. He went over with Howe,
M.L., and shared a hotel dressing-room with a
Queensland squatter. Madley saw a purse on the
tabJe which was a fac simile of his own and put it
in his pocket. “ Give me that purse, ,f said the
squatter, “or I’ll get the police ! ” Madley saw
ins mistake and returned the purse with profuse
apologies, but it took some time to satisfy the
squatter that he had not fallen among thieves.
Time deals gently with the woman who
“Babeskin” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. Thos. S. Loney, 208 William-st., Sydney.*
James Patrick Gar van, manager of the
Citizens’ Life Assurance Co., of Sydney, and an
ex-Mimster of Justice of N.S. Wales, died last
week, aged 53, to the great regret of a wide circle,
lie was born in Ireland, arrived in N.S.W. when
but four years old, and after a bright school career
addressed himself to the study of law—which he
abandoned, when Hill End rush broke out, to
manage mining companies. For many years he
represented Eden in N.S.W. Assembly in con-
junction with Mr. Henry Clarke, and while in
Parliament successfully made a speciality of
financial criticism. He was a man of fine
phymque and the steadiest of habits, an accom-
plished oarsman and good all-round athlete, but
died within a month after it was definitely dis-
covered that he was afflicted with internal cancer.
Mr. Gar van leaves 12 children.

Cole’s Book Areade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic: 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ;5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ;9,
Artists Materials; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland •
13, a Smiling Gallery: 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery : 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department; containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*

journalism in Australia, when in 1863 he reduced
Melbourne Herald to a penny.

The record for an Australian capital city.

Coroner Toul’s father, the Rev. John
Yaul, first chaplain stationed in Northern Tas-
mania (George Town, 1819) was a man of many
parts. In 1809 he was sent to convert the raging
Heathen in the South Seas. Presumably, the
natives couldn’t stand his jokes, for they shut him
up to fatten for a cannibal feast. But feed him
as they would, the Tahitians could not induce the
lean parson to put on flesh; so they gave it up in
despair, promising the Rev. John his liberty on
condition that he shaved 20 of them without
drawing blood. He accomplished the task, and
lived to tell the tale—with clerical embellishments.

to-day (Thursday, Nov. 26).

Judge A’Beckett, of Victoria, has an un-
conquerable antipathy to apples in any shape, and
the smell of them is sufficient to make him ill. He
has been seen to hurriedly leave a tramcar in order
to avoid a grimy small boy who was gnawing a
pippin.
Barristers must be 10 years at the Bar to
be eligible for the title of Q.C. N.S.W. Justice
G. B. Simpson, who urges barristers to take the
title as early as possible after qualification, did
not himself take it till his twenty-eighth year
of practice.

whanged at the Bathurst Convention, and whom
the chubby Kidd designated “a disgraceful

weeping parents of many who now defame him.

in Melbourne University as the only “ professor ”
who has no university degree and never had any
university training. Hence, probably, his marked
success as a teacher. Mining-lecturer Ferdinand
Moritz Krus6 is likewise the only “lecturer”
totally devoid of a university degree, his only addi-
tions being F.L.S., F.G.S. (Fellow of the Linnsean
Society and of the Geological Society, both of
London).

About the smartest pickpocket in Aus-
tralia was lately released from S.A. Labor prison.
The clergy will remember him as the tradesman
who went through nearly the whole bevy of clerics
who assembled to welcome Talmage at Spencer-
street (Melb.) station a few years ago.

the principal of one of Sydney’s largest softgoods
establishments. The original softgoods David
Jones, of Sydney, was raised to the giddy emi-
nence, but tired of the vanity in a few years.

Agent-General Playford (S.A.) cannot be
accused of “ big head.” “Thos. Playford, market-
gardener, Morton’s Summit,” is the legend on
spring-drays frequently seen on Adelaide Block.
Playford’s renown as a vegetable-man earned him
the nickname of “Tomato Tom” when in S.A.
Assembly.
An Irish Judge relates that in his court on
one occasion lately, when the crier called out as
usual, “ Gentlemen of the jury, take your proper
places in the court,” seven of them instinctively
walked into the dock. Which is an old N.S.W.
story, told by Dailey and others of a N.S.W. horse-
stealing case.
To this has it come ! Sydney D.T., re-
porting re the Bathurst Convention: “ Mr. D.
O’Connor, M.L.C., filled in the ten minutes that
remained before the adjournment for the garden-
party in praising Bathurst and everybody con-
nected with it, as well as the speakers who had
preceded him.”
Cheok Hong Cheong, boss Chinese Chris-
tian of Australia, used to run a fruit-shop in
Brunswick-street, Pitzroy (Melb.), studying at
Melb. University in his off time. Cheong, it was,
who, as an elder of the kirk, publicly told Vic.
Presbyterian Assembly that it could get better
money-value christianising the heathen of Fitzroy
than wasting its shekels on China Inland Missions.
Dear Bulletin,—l heard Chief Justice
Madden’s charge to the jury in a much-discussed
“ age-of-consent ” case. Why is it that no one
has done justice to this man’s marvellous sweet-
ness of language? Voice, manner, phrase—all the
grace of perfect oratory. Gaunson had delivered
a (Characteristic attack on Parliament for the
“ age-of-consent ” legislation. The Chief Justice
characterised it as “a speech that would be power-
ful everywhere, that would be appropriate any-
where but in a court of justice.” Then he said :
“We have heard that Love laughs at locksmiths;
it is also true that Love laughs at laws. ” Then he
contrasted the boy in the dock with a “seasoned
seducer,” and so went on to the close of an address
that seemed to be all music, in spite of the thou-
sand tawdry details involved in the case.—F.

Paddy Swift, an aboriginal who has earned
good gate-money at Sydney mission meetings for
some time past, was married the other day to a
half-caste lady from Walgett, Miss Emily
Manager—a name which has its implications.
Patrick seems to be more or less in the stable of
professional-philanthropist Ardill, and is almost
as good a platform draw as the defunct Chinese
choir used to be. The glare in his eye, as shown
in the accompanying photo., when he tells how
desperately King Billy of the backblocks cries to
his white sisters to gib it salvation, rarely fails to
corkscrew the shy threepenny bit from its lair in the
garments of the female votaries of the local branch
of the Ebenezer Grand Junction Society for Re-
forming the Old Man, P. Swift, however, who is
now boss of the Warangesda aboriginal mission
station on the Murrumbidgee, is rather a decent
fellow, barring his gift of slushy gab. But when he
pathetically asseverates that all that is necessary
to the success of the aboriginal missions—mostly
peopled by half-castes—is to keep the promiscuous
white male 10 miles from the boundary, the chair-
man generally squashes him with an announce-
ment of the 107th hymn. His bride, who is 26
(Paddy is 48), is less known to charitable fame,
but the male-reporter of the wedding says she wrs
“ rather superior-looking ” in a “ costume of some
light material 99 which he couldn’t specify, ‘ 6 with a
large pink pattern on a white ground,” and white
flowers in her hair. And The Bulletin honestly
wishes her good luck, and metaphorically scatters
several large handfuls of rice ove*r her portrait
herewith.
The Christmas Number of The Bulletin
will be published on December 12. It will be better
illustrated than any other paper ever published
in Australasia, and will contain a vast amount of
original matter of universal interest by Aus-
tralian writers old and new. Also, it will no*
contain a single second-hand, stolen or cheap-
German block. In fact, it will not contain one
line, either of reading or illustration, not specially
done for the paper. Single copies, post free, to
any Australasian address, 7d. ; to any foreign
address, Bd.
