POLITICAL
POINTS
The N.S.W. Opposition is quite tired of
W. Nicholas Willis, Esq., and thinks of asking
him to “ git ” from the Opposition rooms.
Griffith is much nettled over the Q. Leg.
Council’s shelving of the Federation Bill. Had
it passed he was a certainty as a representative.
He dreaded the Referendum.
“ Popocrat ” is Yankee for a blend of
Populist and Democrat. It is now sought to
name the dominant party in N.S. Wales the
“ Freesock ” Party—from its mixture of Freetrade
and Socialism.
“I will never raise the land-tax higher
than Id. in the £5l, ” said the valiant Premier
Reid several times, in the first flush of his land-
tax. Wonder what he thinks about it now, while
the assessment ructions are going ?
See how the potentates trooped after each
other to the Bathurst Convention —by one, by two,
by three, by the dozen ! The alleged big men
were just as greedy for advt. as the unknowns. In
a month, the whole affair will be a Soudan Donkey.
Professor Gosman, at the Bathurst Con-
vention : “ Australian senators should be men who
have done service in the field or made the name of
Britain great.” Very well, then. British M’sP.
should be men who’ve made Australia great. The
bullfroggery of it !
Sydney Telegraph put its foot in it very
badly a few days back. A muddled cablegram
was read to mean that the Canadian Parliament
had suddenly seen the evils of Protection, and
had abolished the protective duties and gone in
for Free-raid at one jump. The D.T., in its eager-
ness for signs of the glorious spread of Shoddy,
swallowed it all, and roared in a leading article
about the progress of enlightenment and the up-
heaval of the whatsisname and the dawning of the
great thingamy, and so on. Next day a very
obscure par stated that it now appeared the
Canadian Parliament had prorogued six weeks
ago, and wouldn’t meet again for six months,
therefore these beautiful things couldn’t have hap-
pened, but they would probably happen by-and-
bye, and then, &c., &e.
Caldwell, M.P.(S.A.),
in opposing the colored Im-
migration Restriction Bill
on the score of “ human-
ity,” said he looked on the
yellow man as his brother.
“Ah,” said Kingston, “ you
would not like him for %
brother-in-law. ”
A cry from Melbourne
Beacon :
It is only the wall of tariffs
between the English-speaking
nations that prevents them all
joining in one mighty union.
The kingdoms ol the whole
earth will become one, the
federation of the world will
take place, and man to man
the world o’er shall brothers
be, as the last tariff of man
against man disappears. The
day that men learn that the in-
terests of all men are identical
and intertwined, and that it is
safe to trust in the law' of uni-
versal brotherhood, that day
will be signalised by the ob-
literation of the term “Protec-
tion ” from the vocabulary of
nations.
This explains it all, of
course. It is because there
is no tariff between them
that the English Tory and
the Irish Nationalist are
like brothers, and the fact
that there is absolute Free-
trade within the IT. S. which
made the Confederate and
the Federal love each other
so much that 656,000 of
them were killed, and the
mutual admiration of the
Russian and Pole and the
Turk and Armenian both
arise from the absence of
commercial barriers be-
tween them. Blessed is he
who knows all about every-
thing, and whose one single
fad oan put the whole world
to rights. Amen.
Millen, M.P. for Bourke, N.S.W.: “If
the electors knew what went on in Parliament in
the case of the election of a member to the Public
Works Committee, they would never trust Par-
liaments to elect the members of an Australian
Senate.”
So, according to president Machattie, the
Australian Commonwealth is to “ know only one
watchword—and that the progress of the greatest
nation of the earth—the British Empire.” No,
siree. Show that sentiment to the door, Jeames.
And the B.E. is not “the greatest nation of the
earth.” Why, it isn’t even “a nation.”
The unhappy N.S.W. Protectionist-party !
Here you have ex-leader G. Republican Dibbs
proposing success to the new Japanese line of
steamers, denouncing Jap-exclusion legislation,
and declaring that if any vote of his could ever be
the means of obtaining the little' brown man a
welcome to Australia it would enthusiastically be
given !
Another batch of two dozen Chows has
just landed in Tasmania. Six of them had
naturalisation papers, which were duly examined
and declared correct by the boss Chow consignee.
The health officer insisted on vaccinating ten of
the new arrivals. Eighteen paid a £lO poll-tax.
Repelling an invasion with a doctor’s lancet and a
£lO tariff !
Whoop for the kink of that “People’s
Federal Convention ” that wanted it to express its
belief in a Supreme Being ! Corollary : Why not
also in Satan ? In sin ? In dirt ? In colic ?
See the simple vulgarity of its mathematics. If
there is a Last, there must be a twentieth, a nine-
teenth . . . a third, a second, a First! Every
fool knows that much. Let us here affirm our
belief in the flatness of the earth—that is, of its
people.
Trenwith, back in Melb., has an infinity of
matter bottled up, as cannot fail to be the case with
such a keen observer and digger-down. In par-
ticular, the blackness of English poverty, under
Freetrade, is his theme, infinitely surpassing any
conceptions he had formed about it. He says the
Democracy is a seething mass, led by so many con-
tradictory systems that united action seems im-
possible. This hampered him a good deal in public
speaking, and often prevented him speaking at all.
N.S.W. Parliament has closed up for six
months or so, and the Government dodged the
Federation question to the last. Kit is proposed
to wait for Queensland then there will be no pro-
gress for probably another year, possibly longer.
And as the Reid Ministry has flatly refused to say
whether it intends to wait for Queensland, or
when it will go on, or whether it has any definite
intention of going on under any circumstances
whatever, the problem is left in a most hazy state.
First three lines of a recent Sydney Tele-
graph article: “The recent heavy imports of
breadstutfs, combined with the high prices ruling
for wheat and flour under the reign of Freetrade,”
&c. The high prices of wheat and flour under
Freetrade seem to be advanced as a distinct cause
of congratulation. What about the Cheap Loaf
that was promised ? Also, the cheapness that was
to prevail generally, and the load that was to be
“removed from the back of the consumer,” and
all the rest ?
SOUND FINANCE.
MR. ROSENBLOOM (to his little son Ikey) : “ Ven counting money paid
over to you, mein son, nefer pick up de last note — it’s just
bossible dere may be another underneath.”
The case of ex-policeman Green was once
more dragged into Vie. Leg. Assembly last week.
Green has some ancient grievance based upon the
belief that the State is a miloh-cow for public
servants, and in his time he has petitioned far and
wide. Even V.R. didn’t escape his plaintive
wail. Chief-Secretary Peacock seems to have
extinguished the last spark of bogus sympathy for
the policeman by stating that he has drawn a
pension of =8222 from the Government for the last
eleven years. A lot of people would like to be
martyrs on the same terms.
For once the slumberous jealousy existing
between the North and South of Tasmania has
been productive of good. The Great Western
Railway grab now looks like getting through both
Houses, with modifications on the. basis of a 25
years’ lease with right of renewal and a royalty on
minerals raised. The North v. South banner was
hardly big enough to openly fight under, so the
Northern members—who wouldn’t particularly
object to their end of the island being gridironed
—battled back and edge against parting with the
fee-simple of the land reached for by the syndi-
cate. The result is the modified grab aforemen-
tioned.
Significant that the Tory Sydney D.T.
should thus editorialise : “There are interests to
be considered in the northern province (Q.) that
are incompatible with a federation that does not
make liberal concessions. ” Of course there are—
Kanakas. But Federal Australia (the Republic)
can make no concessions of any sort to a slave-
trade and the color question. No, not even at the
cost of ultimate civil war.
“BE SEATED, MADAM!"
Holder village settlement, named after the
S. A. Treasurer, is the first of the villages estab-
lished by the late Lands Minister Gillen to pay
its way. This year, owing to the high price of
cereals, the settlers have paid off all Govt, liabili-
ties and are left with a good balance: a severe
knock for the Ass papers, which prophesied dire
failure, but reckoned without Gillen, who stuck to
the villages through all their initiatory troubles,
and, unfortunately, died just when they began to
turn the corner.
Among N.S.W. Assembly Bills not pro-
ceeded with by the Council in the Session just
concluded are —
Law Practitioners.
Supreme Court Procedure Amendment.
Legal Profession Amalgamation.
Judges Relatives Disqualification.
There are so many lawyers and lawyers’ relatives
in the House of Kidney Fits that it seems hope-
less to expect reform pf Law or Lawyers until the
Council is either reformed or abolished.
“It is easy for Premier Kingston to be
brave on the law-reform path,’’ said a Q. visitor to
Sydney, the other day. “He has a naturally
sympathetic House to talk to on such topics, for
there are but three legal men in his Assembly
altogether. But, take us in Queensland. Why,
five out of our eight Cabinet Ministers are lawyers.
And take N.S.W. House. What sort of law-
reform pudding can you make with five lawyers in
the Cabinet, not to mention the fingers of unofficial
sharks ? Until a community stops sending
lawyers into Parliament, it has no right to expect
law-reform in any but homoeopathic doses.”
Which is true.
A leaky argument from the S.M. Herald :
And it is to be borne in mind that direct taxation in the
other provinces is in addition to the exactions of a Pro-
tectionist tariff, not, as in N.S. Wales, in substitution for
them.
Query : What are the exactions of a Protectionist
tariff ? Victoria for last quarter levied .£457,000
m customs duties ; N.S.W., £367,000. There is
a difference, certainly, but still the exactions of
Free-raid seem to keep up pretty close to those of
Protection. Also, if customs and excise are both
reckoned, the figures are .£580,000 and .£530,000,
and Foreign-trade is very near indeed to being as
exacting as the other thing.
For the purposes of Australian trade, the
natural deep-water port of Tasmania is Devon-
port, in the North, where the big potatoes come
from. The common-sense route for a railway
from the silver West Coast would terminate at
Devonport. Hobart, on the Derwent, is too far
south ; Launceston is 40 miles up a river where
vessels of ordinary draught get stuck on the mud-
banks ; Devonport is near the open sea, nearest to
Australian ports, the centre of a fine agricultural
district, and can float vessels of big tonnage with-
out disturbing the cockles. Hobart property-
owners dimly realise these facts, hence the frantic
haste to rush the Clarke-Hordern-Officer syndicate
proposals through Parliament.
The Tory party in Maoriland has almost
dropped out of sight as a factor in the coming
shindy. Many of the leading Tories have discon-
solately thrown up the game, and those that remain
orate in a depressed fashion to small audiences
who come to scoff and remain to bray, and, rightly
or wrongly, the Democrats have begun to leave
them almost wholly out of consideration. The
principal fight, it is now anticipated, will be
between the Government following and the great
Gold Tea party. This is probably the first time in
any country that the Cold Tea faction has loomed
so large that men are forced to consider the possi-
bility of a Gingerbeer Ministry arising within the
next few years, and to think hard over the possible
consequences thereof. The Gingerbeer Ministry
seems to be a good way off yet, but not by any
means so far off as it looked 10 years ago.
About the worst thing which happened to
the Bathurst Convention was the publication in
Sydney Telegraph of portraits of “prominent
members.” That well-meaning paper made them
a hard, murderous-looking lot, except in one or
two cases where it suddenly reduced them to mild,
beaming idiocy trimmed with a vacant stare and
long Dundreary whiskers. The portraits were
enough to wreck any movement.
SydneyTELEGRAPH’s
“ Horrors of Protection”
man grows more and
more mixed up as he
goes along. He asserts,
first, that the country
with the largest marriage-
rate is the most pros-
perous; second, that the
Victorian marriage -rate
has declined because of
Protection; third, he
publishes a table for the
ten years 1883-1892 show-
ing that in that period
the marriage-rate in Vic-
toria declined ‘8 per
1000 and in N.S.W. it
declined 2'04 per 1000, or
nearly three times as
much. Fourthly, he
adds up his table, and
shows that the average
rate for ten years in Vic-
toria is 7'78 marriages
per 1000 inhabitants and
in N.S.W. 7'71 per 1000,
so that the Free-raid
province is actually be-
hind. Then, fifthly, he

N. S. W. A gent-
General Samuel begins to
kick against the globe-
trotting politician—gen-
erally one of the mental
deadbeats of Parliament
—who drifts casually to
London and advertises
himself as a Commis-
sioner coine on some
State errand or other.
Recently Sammy Lees
dropped in and advertised
himself as having a State
mission toadvertise hard-
woods for paving purposes ; Poet Neild—the
Nocent Waterspout person—caine along with-
some poems and stated that he was a Com-
missioner collecting facts about Old Age Pen-
sions ; and Prank Parnell arrived and gave notice
that Australia was going to secede from the H’em-
pire. Saul naturally wants to know why he is
sent to London to attend to N.S.W. business if
Lees and Parnell and the Nocent Waterspout are
to come along in this casual fashion and do it for
him.
Queensland has suddenly announced a
special postage rate on newspapers from outside
the province. The average outside daily paper
will now pay Id. instead of |d., and the average-
sized weekly such as The Bulletin will be
charged Qd. A specially large edition such as
the Xmas number will pay something like 2§d. in
postage. Judging from the date of this new
announcement it is apparently the answer of the
great Boodle Government to sundry unpleasant
things that have been said down South re the
dreadful Q.N. Bank, and the politicians who ran
that putrid institution. If the Government’s
object is not to shut out the papers which insist
on telling the beastly truth about its bank and
its niggers, it should have arranged the new de-
parture for some date when the coincidence
wouldn’t be quite so smellful.
Ex-Mayor Langford, North Melbourne,
President of the Shirk of Romset,
tells you Warners’ Safe Cure will make you well
and strong. Everybody knows Robert Langford,
Esq., and when he tells people everywhere to take
Warners’ Safe Cure for liver trouble, all know
that he means just what he says.
( Melbourne, Sept. 18, 1896.
“ About seven or eight years ago I was suffering
with severe pains in my right side and under the
shoulder-blades ; also flatulency and indigestion.
My urine was very much discolored and contained
a thick sediment. This naturally unfitted me for
my business, and with loss of sleep and no appetite
I thought my time had come. I had some of the
best medicai men attending me, who prescribed
all sorts of medicine without doing me any good,
sol decided to give them up and try Warners’
Safe Cure, which had not only been recommended
me by several friends, who had derived great
benefits from it, but I had read one of their pam-
phlets of cases similar to mine being cured by it,
and testimonials from well-known citizens whom 1
knew. I quickly decided to try it, and con-
sequently sent for three bottles and some of the
pills. When I took the first bottle, I did not feel
a bit better ; in fact, I thought I was worse, but,
as one bottle does not always have the effect, I
persevered with it, and when I had got through
the fourth bottle, the improvement was so marked
that I still kept at it; and when I had taken
about fourteen bottles, I was so well and strong
that I found I had no necessity to take any more,
as I really felt a new man, and fit for business,
with more energy than I had before. I have
much confidence in recommending Warner’s Safe
Cure to anyone who suffers with liver complaint. ”


The Sunlight Year-Book for 1897, which
Alex. Lindsay, bookseller, of Pitt-st., Sydney, sup-
plies bound in cloth for—6d. ! (post-free lOd.) con-
tains probably more solid information than was ever
given for sixpence before. It is like Whitaker’s
Almanac, only more so ; puts all the statistics of
the British Empire at your fingers’ ends, and
supplements them with notes on sport and litera-
ture, celebrated people, household hints, a com-
pendium of games, and similar matter which must
be seen to be appreciated. In fact, the book is a
regular sixpenny bonanza.*
Mr. Arthur Griffith wishes to notify intend-
ing patentees that he has commenced practice as a
Patents and Trade Marks Agent at 39 Castlereagh-
street, Sydney. He has entered into arrange-
ments with leading agents in Europe, America,
Australasia, and South Africa, so that inventors
may rely upon having their interests protected.
Mr. Griffith’s charges will, in all cases, be so
moderate as to place a patent within the reach of
the poorest inventor. For £2O, exclusive of the
cost of plans, Mr. Griffith will obtain Provisional
Protection for any invention in the seven Austra-
lasian colonies and Great Britain.*
G. Robertson and Co., the well-known
booksellers and stationers of George-street, Sydney,
claims to be the house for Xmas-cards, and their
twin Australian series give some graphic ideas of
this federationist continent which have never been
seen on a Xmas-card before.*
The progressive firm of Gowing Bros.,
486, 488 and 490 George-street, Sydney, has re-
cently purchased the whole contents of a large
Sydney clothing warehouse, and now offers this
£30,000 worth of fine stock at a big reduction.
People are advised to go to Gowing Bros, at once
if not sooner, because, although there are suits
enough on hand to clothe thousands of
people, the longer intending customers wait the
fewer there will be to choose from. Gowing Bros,
is an old-established firm which has always stood
high in public credit.*

Brough plays Burnand’s “Mrs. Ponderbury’s Past”
odder the name of plain “Mrs. Ponderbury,” because, he
says, people think it is a problem play with the word
“ Past ”in the title. He erases the Past in order that the
comedy may have as much Future as possible.
Decima Moore is at London Prince of Wales’, in “ The
White Silk Dress,” with Arthur Roberts.
According to recent flies, W. Bloxam, a South African
barber, is nightly shaving customers in the den of lions
in Fillis’s Circus at Johannesburg. He guarantees a clean
shave in 2min. In ’9l a Spanish barber tried this feat,
but fainted ; and an American razorist in ’93 was clawed
and chawed to death by the lions.
Rignold has acted Henry V. over 2000 times, accounting
for nearly seven years of his dramatic life.
A Sydney lady (Mrs. Molyneux) was to have taken a
leading part in a _ drama to have been produced at
Drury Lane at Christmas, when Gus. Harris’s sudden
death broke up the arrangement. She is, however, to be
leading lady in a new play to be produced soon at the
Olympic.
G. H. Snazelle, the fistically-handy monologuist, has
been doing good business at Douglas, Isle of Man, with
“ Music, Song and Story.” Enormous crowds patronized
his Sunday night shows, and the “ late leading baritone
of the Carl Rosa Co.” threatens to introduce Sawbath
Bong and story to London audiences.
Geo. Musgrove is trying to persuade Seymour Hicks,
and Ellaline Terris to trip Australia.
S.F. Argonaut announces authoritatively that the Mel-
bourne season alone of “Trilby” panned out £5OOO
sterling profit to Palmer and Brady.
At the recent Sydnej' Liedertafel smoke ooncert the
unique spectacle was afforded of four tenor soloists dis-
porting their voices at respective intervals of time. Why
such generosity, is best known to the conductor. Owing
to scarcity of funds, “musical Sydney” was not honored
by any appreciable outside assistance. From a musical
point of view, the result was very mediocre. At the
forthcoming Xmas concert Villiers Stanford’s “ The
Revenge” is to he performed, and many ladies will assist.
Haresfoot: “ The piece to be hurried on the Australian
stage is emphatically ‘TheTwo Little Vagabonds,’ beyond
all cooey the London triumph of this year. Many English
hits will not do here, because relying only on brilliancy
of dialogue, and social fads. Even the analysis of the plot
of this can hardly be read without emotion. It quite
knocks out the ‘ Lights o’ London ’ or ‘ Silver King.’ ”
A Dunedin amateur benefit for destitute ex-slave
George Harris, of “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” fame, has just
sent a draft for £99 Us. 6d. across the Pacific to Yankee-
land.
Dampier and Go. are invading the Vic. provincial
towns.
Complimentary concerts seem to be the order of the
day. A band of musicians meet and suggest the advisa-
bility of organising a “benefit” to one of their number.
A unanimous vote is recorded, and straightway the thing
is advertised. The proceeds are disposed of on the “ share-
and-share-alike ” system. Not a bad idea at present, when
so many capable musicians are struggling for a living.
. Harrison White and Colbourne-Baber have settled down
in earnest in Paris, absorbed in study. The soprano is
working hard under Marchesi, the course including the
traditional rendering of Italian arias, French music, ora-
torio, and, above all, Mozart music, for which Marchesi is
said to have declared Baber to have just the right class of
voice. Harrison White is studying the organ with Guil-
mant, and sits by his side at La Trinity, where the great
Frenchman plays. Harrison White reports that Melba,
at the close of the season, returned to Paris, to study new
roles under Marchesi.
Marian Mackenzie states that Australian audiences prefer
popular” music, and that the tastes of “the colonials”
caused her party a little disappointment at first. She
added that “ popular melodies as old as the hills, which
the audience knew by heart, went remarkably well. What
would really go well is an entertainment on the lines of
Grossmith and Corney Grain.” Miss Thudichum dittoed.
Three young men in an amateur brass band suddenly
developed, recently, tuberculosis of the lungs. Inquiry
showed that the three practised on the same horn, a
secondhand one hired from a dealer. It was taken to
pieces, and tubercle bacilli were plenteous in the con-
volutions and joints. A variation of the “didn’t know
it was loaded” accident, and a first cousin of the “kish
the buke ” death-trap.
A well-known Melbourne actor, who always wears a wig,
is the proprietor of the queerest gambling - machine
known. He is afflicted with “ peripatetic baldness.” One
day he has a bald area over his left ear ; a few days later
the drought-stricken plain will be found perhaps on the
crown of his head, while the formerly unvegetated patch
will be carpeted with a rich thicket of down ; in another
week the desert will be found on his right temple, or at
the top of his spine, or in the middle of his parting. He
goes to bed at night speculating as to where he’ll be bald
next morning. Melb. pros, who know of this freak actu-
jdl.y gamble and get up sweeps on the race, dividing his
idto compartments, and the one holding the number
of the area containing the patch at settling time scoops
th ®P° o1 * M is a joy to see an excited crowd grab him on
settling day, drag off his hat, and finger his pate.

are one flesh.
Item from a Sydney bankruptcy-court
document: “Lunch for six policemen, <£l 10s.”

“ There’s oil, there’s fatness ! ”
Almost the only practical suggestion made
at the Bathurst corroboree came from “Toby”
Barton, in the advice he tendered to the Q.
Government. If a new Bill for sending delegates
to next year’s Federal Convention cannot be
“ constitoosh’nly ” brought in this Session, there
is absolutely nothing to prevent the Govt, from
sending its own nominees.
A female detective has turned up in a
deep-blue Melb. divorce-case. She was young
and neat, with an air of innocence and a dove-
like expression. She followed respondent to a bad
house, and took observations, and placidly related
what she saw to an astounded court, maintaining
all the time the blushless composure of a smooth-
haired Sundayrschool teacher reading Jeremiah
to an infant class. The Judge was horrified, and
advised the young woman to forsake the calling of
private detective right away, and she regarded
him with a baby stare the while, and looked so
like a densely innocent Quakeress escaped from
parental control that half the men in court wanted
to be a big brother to her on the spot, and would
have made the offer probably, but for a vague
suspicion that she was winking inwardly.
Last week, a young lady named Murray,
resident at Thorpe’s Pinch, Mudgee, N.S.W., met
her death through being dashed against a tree by
a horse which had already killed two people.
FEEDORATION AT BATHIRST.
The fell tornado swept upon the City of the Dead
and Gone,
The wind of all the continent on that devoted city
spent,
Toy trumpets blared, the press was squared, tin
godlets all the glory shared,
And, while each penny whistle piped, the local
wags hoorayed and swiped,
Oh ! such a fake ! The earth did shake till every-
one was made awake,
And all the world was told again—“ There is a
City of the Plain ! ”
(In spite of all the blizzard’s whizz, we don’t
believe there was or is. )
For once “ the ghost was made to walk,” by talk—
talk—talk ! —Dipso.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Merman, bought from W. R. Wilson by the
dealer who captured Maluma, will be considered the
handsomest Australian horse they have seen on an English
racecourse—always presuming that he gets there. Ring-
master, Paris, and Maluma ranked as little ones, but
Merman is a full-sized dandy, with a showy chestnut coat
and plenty of “ jam ”in his style. On his latest perform-
ance he is every bit as good as his looks. The habit of
running “stiff” in private gave the stable no encourage-
ment to do what might have been done with Merman,
and after the Williamstown Cup race Mr. Wilson let the
animal go on the assumption that his “ form ” was now
fully exposed.
* * * *
Bloodshot, Disfigured—are all aesthetic names
for horses exhausted ?
* » * •
Plag. : “ Quick and Dead, best racing-heading,
Champion’s copyright.” No. Melb. Boomerano (de-
ceased) first used it. It was invented by a well-known
Bulletin writer (“ M‘G.”) on the inspired night when he
prophesied that Flemington would be laid down as a
cycle-track and Melb. Cup run on pneumatic tyres.
. * * * «
A Golden West race-meeting:—Bunbury Races.
Trial Stakes : Locke’s Redemption 1. There were only
three starters —Flying Stakes : Locke’s Miss Boolka 1. A
walkover—Bunbury Stakes : Locke’s Lochinvar 1, Dryden
2 Railway Handicap : Locke’s Primrose I, Locke’s Re-
demption 2—Farewell Handicap : Locke’s Boolka and
Locke’s Lochinvar were the only starters, and they ran a
dead-heat.
• * * *
“A. W. Steel,” the turf-adviser who has gone
'Ome and left many clients lamenting, used to hobnob of
yore with Hopetoun on the hunting-field. His Excellency
was impressed by the up-to-date Hinglish cut of Steel’s
sporting garments, which were the “ correct thing,” he
said. Unfortunately, the tailor didn’t make the man.
* » * *
At Brisbane, on P. of Wales’ Birthday, the tote
paid £33 6s. for hi over P. of W. Cup, and £43 I3s. for
I Os. over Pace Welter; whilst at Wanganui, M.L., £l6
195., £9 75., £lO 25., £l2 10s., £2O Is. were a few of the
dividends. Doesn’t this make fatuous fatteners of Iky
Mo and co. pray for the tote ?
* * ♦ *
Last Saturday, at Moorefield, N.S.W., Kelso
trained four of the winners, and Cook rode each of them.
Some amends, this, for Reka’s Melb. failures.
* * * *
The Melb. tipster who has to fulfil his promise
of eating Newhaven, with sage and onions, took bis cue
from the English Bell’s Life, or one of its rivals: “If
George Frederick wins this Grand National event, I’ll eat
the ugly brute, hoofs and all.” George Frederick won.
Dreadnaught (Chester—Trafalgar), one of the
heroes of the season ’B9-’9l), is making his mark as a sire
in Maoriland, four out of five of his offspring now racing
having been already returned winners this season. A son
of his (Defiance) recently ran second in Wanganui Derby,
and is thought to have a fair chance of annexing N.Z.
Cup, in which he carries 7st. lib.
* * * *
Cantle : “ English jockeys are superior to Aus-
tralians because more under the whip. They are fewer in
proportion, form a healthy corporation, and, riding nearly
every day at one place or another, are kept pretty free
from razzle-dazzle and bribery.”
* * * *
Not one in a hundred of Tire’s backers are
aware that his real cognomen is Tir6, with the final vowel
sounded. Nearly every cursory racing man believes him
to be one of Dunlop’s colts
* * * *
S.S. ; “The Bulletin is quite excited in its
defence of the V.R.C. against the rabid democracy that
infests the fiat at Flemington on race-days, and is always
wanting more. But really, is the V.R.C. the benevolent
institution it is represented to be? Is it continually
bestowing favors on the ungrateful flatite for which it re-
ceives and expects no return ? As a matter of fact the
club draws a very decent revenue from the flat on big
days ; the intake from vehicles, from booths, bookies and
various machines and contrivances for entertaining and
impoverishing the crowd is a considerable item in its in-
come, large enough to justify the demands made for in-
creased accommodation on the lower level. Besides, the
flat provides a pretty considerable proportion of the
revenue the V.R.C. derives from the sale of railway-
tickets to the course, and also helps to pay for the privi-
lege of free rail-riding which club members enjoy, and
which is worth just £2500 a year. The V.R.C. may be
trusted to watch its own pocket.”
* » * *
A Melb. cyclist, who left his bike in a railway
cloakroom the other day, neatly bowled out the clerk-in-
charge. “Somebody’s been riding this bicycle,” he
remarked, on calling back for it. “ No, sir,” declared the
clerk, boldly, “it hasn’t left the office.” “Then,” said
the cyclist, “how do you account for that cyclometer
registering seven miles more than when the bike was left
here ? ” Then the railway-man confessed.
*»• * *
The entries for the Austral Wheel Race for 1896
numbered 178, of which 126 accepted. As entrance and
acceptance are each £l, the Melb. bicycle club collars
£3o4—from the competitors to whom it allots £3so—in
prizes. So practically the much-boomed generosity of
the club named amounts to exactly £46.
* * * #
A. J. Knowles, the Red Bird who has recently
been all over Australia in the interests of his firm, holds a
mile record of lmin. 57sec. behind pace in Canada.
* * * *
Parsons showed his best form yet at St. Kilda
meeting last Saturday. In the International Mile, he won
his heat by a street from Pither, who looked a certainty
until Parsons went out and shot past the field : the final
he won just as easily by a couple of lengths from Boidi,
the Italian, who made his Melb. d6but.
Some of the foreign contingent seemed to be
thoroughly imbued with a spirit of Communism. One
swarthy cyclist, whose own machine had temporarily gone
bung, coolly removed the wheel from Gargurevich’s
machine and pedalled along the track, leaving the diminu-
tive owner to race with half a jigger. What a mercy for
the Italian that it wasn’t Bilmartin’s bike !
Kellow, winner of the =£loo-Brassey Stakes last
New Year’s Day, and a double at Goulburn recently, added
£5O to his banking-account by annexing the St. Kilda
Wheel Race. The gaunt Kellow, who is the local Red
Bird agent, has really struck form for the first time for
years, and should be one of the most dangerous men of the
season.
Second to Kellow ran young MTnnes, the South
Melb. boy who should have won the last tw'o Australs on
track form. MTnnes has always performed execrably in
a race, and though only 17 now has put in the last three
seasons training hard. With less cycling, the lad would
have been a better cyclist.
Basil Crisp, last year’s Austral winner, won’t
do much this season judging b.y Saturday’s form. The
Hobart man is good enough in a long distance race, but
seems utterly lost when sprinting. Last Austral fell to
him so easily that Crisp had no cause to sprint in the last
lap.
Very disappointing also were the Italian con-
tingent. Boidi, the alleged champion of Italy, on scratch,
with Parsons in the Austral, is, on Saturday’s form, im-
measurably inferior to Porta, and who would think of
classing the latter with Parsons and Ken Lewis? Less
impressive than Boidi again is Cardano—apparently only
a second-rater, and not in the same street with the top 20>
of Australia’s cracks.
Surely it is about time that the public learned
to estimate the advts. of cycling-meetings at their proper
worth. The St. Kilda show last Saturday largely billed
M'Donell, Ralph and Ken Lewis as competitors, vet none
of those named competed. Somebody who has paid his
shilling to see the advertised men ought to initiate an
action for false pretences, or something of that sort, and
bust up the practice.
One of the most interesting points of a recent
big cycling meeting was when a well-known rider retired
from the five-mile race to a spot right in front of the
ladies’ reserve, where his sweater was raised and his bare
stomach energetically rubbed by his trainer.
* » * •
A Sydney bankruptcy application has revived
memories of the Lord Sheffield team of cricketers, inas-
much as the item the “Earl of Sheffield, £lll Os. 1d.,”
appears amongst the judgment debts. Said the bank-
rupt, under examination : “The English cricketers came
to Penrith with a large following, to which were added
the Penrith district cricketers. They drank nearly every
drop of champagne and liquor I had in my hotel, and
never paid me a penn3' for it.” A statement of accounts
included the items :—“ Refreshments, including cham-
pagne, for English and Penrith cricketers and friends, £l4
10s. ; refreshment for 25 Penrith cricketers, including
scorers and umpires, £l2 10s.” No champagne for com-
mon Penrith cricketers!
* * * *
Premier Kingston’s Colored Immigrants Bill,
now before S.A. Legislature, excludes natives of India,
amongst others, from entering S.A. Which means that
Ranjitsinjhi, if he comes out with Stoddart’s team, will
have to be exempted by proclamation. Kingston says if
Ranjy gets going against the S.A. bowlers he will prose-
cute him with the utmost rigor of the law.
* * * *
Charley Samuels, the aboriginal sprinter, is in
Callan Park lunatic-asylum. Very few peds., black or
white, have done much good since the bubble burst.
Frank Hewitt is one of the few who battle along success-
fully.
* * * *
Stanbury: “Fit and well, I can beat Gaudaur.
Australian scullers row a long stroke with plenty of back-
work, though I myself row with a straight back. Gaudaur
rows with a very short stroke extremely quickly; his
sculls, when he reaches out, are very little beyond his
’rigger, and his finish is hard with a screw in one shoulder.
He thinks that his stroke means more pace and less ex-
haustion than the long stroke does.”
• * * •
Harding, according to Stanbury, is a sculling
wonder in the same degree that Michael is a cycling
marvel; a small man but perfect in his work.
* * * *
The game called “ African dodger” is becoming
common at races in the North. It is run mostly by
colored gentlemen. A black human head is stuck
through a sheet of canvas, and the public stands off and
shies eggs at it —three for a shilling, with half-a-crown to
receive for a hit. Occasionally the head is hit, but, as a
rule, the proprietor keeps well ahead of the game.
Dodging a fiver’s worth of eggs per diem should make a
man uncommon spry—and should be admirable practice
for budding politicians.
“African dodger ’’ is a game which must require
a lot of nerve and generalship. The natural tendency
would be for the dodger to shut his eyes when he thought
the missile was coming, so as not to get them full of egg,
and bob his head convulsively. With a view to the half-
crown, however, it is necessary to keep both eyes open,
and fixed on the approaching bird-fruit till the' very last
moment, and then, if it is obvious that the egg must hit,
it is time to close them and resignedly present the hardest
part of the cranium to the shock.' There ought to be
money in “African dodger,” provided a fast bowler
doesn’t get on the job with the eggs, and permanently
bowl out the African ; and there are social possibilities in
it. Supposing the dodger makes a pile and retires on it,
and his beautiful daughter marries a British earl, and
then, at the wedding breakfast, someone recognises the
head that he saw years ago dodging eggs at Is. each, and
mentions the fact, and then the bride falls insensible
under the table, and everybody says “ Damn,” and the
old man gets mad and fatally butts his enemy with a head
that has been hardened by much egg-concussion, and—
well, almost anything might happen to an African dodger
under such circumstances.
* * * *
Edward Walker, the crack Vic. rifle-shot, is 45
years old, an Englishman by birth, and an engineer by
trade. He is a civilian now but a member of the Melb.
rifle club, and his world’s record score, with which he
carried off the Queen’s prize, consisted of 46 pts. at 500vds.,
63 at 600, 45 at 800, and 42 at 900. In the final stage (800
and 900yds.) Walker took 11 bulls-eyes out of 20 shots.

Old lawyer Snowden, M.L.C., announced
himself strongly against the proposal to let police-
officers conduct prosecutions in the inferior Vic
courts, instead of compelling the employment of a
lawyer. He was against it on purely public
grounds. A “glib policeman,” said Snowden,
might keep a small case going all day, merely
for purposes of self-ad vt., and the waste of time
would be awful. Then someone replied that the
other alternative was to hire a glib lawyer, who
was still more likely to keep a small case going all
day for advertising purposes, because he had to
live on ad vt., while the policeman had a regular
salary. And dull old Snowden was quite astonfshed
at this view of the case, which had never struck
him.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities—a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical boolmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

LOCOMOTOR ATAXIA.
A DISEASE LONG HELD BY
PHYSICIANS TO BE INCURABLE.
ITS HORRORS ARE THOSE OF A LIV-
ING DEATH—THE VICTIM HELP-
LESS, HIS TORTURE INTENSE—
AND IS A SOURCE OF CONSTANT
WORRY TO FAMILY AND FRIENDS—
A REMEDY FOR THE DISEASE
DISCOVERED.
Mr. James M‘Lean, a resident of Lefroy, Sim-
coe County, Out., is known to every man, woman
and child for miles around the vicinity of his home,
and all know of the long years during which his
condition has been that of a living death. Mr.
M'Lean tells of his injury, his years of torture,
and his subsequent release from the agonies of
locomotor ataxia, in the following vivid language :
“ In the year 1880 I was thrown from a scaffold,
falling on my back on a stone pile. I was badly
hurt, and narrowly escaped death. Plasters and
linaments were applied and I seemed to get some-
what better. But the apparent improvement was
short lived. My feet began to get unusually cold,
and nothing that could be done would warm them.
The trouble then spread to my legs, and from the
waist down I was attacked with shooting pains
flying along the nerves in thousands, and causing
the most terrible torture for days and nights at a
time. I could get no relief save from the injec-
tions of morphine. Six physicians treated me at
different times, but appeared only to faintly under-
stand my trouble, and could do nothing for my
relief. Some of the doctors declared my trouble
was rheumatism, but two of them said it was a
disease of the spinal cord, that there was no cure
for me, that the trouble would get worse, and that
sooner or later my arms would become affected.
This prediction proved true. My left hand
dropped at the wrist joint and hung dead and cold,
and I had no more control of it than if the hand
were not on me. Fly blisters and electricity were
resorted to without avail. My stomach was next
attacked with a burning, aching, nauseating pain,
causing the most distressing vomiting, and I often
thought I would not see morning. I have vomited
almost continually for thirty-six hours, and no-
thing but morphine or chloroform could deaden
the anguish I suffered. But worse trouble was in
store for me. I lost control of my bowels and
water, and my condition became most horrible,
necessitating constantly the greatest care and
watchfulness. I was now suffering from the top
of my head to the point of my toes. I saw double,
and had to keep my eyes fixed steadily on the
ground, to make a step at all, and the moment I
raised my eyes I would stagger and fall if I were
not grasping something. I could not take a single
step in the dark. For nine long years I suffered
all the horrors of a living death. In 1889 I was
admitted to the Toronto General Hospital, where
I was treated for four months. I was told that my
trouble was locomotor ataxia, and incurable, and
I returned home no better. After returning home
I had further medical treatment, but with no
better result than before. Finally I was given the
following certificate of incurability :
Churchill, July 27th, 1893.
THIS IS TO CERTIFY that James M'Lean
has a disease of the spinal cord (incurable) that
renders him unfit to obtain a living.
A. T. LITTLE, M.D.
About this time I was strongly urged to try Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills, and, oh, how I wish I had
known of this great remedy years ago ! What
anguish and torture I would have been spared !
Soon after beginning the use of Pink Pills I found
myself improving. The pains left me and I was
able to discontinue the use of morphine. I re-
gained control of both bowels and bladder, and
gradually a feeling of life returned to my legs and
arms. I can now walk without the aid of either
crutches or sticks, and can take long strides. My
stomach trouble has all left me, and I can eat as
heartily as ever in my life. My friends, who
never expected to see me about again, are aston-
ished at the wonder Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have
wrought in me. When I began the use of the pills
my weight was reduced to 1351 b. and it has now
increased to 1651 b. lam a new man, and it is
not posssible for me to say enough in praise of
your marvellous medicine. My wife also joins
with me in thanks, and says it was a happy day
for her when I began the use of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, as, since then she has been able to get
rest at night, which she had not done for so many
long years before. I hope Heaven will direct this
into the hands of some other poor sufferer, who
may find, as I did, release from a living death
through your great life-saving remedy.
Yours very gratefully*
JAMES M'LEAN.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills strike at the root of
disease, driving it from the system and restoring
the patient to health and strength. They consti-
stute a perfect blood-builder and nerve-restorer,
curing such diseases as rheumatism, lumbago,
sciatica, neuralgia, partial paralysis, St. Vitus’
dance, indigestion, &e. They are also a specific
for the troubles which make the lives of so many
women a burden, and speedily restore the rich
glow of health to pale and sallow cheeks. Obtain-
able from Chemists and Storekeepers generally,

Queen’s Place, Sydney, on receipt of 2s 9d per
single box, or half-dozen for 14s 9d. Men broken

The night of Melbourne Cup a Bulletin
man ran against a garrulous stranger in a tram-
car. He was so full of goodwill that he had to
talk to someone : he simply boiled over with joy
and conversation and kindly feeling towards the
whole human race. He had backed Newhaven, it
seemed ; and he poured forth all particulars how
he came to do it, and gave the pedigree of the
horse, and briefly reviewed the winners of past
Melbourne Cups, and then branched off into a
long dissertation on horse in general. He was
simply overflowing with horse ; he perspired
horse; there was horse streaming down him; in
fact, he had got horse worse than any other man
The Bulletin scribe ever met. “And did you
back Newhaven ? ” he asked at the end of twenty-
five minutes continuous horse. “No, didn’t back
anything for the Cup.” He looked unaffectedly
sorry at this ; he was so boiling over with joy him-
self that ho wanted all the world to whoop along
with him. Then he brightened up. “Perhaps
you’ve got a horse going for you in some other
£ a ’ he said. The pressman intimated that he
hadn t anything on any horse : the only sporting
event he had on was a small sum on M‘Kinley
against Bryan. Then the affable Australian looked
§• i, alI (t ov ® r - “You’re all right,” he said cor-
he s a good horse.. That blanky horse is
Then he got out, his last words being that
that horse was good enough for anything. Ap-
parently he had never heard of monometallism or
bimetallism or Republicans or Democrats or mug-
wumps or the Tammany vote ; they had all raged
past him unnoticed. His simple little world was
all horse.
The difference between a man “sowing his
wild oats” and another who has sown them. One
is as bad as he can be, the other is only as bad as
he can afford to be. —Proverbs of Solomon; revised
version.

Some signs to prevent living burial. The
subject is alive—when a string tied firmly round
the finger causes the end of the finger to become
red and swollen ; when a bright, steel needle in-
serted into the flesh, and withdrawn, becomes
tarnished inside half an hour; when injection of
ammonia under the skin produces a deep red or
purple spot; when the pupil of the eye, moistened
with atropine, dilates; when a bright light or
bright sunshine, looked at through the fingers
placed closely side by side, gives them a pink
color. The subject is dead, —if the string round
the finger produces no swelling or redness ; if an
inserted needle dots not; tarnish fairly rapidly; if
the eye-pupil does not dilate when atropined ;
if the sunshine experiment shows a dead white
between the fingers; if the body is decomposing.

RAZOR
M ‘Arthur, M.P., whose modest visit to
Australia has just terminated, holds the post of
junior Whip of the Liberal-party in the British
Commons.
Rev. Percival, after a long residence in
Albury, declares that town to be among the most
immoral in Australia. No doubt an insidious
way of commending Albury as a seat for the
Federal Parliament.
Fashionable hotels at ’Ome now have their
menus made up in the form of daily journals, con-
taining a number of dramatic, sporting, and gos-
sipy society paragraphs. Not a bad idea incases
where the English waiters are as slow as Aus-
tralian ones..
Dear Bulletin, —I have often heard that
snakes have a great aversion to tomato-plants, and
that people living where snakes are numerous have
planted a ring of tomato-plants round their houses
and have never been troubled by snakes at bed or
board. Is the tomato the real antidote to snake-
bite ’— Gilhooley.
“ While the - Billy Boils,” by Henry Law-
son, with eight plates by F. P. Mahony, is already
in its third thousand, and maybe had from Angus
and Robertson, Sydney. Post free 5/6.*
When Coroner Youl’s father was chaplain
in Tasmania in the twenties, one of his experiences
was the marrying of 30 couples with the one wed-
ding-ring. Some of the candidates brought two
jor three of their children to be baptised. Most of
the batch had be-n legally married by magistrates
years before the' Rev. John arrived, but the
solemnisation of the contract is dear to the hearts
of women l —and parsons.
In Paris there is a house into which
patients are-received to undergo imaginary opera-
tions. Society touts are employed at good salaries
to persuade hypochondriacs and “interesting
invalids ” that they really are suffering from some
organic complaint which can only be cured by the
knife. They go into “hospital,” are chloroformed,
a few superficial cuts and scratches are inflicted,
and they are discharged with many high en-
comiums as to wondrous endurance, and remark-
able constitution, and the rest of it. The big fees
are paid in advance, and the business is immensely
lucrative.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Another Tichborne yarn sent along to The
Bulletin identifies the real Sir Roger with one
Ramsden, who enlisted in a regiment of Grenadiers,
stationed at Melbourne, when they were summoned
to active service in India. Ramsden, says the
yarn, confided the secret of his Tichborne per-
sonality to a brother private named Moffat, after
which he died fighting for the Queen, and therefore
wasn’t able to return ’Ome and claim his estates.
Moffat forgot all about the defunct Ramsden—
Roger Tichborne—for many years, but eventually
recalled the matter to mind whilst confined in
Wakefield Gaol, and furnished the governor of
that calm retreat with full particulars. Two other
ex-members of the regiment seem to have come
forward and testified that a man known as Rams-
den did get killed, as described by Moffat. The
yarn ends here, and it isn’t a very convincing con-
tribution to the book of Tichborne romance. It
reminds one of Jean Luie, whose famous story (of
how he once met the real Sir Roger aboard the
ship Osprey) seemed so genuine that the Tich-
borne trial was adjourned to permit of enquiries
into the witness’s character. Result a stiff perjury
sentence for Luie on the ground that he had been
“doing time ” at the period of his alleged encoun-
ter with Roger. It was during a latter “time,”
maybe, that Luie manufactured his first-class fic-
tion. In prison a man gets such splendid oppor-
tunities for brain work.

A learned Australian LL.D. recently had
the dreary path of law brightened somewhat by
two girls. They called on him in an irregular
fashion about dusk, and explained blushfully that
one of them was about to get married and wanted
to consult him. “By all means,” said the learned
one; “such cases often come before me. Pray
sit down.” They sat down, and then the younger
and more dazzling of the two removed her boot
and stocking so suddenly that the LL.D. hadn’t
time to protest, even if he had desired to do so,
which he isn’t quite sure that he did, and laid an
unexpected foot before him, explaining that she
had been bitten at one time by a bull-ant or some-
thing which had left a mark, and she wanted the
blemish removed before marriage. The LL.D.
concealed his emotion by examining the foot
closely. “ I think, ”he said at last, “that this is
a case in which legal proceedings would be useless.
You had better consult a medical man.” “But
you’re a medical man, aren’t you ? ” “ No, I’m a
doctor of laws.” Then a scared beauty dispersed
hurriedly out of the front door, and how she got
into her cab she never really knew.
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,

such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full

Dr. de Lautour, of Omaru, M.L., well illus-
trates in a late paper the need of minute obser-
vation in police cases. In connection with a case
of cut throat, there was found a razor having
certain blood-smears on handle and blade, which
suggested the grain of the skin of fingers and
thumb. Dr. de Lautour proved experimentally
that fingers wet with fresh blood could not make
such smears, but that such could only be made by
a hand stained with blood that had begun to dot.
The indication, therefore, was that the smears had
been made, not by the freshly-spirted hand of a
suicide, but by the partially-dried hand of another
person, and murder became the natural conclusion.
Again, in a case of burglary witli violence, there
was noticed on a sheet of white blotting-paper the
dusty impression of a naked foot. This was fixed
by gum, and then photographed. Three men,
' arrested on suspicion, were made to walk bare-
footed along a corridor, first over a coating of
printer’s ink, then over white paper. Excellent
prints of the sole-grainings were got, and the
criminal was thus identified by himself. Other
cases have been recorded in which, through a' pis-
tol being held in the riijht hand of a Ze/Lhanded
person, the attempt to hide murder by hinting
suicide failed. Again, apparent suicides by shoot-
ing or apparent deaths by gun accidents have been
proved to be cases of murder by keen-witted doc-
tors showing that the track of the shot was quite
inconsistent with the supposition of either suicide
or accident, e.g., the Landells case in Lilydale
district, Vic, which was ingeniously shown by
Melb. ITfiiv. Professor Allen to be murder owing
to peculiarities in the track of the missile.

Business, Robbery, etc.
In 1853 Melbourne was an uncivilised assem-
blage of houses, with no roads, drains, or gas, until a
Signor Gabrielli came along. This gentleman, without a
penny of his own, contrived to finance matters so well as
to lend Melbourne corporation £500,000, and Geelong
£200,000, in all £700,000, secured by Govt, guarantee.
The sum was lent at a discount of 5 per cent, (every £95
counting as £100) and charged for at 6 per cent. The
Signor moreover sold his scrip, at a premium of three per
cent., to the very men who ought to have done it for
themselves and reaped the profit. He cleared over
£50,000 by the transaction without investing a cent, of his
own, and at last accounts was still living in gilded splen-
dour somewhere in the south of Italy. The best of the
joke is that the unsophicated borrowers, in gratitude,
gave him a farewell banquet, and ever after referred to
him as their “public benefactor.” They are not built
that way now down South.
• * • *
The secrecy re amounts paid into the income-tax
fund down South has quickly been dissipated. One
official, for disclosing secrets, has been shunted ; but is he
the only one guilty? Southern T.P. societies are credited
with being up-to-date, and what more natural than that
some poor devil who, in need of an extra couple of pounds
a week, can easily earn the amount without any exertion
worth mentioning, should embrace the opportunity.
After the recent postal disclosures in Victoria, anything
seems possible in that province’s officialdom.
* * * *
Wild-cats of various kinds, but some of Wes-
tralia’s in particular, have incited the French Government
to pass a new company law of a self-protecting kind.
This law ordains that henceforward, before any company
can obtain a quotation for its shares in the I’aris market,
a responsible agent must be appointed in France and be
accepted as such by the Minister of Finance or his repre-
sentative. Hitherto the French public were content to
invest in their local Rentes and the Government loans of
neighboring Powers, but during the last few years, and
especially in the early part of 1896, such multitudes of
mining schemes have been engineered from Australia and
elsewhere to London, and thence to Paris, that the Go-
vernment of Jacques Crapaud demands this guarantee of
good faith.
* * * *
Bromley the other day treated Yic Assembly to a
few instances of local commercial morality. Said
Bromley : “He had known a firm holding one of the
highest positions in the city to import fire-proof safes,
not painted or ornamented, and afterwards paint them,
and put fictitious names on them, and sell them to the
public for four or five times their oost. He had known
pianofortes worth £7 10s. or £8 to be imported to Mel-
bourne, and after arrival brass plates with fictitious
names had been screwed on them, and they had been sold
for £25 or £3O each.” What might Bromley not have said
re the fictitious labelling of whiskies, gins, &c., imported,
or Victorian !
• * * *
Bananaland boasts 22 breweries, and turned out
last year 5,000,000 gallons of beer. Consumption of im-
ported beer is declining, while the consumption of Aus-
tralian wines is increasing. Queensland alone last year
produced over 250,000 gallons of native wine. The
Northern aspect of the drink question is decidedly im-
proving.
• * * •
One bitter humorist distinguished himself on
Hospital Sunday in Melbourne. He put into the plate
about £3OO of the paper of a hopelessly dead bank which
the principal shining light of the church had run in the
boom days. When the collection was counted and the
documents rose up in evidence, the various lights, most
of whom had run banks in their time, made no remark,
but their expression signified that they regarded that
depositor as Lost.
Somebody forwards The Bulletin a copy of a
Sydney publication called the Rescue, which is apparently
carried free by post at N.S.W. taxpayers’ expense. En-
closed is a collecting-form for several charitable societies.
Rescue consists of 16 pages, of which about six con-
tain advts. The remainder are chiefly made up of appeals
to friends and children and the general public to forward
cash and other things to G. E. Ardill, Director, Sydney
Rescue Work Society. Mr. Ardill’s name occurs some two
dozen times throughout the paper; and as he has been
soliciting contributions from the public for a good num-
ber of years now, The Bulletin is curious to know
whether' he is a philanthropist of independent means, or
whether he is paid for his services, and, if so, how much ?
Therefore, this paper, which doesn’t remember to have
seen any duly-audited balance-sheets of the Home of
Hope, or the Babies’ Homes, or the All-Night Refuge, or
the Cruelty to Children society, or any of the other
Ardill societies, for some time past, will be glad if Mr.
Ardill, or Mr. C. E. Wigzell, or Mr. E. Vickery, M.L.C.,
or any of the others concerned, will send along the last
balance-sheet of each society to this office for perusal.
* * * *
In Sydney Bankruptcy Court. A publican,
asked if he’d kept a book of accounts (as required by the
Act), replied, after deliberation, that he hadn’t. Subse-
quently he stated that he had, but as it disclosed Sunday
trading he had thought the matter over and, deciding
that he’d come off lighter if he committed a breach of
the Bankruptcy Act than if he broke the Licensing Act, he
burnt the book. Another man said that he owed a female
relative a large sum of money, but she had promised to
liquidate it if he would give her the custody and control
of his child, of which she was very fond. So he had
handed the child over and wiped the debt out.
* * * *
The facts re the West Bogan (N.S.W.) scrub-
clearing works—concerning which there has been much
pother in print and in Labor-led deputations—are these :
The men get so much (variable) for work done ; the
amounts they are alleg'ed to earn are set down in official
statistics and quoted by' the Minister whenever he is
called on to talk in reply to anything ; those amounts are
correct, as such ; but there are such grave deductions for
rations, miscellaneous charges, and a something called
“discount” on moneys advanced for engagement, fares
up, &0., that the sum (cash) actually received by the poor
devils is not enough to play pitch-penny with, let alone
to send anything to their wives and families. “Figures
can’t lie?” Pardon this paper : Figures nearly always
beat even a snake-and-bible liar.
* * • •
Maoriland shipowners discharge a cordial and
well-deserved curse at their country’s shipping regula-
tions. The local vessel is strictly supervised by law ; it
must comply with a certain fixed standard of seaworthi-
ness and carry a certain complement of men ; must not
have its load-line submerged, and is prohibited from
carrying deck-cargo. But the Government never had the
courage to extend these regulations to foreign vessels,
therefore the casual ship from Honolulu or ’Murka, when
she discharges her cargo at one port, will do a little
coasting business, or a great deal of it, under very advan-
tageous circumstances, before she loads up again for
foreign parts. She can carry twice as much cargo as the
local craft is allowed to take, have her decks loaded to
almost any extent, bury her 1 lad-line about as deep as she
pleases, and go from port to port with as few sailors as her
captain likes to carry. Therefore she can cut freights far
below the figure that will pay the Maoriland shipowner
and still make a profit. In the United States, this diffi-
culty is settled in one act by a law which says that no
foreign vessel whatever shall go into the coasting busi-
ness. Either the same law should be adopted in Austral-
asia, or, at the very least, the foreign vessels should not
be allowed to carry one ounce of freight from one Austral-
asian port to another unless under the same regulations
which apply to the locally-owned vessel. Protecting the
foreigner against the local man is a curiously upside-down
policy.
N.S.W. Patents Law Amendment.
A capable correspondent writes :
Re Want’s Patents Bill and your references thereto —
one way or the other, the only questions really at issue
are whether the principle involved is just and whether its
application is expedient. The fact that at any moment
particular persons happen to be obviously interested is
not ail argument. The second part of the Bill—that
relating to revocations-is mere padding. Only one re-
vocation case has ever been heard in N.S.W. : that was a
purely formal proceeding in which one person owned two
patents, and required to annihilate one so that the other
would stand clear.
The circumstances of N.S.W. are such that argument
based on the practice of other countries in connection
with amendment of patents, is not final. England,
with almost every other country, has, for all modern time,
had incorporated in its law provisions permitting amend-
ment on terms practically identical with those of Want’s
Bill; but N.S.W. law never contained such a provision.
In those other countries, therefore, the position of the
public is this : so long as a patent is not so utterly out of
form as to be incapable of amendment it must be re-
spected, for even though in its original shape it may be
void and ineffectual, yet if amended within permissible
limits it will become valid and effectual; so only that por-
tion which the patentee has wrongfully claimed may be
used by the public without fear of interference from him.
In N.S.W., however, a patent bad in part is bad totally,
and cannot be rectified ; hence the public are empowered
to appropriate the whole fruit of a patentee’s brains if for
any reason, accidental or otherwise, he happen to have
claimed an item too much, or otherwise erred. The
rights of the public as against patentees are therefore
different under the two sets of conditions, and the rights
of both parties must be adequately respected. It is
clear, however, that so far as N.S.W. is concerned an
equitable vested interest resides in only those who have
already accepted the chance offered, and gone into posses-
sion of the patent property in defiance of the patentee.
The amendment accepted by Gould adopts this idea, for
it declared an amended patent to be ineffectual against
parties who had commenced before the passing of the
Act, whilst it preserved the efficacy of the patent against
all who had not so established an equitable vested interest.
The Broken Hill discussion is only by the way. If Ash-
croft or anyone else robbed the company of an invention
to which it was entitled then the co. had its remedy ; but
as it did not proceed to assert itself against Ashcroft
very probably it had no case. But even though the co.
has not proceeded, if the circumstances are as the B.H.
members state, it can use the weapon fatally against Ash-
croft’s patent when it pleases.
To make the Act applicable to future patents only is
preposterous. If the principle of amendment is good
then adopt it, having made such provision as will ade-
quately protect equitable rights which actually exist.
And there is danger of a patentee making a mistake in
his specifications. Ask any patent-agent how often in the
year he meets cases of re-invention, or overlapping sug-
gestions, or adumbrations of subsequently developed
ideas. A patentee or his patent-agent cannot know
everything, and in a very large proportion of cases the
most extreme caution and the widest experience will be
baffled. I have before me two sketches of mechanism
designed by myself, parts of both of which were indepen-
dently invented within a few weeks by a Parisian and a
South Australian and patented by them. Also, I once
made a search to ascertain novelty in a nut-lock ; examined
over 700 patents for different devices, none of which
touched the one in question ; but the German Patent
Office discovered one morp somewhere, and it had some-
thing in common with the lock searched against. And
what is the use of saying that patent-experts are familiar
with up-to-date progress? The Dunlop pneumatic tyre
looked new enough, so far as everyone knew, until some-
one discovered that a patent was granted for it 50 years
ago. The writer has just completed a search in British
patents, extending back only 20 years, and he has dis-
covered over 100 patents all for inventions more or iesj
meritorious, but nearly every orle overlapping more ot
! ess ’ , when th ® s ® a f e hf ! w dde * an u i,1 h v o e ” t °!
know how much he is entitled to claim, though he may
know how much he has invented—or reinvented—and, if
his claims cover more than he is enr.itled to, though no
more than he has honestly invented, it will not avail him,
for the courts must and will invalidate his patent,
The very numerous decisions in the law-books define
what “substantially” means, and a reference to any
hand-book of patent law will readily clear the mind as to
what is and what is not, generally speaking, art allowable
amendment under the provisions of the British Acts 1883-8*
Make a final appeal to the Supreme Court by all means,
and g 0 gj V e all parties every chance. But, because the Bill
provides that leave to amend is conclusive as to right to
amend and that the amendment shall, for all purposes,
stand part of the specification, do not run away with the
idea that the patent as amended is riot subject to the same
revision when it comes before the courts as if it were not
amended. It must, if contested, stand its trial just the
same, and you will find numerous cases of British amended
patents having been declared void for reasons for which
unamended patents could be voided, and also because the
amendment was not of the kind contemplated by the law,
though it had been allowed in the regular way. (See Van
Olde’s Pat., 6 P.0.R., p. 22, and Lane Fox’s case.)
The objection which many, whose opinion should be
respected, have against the Bill, is that it should have
been introduced as part of a consolidated measure. For
many years efforts have been made to induce various
N.S.W. Govts, to codify the law relating to patents and
to bring it up to date. At present there are three acts
in existence ; the present Bill, if passed, will
make a fourth. There is no more pressing need
for the two provisions of this Bill than there is for
about a dozen other provisions, equally important with
the first. Let the Government abandon the present
Bill and bring in a comprehensive measure, dealing with
the whole subject. The interests involved in N.S. Wales
patents total millions of pounds sterling. Surely paten-
tees and the public are entitled to ask that the old out-of-
date legislation of 1852 be swept away and something
suitable to the needs of the day put in its stead. The
Patents Office, which provides a yearly profit of about
£2OOO to the revenue, has been recently stuck into a
corner of the Registrar-General’s Department, where
there is not half accommodation, and seems to have been
relegated to the status of a mere recoi ding-office , and
the very important legal and technical functions of
F^ 1 a V liri ®, r *? ave keen r ust into the alrea ,}
tud hands of the Registrar-General. The tune is cone
when the Patents Office of this country should be an inst-
tution, but the idea of the authorities seems rather to be
to L lfc as a means for extracting revenue from the brains
of the community, to stunt the usefulness of what should
be the Alma . Ma ter of industrial development, and to give
as few facilities as possible for the distribution of technical
knowledge through its medium. It is a principle acknow-
led d u^iversa ,ifr that the Patents Institution should do
nQ more than •' its W ay, for ibs mission is to develop
techniea j knowledge, and the result of its work is
dilninighed in proportion as those who avail themselves of
its advantages are taxed. If the N.S.W. authorities will
not therefore give the inventing public the advantages for
which they ar ° pay ing, then the inventing public should
not be cal j ed 0 / to ply into the Patents Office so large a
gum annua ji y j n excess of the oost of working the depart-
nlent Eiu , er patent-fees must be reduced, or patentees

Fortunate Cure of a General’s
Daughter.
(From, the “Hampshire Independent."
The Hampshire Independent sent a special commis-
sioner to ascertain the facts in the following- case. His
report was as follows :—“ I received instructions to inter-
view Lieut.-Gen. Clement R. Shaw, who has won his
spurs in India, and is now living with his family in honor-
able retirement at St. Paul’s Vicarage, Shanklin, Isle of
Wight.
“ I explained to the General that I was anxious to ob-
tain some personal explanation as to the narrow escape I
had heard one of his daughters had recently experienced.
“At that he brightened visibly. ‘ You must know,’ he
said, ‘ I’m just a bit of an enthusiast on this point ; but
the tale is very short. Nearly two years ago my daughter
—the one you referred to -came home from India, and
when I joined her in London I found her ill in bed. She
had rheumatic and neuralgic pains ; she was perfectly
bloodless, listless, and in a generally weak and prostrate
condition. We called in a doctor, and lam bound to say
that he succeeded in getting her out of bed; but she was
still absolutely colourless, was in great wretchedness, and
suffering from what I should call anaemia, or bloodless-
ness. She had a kind of fever, nervous headache, and
other pains. We went to Bembridge, in the Isle of Wight,
and there I first heard of Dr. Williams’s Pink Pills for
Pale People. Indeed, I was recommended to try them,
and I did so. My daughter took the first box, and they
had a marvellous effect upon her. She regained her
colour; she lost her pains, and became altogether dif-
ferent ; and after she had been taking them some time she
came in one day after her walk and said to me, ‘ Why,
father, I feel quite a glow through me.’ She went on
taking the Pills, and I am glad to tell you that she re-
covered completely. My daughter’s recovery was due to
Dr. Williams’s Pink Pills for Pale People without a doubt.
Their effects were marvellous, and I did not speak with-
out first ascertaining the facts. I have had a great deal
to do with medical men in this country and in India, and
they laugh at the idea that patent medicines cure any
disease. But I have given you this case, and that I know ;
I won’t deal with any beyond my own personal experience
and knowledge, further than to say that I believe the
results to have been satisfactory.’ ”
