An Answered Prayer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Johnny Brown sat on the paling-fence watching
with the intentness of his nine years the boys as
they passed in ones and twos along the street lead-
ing to the Cleveland paddocks. Johnny was
shabby enough, and his little shirt peeped out of
his nroken trousers ; but what troubled him most,
however, were his boots. His mother, over-
burthened with the care of a large family, had
ordered him not to go out of the yard, as he was
not tidy enough ; besides, running about the
streets would wear out his only pair of boots, and
1 1 would be many a day before he was likely to
get another pair. His mother had hidden away
liis boots lest he should disobey, and he was hardly
likely to go out without them. It was a sort of
recognised rule amongst working people who lived
in those days at the Strawberry Hills that boys
who wore boots were to be considered respectable
—the condition of the rest of their raiment did
not count.
Johnny was aware that he would be looked
down on if he went out without boots, and he
respected public opinion ; but he also knew that
boots or no boots he would get a ‘' hiding ” from his
father if he disobeyed his mother, and the fear of
chastisement was an even stronger incentive to
obedience than anything people were likely to say
about him. If only his mother’s wrath was con-
cerned, Johnny would “ chance it,” but his father’s
hand was heavy, especially if he were ill-tempered
• —and he mostly was so unless he had taken drink,
when he was disposed to be playful as a child.
All this passed through Johnny’s mind as he sat
drumming his bare heels against the fence. Tommy
Bray and Arohie Mitchell passed under him carry-
ing bat and ball, with “Ain’t yercoming, Johnny?”
which was very trying to the said Johnny, who
found it necessary to hold tight to the fence in
order to combat the magnetic pull of the cricket
things. So long as they were in sight he managed
to resist, but when they had turned the corner,
Johnny found himself outside the fence, but still
irresolute. Just then a shout from some boys as
they rushed along the main street broke down any
lingering resolution, and away Johnny scampered
to join the fun.
In the whirl of excitement everything but the
game was forgotten—want of boots, out without
leave, the certain thrashing. But all things end,
and, play over, Johnny found himself once more
climbing the fence and back again in his own
yard.
The Conrads lived next door to the Browns,
and Tom Conrad, who had been sober for a full
month, felt very virtuous. “ Well, Polly,” said he
to his wife as he lifted the latch, “ here I am. I
saw Brown in William-street, and he wanted me
to have a drink, but I wasn’t on.”
“Well, I must say,” said his wife, “those
Brown youngsters are very good, considering their
father, Look there,” continued she, “there is
little Johnny kneeling down saying his prayers in
the yard. I’ll give him a piece of bread-and-jam
presently, that I will.” And so it was—there was
Johnny kneeling against a tree thumping his
breast and praying as if his little heart would
break.
Safe inside his own yard, Johnny began to fear
the consequences of his escapade. He was a
nervous boy, who took his trouncings very well,
finding more pain in the anticipation than in the
reality. His mother was certain to inform his
father of his disobedience, and his father rarely
spared the rod where his children were concerned.
It was Saturday and pay-day, and sometimes his
father came home drunk on pay-day, and when he
was drunk he was incapable of punishing anyone.
This was Johnny’s only chance of escaping. He
was not sure whether his father had yet come
home or not, but Johnny was hardly over the
fence before he fell on his knees and poured out
his humble little heart in supplication that the
Almighty might be pleased to send his father home
—drunk.
It was in this attitude that Mrs. Conrad saw
him. “ Poor little fellow,” she said ; “ may my
boys grow up like him ! ” Presently, looking
up, she saw Johnny’s father passing the door.
“ What a drunken beast he is ! ’ she said.
Little Johnny’s prayer had been heard.
HENRY SIEREL.
The clergy of Melb. (except the R.C.’s)
have risen as one parson to denounce lotteries of
all kinds. What about marriage ?
At Balclutha (M.L.), the district that is
sicklied o’er with the pale cast of Cold Tea, and
where good brown ale is sold secretly disguised in
a cruet down a cellar by a person who lies about it
afterwards, the police lately raided a sly groggery
at 3 a.m. They found five men who all explained
eloquently before the local authority next morn-
ing. One had been going somewhere (at 3 a.m.)
to get his coat and boots (what his coat and boots
were doing there weren’t explained), and just out-
side the groggery a complete stranger had hit him
such a welt that he had to be carried in and
attended to at once. The second man was the
person who had helped him in. The third, a
barber (who had dropped in at 3 a.m. to borrow a
match) had stopped to cut the hair off the wound.
A person alleged to be a doctor, seeing the barber
going in, had gone in after him (at 3 a.m.) to tell
him how his brother was, and stayed to dress the
injury. The remaining individual saw the light
in the window and burst in to see if the place was
on fire. Then the police unfortunately arrived.
Whereupon The Bulletin is of opinion that there
are some very futile Jules Vernes in the cranky
region of Balclutha. Anyhow, in a Prohibition
district no tale that anyone can invent is likely to
account for five men and a smell of whisky being
found in a strange house at 3 a.m. Therefore, it
would have been much more sensible and a better
advt. for the barber and the other men if they
had told the P.M. straight that they were drink-
ing, that they had been drinking before and
meant to do it again, that Cold Tea was stupid
and comfortless and indigestible and bad for
the kidneys, and that if he didn’t like
it he could lump it. There is room at
Balclutha for the biggest and most theatri-
cal protest against Cold Tea that has yet
been attempted. What is wanted is for all the
drinking residents of the district to get together
by agreement on a suitable occasion—say Xmas
Day—and sell drink publicly to each other till the
solitary policeman comes and runs in or summons
the whole 700 or so. Then let them all own up
straight, and say they will take it out instead of
paying the fine, and defy the P.M. to get them
into the lock-up and get the door shut. And if the
State sends two special trains to take them down
to Dunedin to serve their sentence the great un-
controllable mob could break out at every stop-
ping place and drink itself incapable, and when
it got to its destination there wouldn’t be
enough wheelbarrows in the city to remove it
on. Then the Big Drunk of Balclutha would
become historical.
He Let His Heart Go.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The wind whistled, and every now and
then threw a splash of sleety rain against the
fissured face of a bleached, grey rock. A cavity
in its centre looked to the south-west, whence
wind and rain came ; and although the hole ex-
tended a good way the chill gusts occasionally
reached the farthest corner. Gyp Pringle crawled
back as far as possible and built up a barricade of
coarse packing-paper which he had carried on his
back from Sydney warehouses.
Dog-weariness made him sleep. He had tramped
town and suburbs all day in search of work—the
gate to bread and beef and, mayhap, ale. As he
closed his eyes the rattle of reed and brass music
was borne across the Domain from the precincts
of Government House.
Soon he dreamed. What a heaven of gratifica-
tions was spread before him ! Beef, potatoes, ale,
whisky, tobacco. He stretched out a hungry
hand. Alas ! here he awoke.
Realising the cruel hoax, he kicked viciously.
To his surprise his foot struck something soft, a
composite of flesh and blood, and a plaintive
whine made humble protest.
“Now, then,” said Gyp, “who the devil are
you? This hole’s mine. No doubt you’re a
Socialist. Well, clear out. I didn’t slope the
town an’ hump this here swag o’ old paper an’ root
out this bloomin’ marble hall for the benefit o’ the
community at large. I done it for mesolf. I’m a
individualist—l am. So take yerself to gaol or
glory out o’ this.”
Only heavy breathing answered.
“D’ye want me to bash yer? G’up to Guv-
ment House, can’t yer ? They’re singin’ an’coort-
in’ up there, I believe, an’ ye might be able to
collar a fan or spoon when nobody’s lookin’. Clear
out, I say ! ”
This time the whine became a subdued bark,
and a cold nose was thrust into his hollow hand.
“Me bloomin’ ead ! ye’re a dawg ! An’ I took
ye all the time for a Socialist ! I beg parding
humbly. All the same though—out ye go. If a
coal-heaver snooked into ’Ampden’s, out he’d go.
If I snooked into a coal-heaver’s, out I’d go. An’
as this hole’s my castle, out you go. An’ if you
had a kennel an’ I attempted to snook in, out /’d
go. See? We’re all, except them mad Socialists,
thorough individualists. ”
He kicked.
“ Sometimes though, notwithstanding me logic,
I’ve thought that if those on top were a bit kinder
to the folk below it might be better all round.
It’s not me brain says that ; it’s me heart.”
He kicked again.
“If, when a coal-heaver snooked into Governor
’Ampden’s, that well-paid Nabob gave ’im time to
draw breath before firin’ ’im out; or when I
snooked into the coal-heaver’s, or when this out-
cast dawg snooked in here ”
He drew up his foot smartly.
“Well, now,” he said, sitting up, “dammut,
but I’ll give me heart her way for onst. Let her
go, Gallagher ! ”
* ♦ * *
When daylight came Gyp examined his
partner in adversity, and recognised that the dog
belonged to a lady in whose garden he had had
a couple of days’ work a week or two before.
This he considered an extremely gratifying dis-
covery.
“Me bloomin’ ’ead!” he exclaimed; “this
means a big reward, if it doesn’t lead to a con-
stant job, with a liberal pension for me old age ! ”
Laying hold of a piece of rope and looping it
round the famished creature’s neck, he continued :
“ It’s a pound if it’s a thankee. No doubt, the
dawg’s been lost for a time, for the hair’s peelin’
off. He’s seen the inside o’ Belmore more’n onst,
I’ll go bail. An to think I struck this patch o’
luck the first time I let me heart go ! This’ll
make a different man o’ me for the future. ‘Do a
kind hact ’ will be my motter. I venture to say
most people with that motter found a dawg to
start them. Come along, old boy ; off to Darlin’
Point to reap the reward o’ virtue ! Yer mistress
’ll be that glad to see yer face that she’ll likely
keep me for dinner an’ fit me in a suit o’ good, dry
clothes.”
» * * *
“What have you to say?” asked the
spectacled Stipendiary.
“That dawg came to me under a rock in the
Domain, an’—
“ There’s too much of this going on. You ob-
tained work in this lady’s garden with the clear
intention of making friends with the dog. You
were seen about the place several times since.
Then you induce the dog off and keep him in your
low haunts until a reward is offered. Sydney is
cursed with loafers of your stamp. Three months’
hard labor.”
Gyp : “ This is the first time I ever let me heart
go, an’ if ever ”
But he was hauled away, crying, at the top of
his voice : “ I’m a individualist for the rest o’ me
bloomin’ life, I am ! ”
OMICRON.
My Heart’s Desire.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I watch her in the early morn
Trip down the street, my dwelling past;
She hurries on, and I, forlorn,
Lament, yet love, her pace so fast;
Her vision haunts me all the day,
My work I leave but scarce alive,
And pass the hours as best I may
Till she returns at half-past five.
I watch her in the gathering gloom
Come speeding homeward cheerfully,
And quake to think I’ve met my doom.
The future has grave fears for me :
For I am sure she is my fate
(Or, as it’s called, “affinity”),
Yet am I feared that May and Kate
And she won’t prove a “ trinity.”
What shall Ido ? May is my wife,
Kate is her sister tall and fair :
One holds the cash, one rules my life,
And both abhor—that chestnut mare !
L. R. MACLEOD.

A NARROW ESCAPE,
“The Men who Make England.*
We are indebted to the courtesy of a gentleman well known in
Liverpool for the following interesting and instructive particulars. He
writes
I venture to think the following will be read with interest by
you and any English people who have friends abroad, and their name
is legion. Quite recently I was a passenger by the midnight train
from Euston to Liverpool. I was travelling in a third-class compart-
ment, and with me there was a quiet, resolute - looking man about
thii ty-five, a middle-aged sailor, and a young fellow who stated he was
twenty-two. The latter informed us he was sailing from Liverpool to
Lagos, on the West Coast of Africa, in the course of a few days,
which drew from our quiet-looking friend, ‘Queer place, Lagos. I was
there eighteen months.’ ‘Unhealthy?’ ‘Rather; just about the worst
you can hit on. but X kept fairly right.’ ‘ How did you manage ? ’
‘Well, take this from me straight—cut all spirits, brandy especially,
and always have Beecham’s Pills handy.’ ‘Beecham's Pills?’ ‘Yes;
I’ve knocked about the world a good deal, and am just off to Rangoon,'
where I have a berth cutting teak, and I should never feel safe without
them.’ ‘Right you are,’ chimed in the sailor. ‘I was in Brazil,
Santos, but a few months ago, and they were the regular thing for
keeping you right out there. I came home last week, and here is my
last last box, producing one from out of his waistcoat pocket. This testi-
mony from ‘ the men who make England ’ was perfectly genuine, and it
gives me pleasure to communicate it to you.
“I will just add the following, which was read to me the other
day, it being an extract of a letter from a prosperous young magistrate
at Esk, Queensland, to his father in Liverpool : ‘I am exceedingly busy,
and on Saturdays can hardly get through; and just now, if a customer
does not ask for wire fencing, he is certain to want Beecham’s Pills.
You had better send me further consignments of these sharp.’*
Here you have Australia, West Africa, Eastern A.ia, and Brazil*
And climates good and bad. We often hear, “ Glo where you will you
will find Englishmen,” but we must now add! “ and BEE'CHAM’S
PILLS.”
